==PAGE:4==
ABSTRACT
Diabetes is a chronic disease that 253,000 persons in Denmark experience very differently1. Persons with 
type 2 diabetes (T2D) can mainly treat their disease by self-management of ordinary lifestyle factors, as 
diet, exercise, sleep and medication, as well as self-monitoring of blood glucose levels. Through a regular 
measurement of blood glucose levels, a person with type 2 diabetes can detect potential deviations that 
may indicate factors that have a negative influence on the blood glucose. With these insights a person with 
type 2 diabetes can regulate their lifestyle factors according to what their body is positive about.
Previous studies have shown that education and knowledge about self-management may improve pa­
tients psycologial status affecting the blood glucose in a positive direction (Sanbao Chai et al., 2018). 
Studies have also shown that mobile health apps (mHealth apps) may improve quality of diabetes care 
and enhacement of self-management (Fatehi F., C. Gray L., W. Russell A., 2017). Other research state 
that gaining insight and knowledge about blood glucose and affecting factor may help persons with type 
2 diabetes make better life choices (El-Fil, 2021). 
However, measuring blood glucose can for most people be discouraging, demotivating and stressful due 
to the current practices and user experiences. Measuring blood glucose requires the users to remember 
to perform this process at specific time, to interrupt their daily life and work, create uncomfortable situa­
tions with the measuring devices, etc. Knowledge is furthermore gained by the users themselves as health 
professionals, perceived by persons with T2D, do not guide the patients and do not enlighten about all 
possibilities.
The goal of this project is to improve the user experience of measuring blood glucose levels on a regular 
basis for persons with type 2 diabetes. Methods used to explore the problem statement and create a solu­
tion for are: research of current studies and market, analysis of the current state, interviews for explora­
tory purposes, workshops through a co-creation process and testing of final solutions. Three workshops 
are facilitated with persons with T2D, experts from the Danish Diabetes Association and designers from 
DTU. Co-creation process methodology is chosen to invite the users into the whole design process where 
they can define problems and create solutions for their need in collaboration with experts and designers.
Defined through the co-creation process, user experience of measuring blood glucose levels may be im­
proved by an app that collects different kind of data from multiple devices and presents them to the user 
in a graphical manner. The user can add actitivites to the blood glucose measurements to give the data 
a context and freely choose what data to input/view. This personal platform informs the user about their 
status and rewards them when it goes good. Seeing and feeling an improved wellness status keeps the 
users motivated to reach and maintain their goals.
For future work, these values must be tested further with a significant amount of users over a longer peri­
od of time to make sure that the user experience of measuring blood glucose is actually improved.
1	
https://videncenterfordiabetes.dk/viden-om-diabetes/generelt-om-diabetes/diabetes-i-tal

1.	 READING GUIDE
READING GUIDE
Throughout the thesis, you will be introduced to a couple of terms for 
the same stakeholder that change depending on which stage of the de­
sign process you are in. This description may help you follow the re­
port and the project, as well as the process conducted behind.
User:
User is defined in this project as a person with 
type 2 diabetes. 
Stakeholder:
When the current state is analysed, the user is re­
ferred to as a stakeholder on an equal level as the 
rest of the model. The user is then the centre for 
analysis and will therefore be referred to as centre 
stakeholder.
Target group:
 During a discussion of the problem statement and 
scoping throughout the project, the user is referred 
to as a target group as they are the users we wish 
to solve a problem for. 
Interviewee:
During the exploratory interviews, the users that 
have joined the sessions are referred to as an in­
terviewee. 
Participants:
Participants is a broader term in this project as 
it refers to all types of people participating in the 
three workshops. This includes both the users, the 
experts and the designers. 
Each chapter starts with an introduction with the used methods and 
ends with a conclusion. The report can thereby more briefly be read 
through these sub-sections.

Abbreviation
==PAGE:14==
10
253,000 persons in Denmark are diagnosed with type 2 diabetes, 293,000 have prediabetes and an ad­
ditional 61,000 have undiscovered diabetes (Diabetes i tal, 2020). By the year 2030 around 420,000 
persons will have diabetes, which is an increase of 66 % (Carstensen B, Rønn PF, Jørgensen ME, 
2020). Diabetes is a chronic disease that occurs when the human blood glucose levels are too high 
due to reduced or absent production of insulin by the pancreas or the production of non-functional 
insulin that the cells fail to respond to. If diabetes is not prevented and not treated a row of serious 
further complications, such as heart disease, kidney, eye and nerve damage, dementia, etc. can arise 
(Everything You Need to Know About Diabetes, 2021). Diabetes is highly different from person to 
person, including symptoms, causes and risks, as well as prevention and treatment of it. Thereby, 
there are no general guidelines, rules, or recommendations that all persons with diabetes must follow.
Current treatment practises recommended by the health professionals include medication and a visit 
to the doctor every third month for a blood glucose level check. This creates several problems as 
first of all most patients are not satisfied treating their disease through medication, and second of all 
measurement, every third month is mostly not enough to understand the reasons for fluctuations in 
blood glucose levels.
Most prevention and treatment of type 2 diabetes is based on personal management of ordinary life­
style factors, such as diet, exercise, sleep etc. These factors highly affect blood glucose levels and by 
ignoring them the levels can increase to dangerous levels. Through regular observation and control 
of a healthy diet as well as regular exercise, the risks of developing further critical complications can 
be decreased. However, this may be difficult as treating diabetes requires a change of lifestyle, daily 
habits and mindset within a short time, which may be stressful, demotivating and even not accepted.
One way that a person with type 2 diabetes can control lifestyle factors that may affect blood glucose 
levels (such as diet and exercise) is through regular measurement of blood glucose through monitor­
ing devices. By following this process, one may detect potential deviations in blood glucose values 
that can indicate which factors must rather be decreased or eliminated due to their negative conse­
quence on the body (Blodsukker og måling, n.d.). A previous study by (El-Fil, 2021) has shown that 
day-to-day self-monitoring of blood glucose has helped persons with type 2 diabetes gain insights 
into what lifestyle factors can affect their blood glucose. A study by the Danish Diabetes Association 
has furthermore shown that regular measurement of blood glucose has contributed to change of diet, 
exercise and medication as well as a combination of these (Festersen, 2021).
3.1	 PROBLEM AND MOTIVATION
Measuring blood glucose regularly has several benefits for persons with type 2 diabetes, both for 
their health and their living conditions. However, conducting measurement every single day for sev­
eral weeks may for some people not decrease the fact that changing lifestyle, habits and mindset is 
stressful and demotivating. Furthermore, through the study by (El-Fil, 2021) and the Danish Diabetes 
Association (Diabetesforeningen, 2021), the current experience of starting and continuing measuring 
blood glucose is for some discouraging and not user-friendly. 
Previous studies have researched and created products for persons with type 2 diabetes through a 
INTRODUCTION
Introduction
==PAGE:15==
11
user-centred, (Catherine H Yu et al., 2014) and (Giuseppe Fico et al., 2019), and participatory design 
(Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) approach. Potential end-users were invited into the development or 
testing process and could contribute with ideas and suggestions for improvement. However, no stud­
ies have involved end-users through a whole design and development process, from problem defini­
tion to solution creation on issues regarding type 2 diabetes and self-management of blood glucose.
The problem statement for this project is:
The target group is furthermore is decided upon persons with type 2 diabetes. In the following section, 
the process of defining this target group will be discussed in more detail.
This thesis is part of a PhD project by Dan Roland Persson on the topic of: “Personalized Micro-Inter­
vention Technology for Diabetes Self-Management”. On a higher level, this PhD project researches 
how individual persons with type 2 diabetes can be helped to manage their lifestyle through tailored 
interventions in context-specific situations (Persson, 2022). 
The goal of the thesis is first of all to plan and conduct several workshops focusing on co-creation 
between the participants and the planners. Using the output from literature studies, analysis of current 
state and the workshops, problems regarding the measurement of type-2 diabetes are outlined, and 
desirable solutions for improvement of user experience are generated. While processing the output 
of further workshops, a design idea is chosen, and a prototype is developed. Through iterative testing, 
the prototype is refined to achieve user goals in a greater way compared to solutions used today.
The thesis collaborates with the Danish Diabetes Association who supports the project in relation to 
workshop planning and facilitating, as well as participants recruitment.
Side note: The original title of this thesis was “Improvement of user experience regarding measure­
ment of blood sugar level for type-2 diabetes, through co-creation and participatory design”. As the 
thesis scope changed throughout the project, the title of the thesis has accordingly also changed.
How may we improve the user experience of measuring blood glucose 
levels on a regular basis, through a co-creation process methodology?

4.1	 WHAT IS DIABETES?
Diabetes, or diabetes mellitus (DM) is a disease that occurs when the human blood glucose levels, 
or blood sugar levels, are too high. The glucose in the body is the main source of energy and comes 
from the food that is consumed. A hormone named, insulin, created in the organ pancreas, controls the 
amount of needed blood glucose in the cells (Type 2 diabetes, 2017) and makes sure that the glucose 
from the food enters the cells (What is Diabetes, 2016). However, if you have diabetes, the body will 
either not receive the blood glucose or will receive it in small amounts. This is mainly due to reduced 
or absent production of insulin by the pancreas or production of non-functional insulin that the cells 
fail to respond to (Everything You Need to Know About Diabetes, 2021).
Diabetes is categorised into four primary types: Type 
1 Diabetes (T1D), Type 2 Diabetes (T2D), Prediabetes 
and Gestational diabetes. In case the pancreas fails to 
produce insulin for the body cells, you have type 1 di­
abetes. If the pancreas produces a reduced amount of 
insulin or the cells cannot take in the insulin properly, 
you have type 2 diabetes. People with prediabetes have 
blood glucose levels higher than normal but not enough 
to be diagnosed with type 2 diabetes and people with 
gestational diabetes have high blood glucose levels only 
during their pregnancy (Everything You Need to Know 
About Diabetes, 2021). Untreated prediabetes has a high 
risk of developing into type 2 diabetes (What Is Predi­
abetes, 2021) and gestational diabetes often disappears 
after giving birth (Everything You Need to Know About 
Diabetes, 2021). Figure 1 shows a graphical representa­
tion of the difference between a healthy production of 
insulin and intake of glucose, type 1 diabetes and type 
2 diabetes. 
4.1.1	 Complications
In all four types of diabetes, the 
blood has too much glucose 
which can cause very serious 
problems and complications. If 
diabetes is not diagnosed and 
not treated you can get chron­
ically high blood glucose lev­
els also called hyperglycaemia, 
or in extreme situations, as for 
people with type 1 diabetes, 
ketoacidosis. Ketoacidosis is a 
serious complication of diabe­
tes and mostly occurs for peo­
ple with type 1 diabetes due to 
the absent production of insulin 
Table 1: Complications of diabetes
Figure 1: Difference between healthy 
production of insulin, type 1 diabetes and 
type 2 diabetes
PRELIMINARY RESEARCH
Preliminary research
==PAGE:18==
14
and thereby extremely high levels of glucose in the blood. Both ketoacidosis and hyperglycaemia are 
severely dangerous, and complications are shown in Table 1 can occur for all people with diabetes if 
they are ignored (Everything You Need to Know About Diabetes, 2021). The higher the blood glu­
cose levels are and the longer you live with it, the greater is the risk of getting complications.
4.1.2	 Symptoms of diabetes
To treat diabetes most effectively and in a timely manner, before serious complications occur, you 
must know you have diabetes and be diagnosed by a health professional. Major diabetes symptoms 
are caused by a high level of blood glucose, and the higher the glucose levels are, the more noticea­
ble the symptoms will be (Diabetes, 2020). Symptoms for type 1 diabetes can begin drastically and 
dramatically and are mostly seen in children, young adults, and adolescents. On the contrary, people 
with type 2 diabetes often do not experience symptoms, or the symptoms develop slowly. A complete 
list of general symptoms and symptoms especially occurring in type 1 and type 2 diabetes can be seen 
in Table 2. The list is however not a rule-of-thumb, as 1) symptoms can vary a lot from one person 
to another and 2) symptoms are similar to many other medical conditions and thereby you might not 
have diabetes (How to Avoid a Diabetes Misdiagnosis, 2021). Diabetes symptoms can be very mild 
and hard to spot, so therefore some find it easy to dismiss them or mistake them for other conditions. 
In either case, diabetes with and without symptoms must be taken seriously!
4.1.3	 Causes and risks of diabetes
Each type of diabetes has its cause of occurrence. For type 2 diabetes and prediabetes it is mainly 
genetics and lifestyle factors that play a role. If you share genes with your family members who have 
been diagnosed with diabetes there is a higher chance of you also inheriting diabetes. Being obese or 
overweight and not having an active and healthy lifestyle also increases the risk of developing diabe­
tes (Everything You Need to Know About Diabetes, 2021). Table 3 summarizes all the risks that may 
develop type 2 diabetes.

Increased thirst

==PAGE:19==
15
Gestational diabetes may be developed 
in pregnant women due to hormones 
in the placenta that interfere with insu­
lin response (Diabetes Causes, 2021). 
Type 1 diabetes is however more diffi­
cult as doctors and experts do not have 
exact reasons for the cause. In some 
cases, genes may play a role, as for 
type 2 diabetes, in other cases age may 
have an influence (mostly kids, teens 
and young adults develop T1D) and in 
other cases, a virus may attack the im­
mune system that can destroy the pro­
duction of insulin in the pancreas.
4.1.4	Prevention and treatment
Type 1 diabetes can currently not be 
prevented but can be treated and man­
aged by taking medications (either 
drugs by mouth or injections) (What Is 
Type 1 Diabetes, 2021). Insulin is the 
main treatment which is injected into 
the body and acts as the “real” insulin 
that the pancreas is not able to produce. 
Prevention and treatment of type 2 di­
abetes is very different from person 
to person however, in most cases the 
diagnosed person can do a lot him-/
herself. This mainly includes starting 
and keeping a healthy diet (eating few­
er carbohydrates, choosing food lower 
in fat and calories), regular exercise 
(avoiding inactivity for long periods) 
and losing weight. For some people 
medication in form of drugs and injec­
tions (i.e., metformin, DDP-4 inhib­
itors, insulin, etc.) can also help treat 
type 2 diabetes. The goal in both treat­
ment scenarios is to decrease glucose levels in blood and keep it within a range that has been set by 
the diagnosed person in collaboration with his/her doctor. 
Medication that decreases blood glucose levels in your body (e.g., insulin), must simultaneously be 
taken carefully as the blood glucose levels can go beyond the set range and become too low, also 
called hypoglycaemia. This state can equally be dangerous as hyperglycaemia, with complications as 
fainting, rapid heartbeat, sweating, dizziness, confusion, anxiety, loss of consciousness etc.
4.1.5	 Diagnosis and regular check-up
In Denmark, it is your general practitioner or the doctors at the hospital who makes the diagnosis.

HbA1c, which tells the average number of 
your blood glucose over the past 2-3 months 
(8-12 weeks). This long-term blood glucose 
level can be translated into a so-called medi­
um glucose level, which is a calculated aver­
age that tells what the blood glucose levels 
would have been on the daily basis (Table 
4). Long-term blood glucose level is meas­
ured in mmol/mol, and medium glucose lev­
el is mmol/L. To be diagnosed with diabetes, 
long-term blood glucose level must for most 
people be above 48 mmol/mol or 7.7 mmol/L 
(Langtidsblodsukker (HbA1c) og type 2-di­
abetes, n.d.). However, values do vary from 
person to person and it is therefore always a good idea to talk to the doctor. 
Each third to sixth month patients must go to the doctor to measure the long-term blood glucose level 
again. Here the doctor checks the blood glucose levels and makes modifications to the treatment if 
needed (this includes medication or recommendation for diet and exercise). To decrease risks of fur­
ther complications, the boundary of long-term and medium glucose levels for type 1 and type 2 dia­
betes are listed in Table 5. If you already have a heart disease, your blood glucose levels fluctuate too 
much or of if you have lived with diabetes for several years, your boundaries can be slightly higher.
4.1.6	 Conclusion: Diabetes is different from person to person
Describing both the definition of diabetes, complications, symptoms, causes and risks of it, as well 
as prevention and treatment when diagnosed, it can be concluded that iabetes is a disease that is very 
different from person to person. Beyond being diagnosed with different types of diabetes, different 
people can experience different symptoms, have different causes and risks of being diagnosed based 
on different genetic or lifestyle factors, develop different complications at different periods of time or 
have different treatment plans that work differently for the individual. There are therefore no general 
guidelines, rules, or recommendations that all people with diabetes must follow.

Blood sugar values in average for the past 2 - 3 months.
Medium glucose values
Calculated average of blood sugar value on a daily base.
Blood glucose values
Blood sugar values measured at a specific times.

4.2	 BLOOD GLUCOSE MEASUREMENT
In case you take insulin that gives risks of hypoglycaemia it is highly recommendable to control your 
blood glucose levels in parallel. By measuring the glucose levels around meals and before bedtime 
you: 1) get to know how these factors affect your blood levels, 2) can regulate insulin doses, so that 
when you inject insulin, it will not go below normal and 3) take more insulin if your blood glucose is 
too high (Insulinbehandling, n.d.).
Since people with type 1 diabetes take insulin, it is highly recommendable to measure their blood 
glucose. The same applies for insulin dependent persons with type 2 diabetes. Persons with type 2 
diabetes who do not take insulin can measure blood glucose if they wish, it is not a standard recom­
mendation.
Measuring blood glucose levels can help 
people with diabetes detect and control 
general potential deviations, that is be­
yond factors affected by insulin (Blod­
sukker og måling, u.d.). All people have 
an increase in blood glucose after for 
example eating. The difference between 
people with and without diabetes is how 
high your blood glucose levels get and 
the time it takes to fall back to normal. 
This can be seen in Figure 2, made by 
the be author. 
The percentage of increase depends also 
on the different types of food and the 
amount of it eaten. An example of three 
various food types and their effect on 
the blood glucose can be seen in Figure 
3, made by the author. If you measure 
your blood glucose before and after you 
have eaten, you can learn what types of 
food gives a high increase and thereby 
eliminate or reduce them from your dai­
ly meals to decrease the risk of hyperg­
lycaemia and further complications.
The same applies to people with dia­
betes who take medication, or who ex­
ercises on and off. Different lifestyle 
factors, like diet, exercise, medication have a major effect on blood glucose, and by measuring it reg­
ularly you can learn what factors your body like or dislike. Factors that your body does not like you 
can reduce or eliminate and change it with factors that your body does like. After some modifications 
to your lifestyle factors due to regular measurement of blood glucose, you can get more regulated 
blood glucose, thereby reducing risks for further complications.

4.3	 FIRST SCOPING
The focus of the first diamond in the research space was to establish a background for the project, in­
cluding the definition of diabetes, symptoms and risks, prevention and treatment, as well as the diag­
nosis at the doctor. This background knowledge covered all four types of diabetes, mainly focussing 
on the difference between them.
As it was concluded in section 4.1.6 diabetes is very different from person to person. Especially be­
tween the four different types of diabetes, the life quality and life conditions may vary a lot. In this 
project, the focus is chosen to be on persons with type 2 diabetes who manage a lot of their daily 
life and treatment of their disease themselves. 
Some persons with type 2 diabetes are given insulin as part of their medication. As for persons with 
type 1 diabetes, one must measure their blood glucose if they are treated with insulin to control that 
values are steady. However, measuring blood glucose can in general help people with diabetes de­
tect and control potential deviations, that is beyond factors affected by insulin. These factors can be 
reduced or eliminated to decrease the risk of hyperglycaemia and further complications. People with 
type 2 diabetes who do not take insulin may measure their blood glucose if wanted, it is not a standard 
recommendation given by the healthcare system.
The goal of the project is to improve the user experience of measuring blood glucose, it will be most 
interesting to explore the current state of persons with type 2 diabetes who are not insulin-treated 
about measuring their blood glucose themselves. Additionally, it will be interesting to understand 
why some measure their blood glucose and others do not, as well as explore the potential of motivat­
ing the non-measuring group to measure their blood glucose themselves regularly.
As seen in Figure 4, the project scopes from this point on to only focus on persons with type 2 di­
abetes who do not take insulin (red circle). This group of persons include both experienced and 
non-experienced users in relation to measuring their blood glucose.

Studies and research that previously have been made con­
cerning the topic of type 2 diabetes and self-management of 
blood glucose must be investigated and analysed to under­
stand the success of these processes and the space we are 
heading towards in the project. Furthermore, we wish to de­
fine a need for a new methodology. 
In this chapter, you will therefore read some examples of 
previous studies and work. This is followed by a discussion, 
where research questions related to this project are defined 
and used for further investigation throughout the process.
RELATED WORK
==PAGE:26==
22
Type 2 diabetes, blood glucose measurement, self-management, etc. are topics within this project 
that several previous studies have researched upon and created solutions for. In this section, related 
work and studies are described with a focus on methodology to better understand the success of these 
processes and define a need for a new methodology. 
(Catherine H Yu et al., 2014) focused their research on testing an evidence-based, theory-driven 
website that was created for persons with type 2 diabetes for self-management. Focus groups and in­
dividual usability testing sessions were conducted to assess the following factors: acceptability, sus­
tainability, strengths and weaknesses of the website. Through the tests, it was concluded that several 
healthcare needs were still unmet, including access to tailored knowledge, mechanisms to control and 
track their disease as well as opportunities to share the experience with other persons with diabetes.
Together with persons with diabetes and health professionals, (Giuseppe Fico et al., 2019) developed 
a self-management system for diabetes through a user-centred design approach focusing thereafter 
on evaluating the use and acceptance of this system. The different stakeholders were involved in the 
design, development and evaluation phases to define scenarios and personas as well as develop the 
system. Thereafter the system was evaluated based on the use, compliance, attractiveness and per­
ceived usefulness. In their research (Giuseppe Fico et al., 2019) state the system was co-designed and 
that involvement of patients in the process contributed to better adoption of the self-management tool. 
However, in their process, the users (persons with diabetes and health professionals) collaborated on 
different definitions of  scenarios, which thereafter were evaluated through interviews. In relation to 
creating personas, (Giuseppe Fico et al., 2019) defined four types of supporting factors for self-man­
agement of diabetes which was thereafter used to create and evaluate mock-ups through focus groups 
and interviews. The final evaluation feedback from the users was received through additional usabil­
ity tests and interviews. 
(Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) created a mobile health app to enhance the ability of self-managing 
and to avoid further complications by including and reinforcing the patients’ social environment, 
consisting of family, household, and community, into the app. Before establishing an app, (Wayne 
W Zachary et al. 2017) have not identified need features or functions that the app should contain, nor 
the user experience or design of the app. Therefore, they used a participatory design process to de­
fine these aspects for a better product. Through so-called forums end-users, interface designers and 
health professionals were included to explore different functions that define the ideal user experience. 
As a result, (Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) discovered problems that end-users were living within 
their daily life as well as suggested functions that the team found original and highly useful. Howev­
er, (Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) discuss that the outcome of this participatory design was highly 
focused on functions that the app could not include or incorporate. Suggested features, according to 
(Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) were more advanced and sophisticated than they hoped to build in 
future versions. 
5.1	 DISCUSSION 
Through initial research and relevant work analysis, it was found out that several studies have ex­
plored the topic of type 2 diabetes and self-management of blood glucose. A couple of papers were 
found describing the process of designing and developing apps and systems for persons with type 
2 diabetes. Both user-centred and participatory design approaches were used by including potential 
end-users to help improve the concept and usability of it. 
RELATED WORK
Related work
==PAGE:27==
23
(Catherine H Yu et al., 2014) used a user-centred approach to test their evidence-based and theo­
ry-driven website with potential users. Through interviews and focus groups several needs were 
unmet as some important functions were lacking. In this research, it is most interesting to discuss 
whether these needs and functions were accommodated if (Catherine H Yu et al., 2014) invited the 
users earlier in the process to form the website. 
(Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) started by creating an app followed by a participatory design process 
to improve the app with the needed functions and features. It seemed that the process, including 
both end-users, designers and health professionals, was successful in gathering a high amount of 
valuable knowledge. However, some features could still not be included or incorporated due to their 
complexity and sophistication. Why have (Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) not started by following 
a participatory design process prior to creating an app? It is interesting to see that the users through 
the so-called forums could identify issues and missings that the team could not include. The question 
is whether these functions and features could have been included in the app already if the team had 
started identifying the users need before spending resources on developing the product.
In the last research example, (Giuseppe Fico et al., 2019) mention both user-centred design and 
co-design as their process method to involve patients in the development process. However, the 
methods used to design and evaluate the system where interviews and focus groups. It did not seem 
that the patients were collaborating with the designers to develop the system solution, and therefore 
it may seem questionable in regards to how much the patients were involved in designing the product. 
Several other papers mention co-creation in their project however it is not clear, how the process was 
executed and conducted.  
Related work research has therefore defined the need of involving the users throughout the whole 
development process, from problem definition to solution creation. Users must be included in the 
design team to ideate upon issues and ideas based on their experiences. Instead of finding missing 
features and unmet needs after the whole development process, these can be identified and incorpo­
rated early in the process while the product design has not even taken shape yet. 
Furthermore, there must be a clear definition of co-creation and the methods included within. Addi­
tionally, a description of the user’s participation and how they contributed to forming the final solu­
tion must be clear for this report. Having a clear definition and description may contribute to an easier 
discussion and reflection of the used methods.
Related work
==PAGE:28==
24
Beyond previous studies and research within the topic, sev­
eral different stakeholders have already created solutions 
(product and services) that persons with type 2 diabetes are 
offered and use in their daily life. In this chapter, we wish to 
investigate the market to define the direction the project is 
moving towards. As for the previous chapter we futhermore 
wish to explore potentials for new solutions.
Throughout the chapter, you will become familiar with so­
cial offers, media and social platforms, measuring devices 
and apps that are offered to the users. The chapter ends with 
a discussion where additional research questions are defined.
STATE OF ART
==PAGE:29==
25
Persons with type 2 diabetes are these days offered and provided with several different products and 
services to treat their diagnosis. This includes private and public offers for knowledge and support, 
media and social platforms for motivation, as well as devices for medication and measurement (med­
ication will not be covered in this section, as this is outside of the project scope defined in further 
section 8.2). In this section, an analysis of current products and services is conducted to define the 
market this project is moving towards and to explore potentials for new solutions.
6.1	 SOCIAL OFFERS
Social offers define services that either public or private sector provides to persons diagnosed with 
diabetes. The majority of these offers are free as well as voluntarily to participate in.
6.1.1	 The Danish Diabetes Association and Local Associations
Persons with type 2 diabetes can join the Danish Diabetes Association with their 90,000 members 
focus on improving life quality and conditions of living withing diabetes. Additionally, their mission 
is to increase health opportunities for people with the diagnosis as well as prevent diabetes in general 
and its further complications (Om Diabetesforeningen, n.d.). With their website, the association pro­
vides knowledge of all types of diabetes as well as inspiration for diet, exercise, mental and physical 
health. Newsletters are sent out to their members every second week with content that includes recent 
studies and research, events, discussions, etc. Furthermore, Danish Diabetes Association has social 
media pages where they reach out to persons with all types of diabetes (also those who are not diag­
nosed). Events, courses, lectures, workshops, etc. are created by the Danish Diabetes Association to 
provide knowledge, support and motivation through physical platforms. 
The Danish Diabetes Association highly support future research and thereby have conducted several 
projects to create a more detailed understanding of diabetes and the different health aspects. Persons 
with type 2 diabetes participate in most of these projects, as the Danish Diabetes Associations desire 
to learn first-hand from the users (Projekter og undersøgelser, n.d.). The users on the others side 
receive transparency and insights into the work of the Danish Diabetes Association, which may mo­
tivate them in learning more about their disease.
Additionally, Danish Diabetes Associations highly desire to reach out to all persons with diabetes, 
thereby they have created local associations that work and create events for a better knowledge, 
prevention and improved treatment of diabetes within municipalities (Lokalforeninger i Diabetes­
foreningen, n.d.).
6.1.2	 Motivational groups
Motivational groups are created by The Danish Diabetes Association to gather people with type 2 di­
abetes together where they can exercise, cook, socialise and exchange insights together. These groups 
are created by private members of the Association who wish to become instructors by guiding and 
motivating other people. Through motivational groups, people with type 2 diabetes can share ideas, 
experiences, stories with others who have the same diagnosis (Motivationsgrupper, n.d.). 
6.1.3	 Public health services
Municipalities and hospitals in Denmark provide several health offers to people with diabetes and 
their relatives. The goal of these offers is to increase knowledge about the diagnosis and prevent 
further complications. People with type 2 diabetes can sign up for different courses and lectures that 
each focus on different areas of diabetes (e.g. disease management, diet, exercise, etc.).
STATE OF ART
State of art
==PAGE:30==
26
6.1.4	 Related studies on education
(Sanbao Chai et al., 2018) evaluated how professional education of diabetes self-management may 
affect the psychological status and blood glucose levels. 118 patients were split into two groups: a 
control group receiving routine outpatient education, and an education group receiving professional 
education. The results clearly showed that patients receiving self-management education had an im­
provement in psychological status and blood glucose levels. Furthermore, the study concluded that 
diabetes may be linked to a higher risk of depression and anxiety which also may affect blood glucose 
levels. Therefore, education is highly valuable and important for improving patients mood and blood 
glucose control. 
(Yee Cheng Kueh et al., 2015) examined important factors that may impact the quality of life for 
persons with type 2 diabetes and their inter-relationships. Through questionnaires and further anal­
ysis, they could clearly identify that knowledge about diabetes, attitudes and self-management are 
important factors to increase the quality of life. 
6.2	 MEDIA AND SOCIAL PLATFORMS
Facebook is a major stakeholder for people with type 2 diabetes, as this platform provides features 
that users can operate to share knowledge with each other. Facebook groups gather people together 
to form a society around a specific topic. Posts and comments allow users to discuss anything from 
their own experiences, gained insights, or other peoples research. With these functions, people with 
type 2 diabetes can learn new aspects of their diagnosis based on others experiences as well as be 
motivated towards a healthier lifestyle. 
On the Danish Diabetes Association’s website, a list for inspiration of several different Facebook 
groups one can join is provided. These are gathered together and can be seen in Figure 5. In general, 
running a search for “Diabetes” and “Type 2 diabetes” on Facebook, over 50 different groups appear. 
This shows that a person with type 2 diabetes have several different opportunities to find a social so­
ciety to join and to share or follow discussions within any topic.
“Diabetes 2 - Danmark” is the largest Facebook group for people with type 2 diabetes in Denmark. 
With 4978 members and 3 administrators, this group forms a society where all are welcome to dis­
cuss and share insights from their own daily life. This can be experiences they had with their doctors, 
pictures of their meals, questions about medication and symptoms, etc. 
Additionally to the Facebook group, 2 of the administrators created in parallel a webpage ‘www. di­
Figure 5: Different Facebook groups for people with type 2 diabetes (Facebookgrupper 
om diabetes 2, n.d.)

abetes2danmark. dk’ that works as a library for 
members of the Facebook group. Knowledge 
and insights that are shared through the Face­
book group are gathered together and visually 
represented through appropriate categories on 
the website. The website has a great balance be­
tween real facts and personal experiences.
6.3	 MEASURING DEVICES
Blood glucose levels can these days be measured 
in several different ways using different kinds of monitors. These monitors can be found in many 
various shapes with different functions depending on the individual user needs. 
6.3.1	 Blood Glucose Monitors
A blood glucose monitor is a measuring device that tests the concentration of glucose in the blood 
at the specific point of time when a blood drop is read (Blodsukkermåling og type 2-diabetes, 2021). 
This method is called Blood Glucose Monitoring (BGM) and is defined as “a static measurement that 
provides an accurate instant glucose level” (Heinemann, 2018).
To measure blood glucose levels through BGM three product types are needed: a monitor, test strips 
and a lancet. Each product type can be found produced and developed by several companies, so de­
termining which product one shall acquire can be difficult. In most cases products are developed for 
each other meaning that some blood glucose monitors cannot accept test strips from other models, 
and instead have their own produced. 
Baxter International Inc.  has developed a list containing all blood glucose monitors and their mod­
els available in Denmark in 2019. The list contains 6 different manufacturers within a total of 101 
different monitors (Baxter International Inc., 2021). Each manufacturer has its own website through 
which persons with type 2 diabetes can buy monitors. Examples of some blood glucose monitors can 
be seen in Figure 7.
Figure 6: “Diabetes 2 - Danmark” Facebook 
and webpage logo (www.diabetes2danmark.dk) 
Figure 7: Examples of Blood Glucose Monitors
State of art
==PAGE:32==
28
Most blood glucose monitors operate on the same features. Contour NEXT will be used as an ex­
ample in the following description. In Figure 8 a visual representation of this monitor can be seen. 
Besides the main features that are seen in this figure, the manufacturers have incorporated personal 
goal areas, smartLIGHT function, Second-Chance fill and connection to CONTOUR DIABETES 
app (next section).
•
smartLIGHT function makes it easier for the user to understand his/her glucose values. De­
pending on the measured value, the light area turns either red, green or yellow, showing whether 
the user is over, within or under the specified boundary goals. 
•
Second-Chance fill is a function that allows the user to apply more blood to the same test strip 
within 60 seconds. Sometimes the applied blood is not enough to determine the value of the 
blood glucose. In most cases, the user must throw the test strip out and try again. According to 
Contour, this function is the only one available compared to the market (Contour, 2019).
Other blood glucose monitors, such as Accu-Chek Guide, include notifications and a special ejection 
button for more hygienic disposal of test strips (Accu-Chek, 2021).
The municipality and healthcare system in Denmark can support persons with diabetes economically 
with assistive devices like blood glucose monitors and test strips. This requires however that you are 
either insulin-dependent or take other medication. The municipality can additionally help determine 
which blood glucose monitor is best suited for the individual. The user can always desire another 
product or model, however, there might be additional costs included (Hjælp og støtte fra det offent­
lige, n.d.).
6.3.2	 Continuous Glucose Monitors
In recent years, new devices that can measure blood glucose levels continuously and automatically 
have entered the market. These devices require only a set-up by the users, while the rest of the time 
the monitors receive measured glucose values through a small needle and a corresponding sender. 
This method is called Continuous Glucose Monitoring (BGM) and is defined as “CGM is a dynamic 
measurement that provides information about both the current value and changes in glucose levels 
over time” (Heinemann, 2018).
CGM differs from BGM by not measuring the glucose values in the blood but instead in the subcuta­
Figure 8: Features of Contour NEXT (Contour, 2019)
State of art
==PAGE:33==
29
neous tissue. As glucose enters the bloodstream first a BGM will show values that correspond to the 
specific time when a measurement was made. Glucose proceeds into the subcutaneous tissue which 
will contribute to a delay of actual blood glucose values within 5-15 minutes. A CGM will therefore 
show blood glucose curves over a longer period of time, which is beneficial for the user who wishes 
to gain a more general, detailed and comprehensive picture of the blood glucose behaviour. 
Additionally, a CGM device typically lasts one to two weeks after which it must be disposed of and 
replaced by a new one. Some devices might also even need a calibration each day. Examples of con­
tinuous glucose monitors can be seen in Figure 9.
As for blood glucose monitors, CGM devices operate on the same basic features. The sensor meas­
ures the glucose values and sends them to the monitor. On a monitor, the user can view glucose values 
through a graph as well as see an arrow in which direction the glucose values are going. The monitor 
can additionally send customisable notifications when glucose values become too high or low, as well 
as when a connection is lost (FreeStyle Libre 2 Features, 2022). 
Continuous glucose monitors are in Denmark mostly used by persons with type 1 diabetes as they 
are insulin-dependent. The majority of persons with type 2 diabetes are not insulin-dependent which 
means their request of receiving a CGM will in most cases be rejected by the municipality. Further­
more, in Denmark people cannot buy a CGM privately, which means that getting a CGM within the 
border of the country is completely impossible for most people with type 2 diabetes. A CGM can 
however be bought in Germany or Sweden (Birkbak, 2019). 
6.4	 APPS
A quick search on Google Play for ‘Diabetes’ and ‘Type 2 Diabetes’, at least 250 apps under each 
appeared as available for free1. The top 5 most downloaded and rated apps under the search ‘Diabe­
tes’ can be seen in Figure 10. Through rough research and analysis, most apps either focus on blood 
glucose measurements, diet, exercise, or on a slight mixture of these. Following common features 
between these apps were instantly noticed:
1	
Google Play does not show the amount of found apps under a search. Only the top 250 apps 
are visible.
Figure 9: Examples of Continuous Glucose Monitors
State of art
==PAGE:34==
30
•
Log blood glucose values and additional informa­
tion and viewed through charts/graphs and lists.
•
Food database that keeps track of food intake
•
History, trends and statistics over measured data.
Some apps have also an additional exercise focus, have 
educational modules included, available coaching, and 
several other interesting features. Some of these apps 
can also directly be connected to BGM and CGM de­
vices to collect data directly from the sensor and present 
it graphically. Besides apps that focus on blood glucose 
measurements and representations of these, there is ad­
ditionally a very high amount of apps purely for record­
ing exercise and diet. Thereby, a user has almost an un­
limited amount of apps to choose between if one desires 
a platform that helps treat diabetes.
6.4.1	 Related studies on health apps
In general amount of mobile health apps, or mHealth apps, available for download in major app 
stores have increased by 57% in 2016 and reached an amount of 250,000 apps. mHealth is defined as: 
“use of mobile phones and wireless digital devices to support the achievement of health objectives, as 
diabetes” (Fatehi F., C. Gray L., W. Russell A., 2017). It has been highly emerging in the healthcare 
sector and uses devices for health services and information. As the many apps available for diabetes 
in Google Play, mHealth apps can collect, transmit, analyse, store and present data needed for the 
user. These can be health professionals that use data to be assisted in making decisions or patients that 
can make decisions based on data without the need of waiting for a doctor (Klonoff, 2013).
Several studies have shown potential benefits of mHealth systems and apps in relation to diabetes, 
including improvement of quality of diabetes care and reduction of costs. Furthermore, mHealth 
systems can contribute to an improvement of communication between the patients and health profes­
sionals, facilitation of remote monitoring of patients and enhancement of patients self-management 
of their own disease (Fatehi F., C. Gray L., W. Russell A., 2017). 
However, according to (Povalej Brzan P., Rotman E., Pajnkihar M., Klanjsek P., 2016) most of the 
apps that are developed for self-management of diabetes do not capture the users for long-term and 
thus actual self-management of diabetes cannot be achieved. The project performed a systematic re­
view of free apps in most popular app stores based on the following criteria: “apps must support all 
basic self-management tasks as, monitoring blood glucose level and medication, nutrition, physical 
exercise and bodyweight”. Based on this criteria, only nine apps out of the total 65 evaluated showed 
to be versatile and useful for successful self-management of diabetes. (Povalej Brzan P., Rotman E., 
Pajnkihar M., Klanjsek P., 2016) suggest that future apps must include more features and must be 
customised to the specific type of diabetes to capture the users for long-term use. Additional features 
may e.g. be: allow entry of exercise strength, use smartphone’s built-in sensors, send reminders and 
notifications to the user, education about self-management, communication with health professionals, 
etc. 
Report by (D. Adu M., H. Malabu U., E. O. Malau-Aduli A., Drovandi A., S. Malau-Aduli B, 2020) 
have additionally stated that several apps developed for self-management of diabetes lack education­
Figure 10: Top 5 “Diabetes” apps on 
Google Play
State of art
==PAGE:35==
31
al components that may enhance knowledge for patients behavioural change. Some apps also lack 
a description of the development and design process as well as have limited considerations of users’ 
preferences. 
6.5	 DISCUSSION
Since the goal of the project is to include the users in the design and development process, the out­
come of the project is unknown. The generated ideas and concepts depend on the users’ insights and 
experiences as well as their pains and needs. Thereby it is also unknown which type of product the 
project will end with. In either case the research of the market (section 6) was highly beneficial to 
outline the currently used products and services.
In relation to the social offers that public or private sector provide to persons with type 2 diabetes, 
many different stakeholders are enthusiastic in improving the life quality and conditions of living 
with diabetes. However, it seems that several stakeholders offer the same value to the users by invit­
ing them and their relatives to courses, lectures, events, etc. In this project it is therefore interesting 
to explore the users opinios regardring these offers and learn about their need. What do people think 
about these offers? Are they nice to have? Or are they more confusing and unnecessary? Furthermore, 
studies show that providing knowledge to patients may improve blood glucose levels and quality of 
life. However is there any difference between the different knowledge provided and will the users be 
satisfied to receive any knowledge? 
Media and social platforms have always been full with different kind of people to follow, groups to 
join, pages to read, etc. Opportunities are unlimited and diabetes-related topics are not an exception. 
Having a social account or access to online webpages opens the gate for persons with type 2 diabetes 
to endless knowledge, inspiration, motivation, etc. What do people think about this major opportunity 
and how do they manage it? How do they choose where to go and what to read? And what type of 
media or social platform do they prefer, and why?
For self-management of diabetes, several different measuring devices are available for persons with 
type 2 diabetes, including Blood Glucose Monitors and Continuous Glucose Monitors. These moni­
tors are mainly different in the way they collect data, as BGM requires manual measurements while 
CGM automatically measures glucose levels. The most interesting difference is however not the 
functionality, but the opportunity to receive or buy such monitors in Denmark. While BGM can be 
acquired by most persons with type 2 diabetes, a CGM is provided mainly for persons with type 1 di­
abetes and cannot be bought by private persons. Thereby an inequality between persons with diabetes 
arises which is additionally strengthed by the fact that a CGM can be bought in Germany or Sweden 
(Birkbak, 2019). What do persons with type 2 diabetes think about this inequality? Have they found 
any other possible opportunities that work better for them? And how do they choose which BGM to 
purchase?
As there are several thousand different apps for diabetes, how does one choose which to use? Beyond 
the studies previously made on mHealth systems and apps, in this project, it is interesting to hear the 
users opinions and experiences of current apps. More interesting is whether the project will end up 
with a digital concept or an app, due to the higher involvement of the users in the design process. 
Studies as (Fatehi F., C. Gray L., W. Russell A., 2017) discuss that future research must develop 
mHealth apps to engage more patients and to retain them over a longer period. (Povalej Brzan P., 
Rotman E., Pajnkihar M., Klanjsek P., 2016) discuss the need for additional features in apps to in­
crease usefulness for successful self-management of diabetes and to preserve users long-term. What 
if users are part of creating solutions themselves, will they design them in a way that they would find 
State of art
==PAGE:36==
32
useful and satisfying over the long term? “Research into the adoption and use of user-centred and 
sociotechnical design principles is needed to improve usability, perceived usefulness, and, ultimately, 
adoption of the technology. ” (El-Gayar O., Timsina P., Nawar N., Eid W., 2013)
In general, the market of products and services available for inidividuals is enormous. It is therefore 
interesting for further research to answer some of the questions to understand the potential of new 
solutions.

To form the project with the aim of designing and improving user experience of blood glucose meas­
urement for persons with type 2 diabetes, several different methods will be used to collect insights, 
make decisions and create solutions.
To improve this user experience designers 
must create interactions that the users find 
productive, fulfilling, satisfying, and joyful 
(Rex Hartson, Pardha Pyla, 2019). To make 
the users achieve these feelings about a prod­
uct or a system the design team must follow 
a process called UX Design, which means 
designing for user experience. In this pro­
ject, UX Design will be followed to create a 
product that is usable, useful, meaningful and 
creates emotional impact (Figure 11) for the 
whole user experience (Rex Hartson, Pardha 
Pyla, 2019).
Double Diamond
In parallel with the UX Design process, this project will follow the Double Diamond process model 
that divides the project into several phases and defines the thinking pattern, activity and outcome of 
each phase. A typical Double Diamond process follows two spaces divided into four phases: discover 
and define (problem space), develop and deliver (solution space). The thinking pattern shifts between 
convergent to divergent for each phase (Design Council, 2007). 
In this project the Double Diamond model is extended to ‘Quadruple’ Diamond consisting of four ‘di­
amonds’ and eight phases. This model can be seen on Figure 12. The four diamonds together establish 
three spaces: research space, problem space and solution space.
•
The first diamond in the research space explores the overall topic and the absolut background 
of the project as well as defines an initial scope and motivation. This formed the preliminary 
research.
•
In the second diamond more comprehensive research is made, including the research of previ­
ous studies and the current market as well as analysis of the current state. In this diamond, we 
define a further boundary for the project and a problem challenge for the workshops.
•
Three workshops are planned for the project which will form a so-called workshop space. The 
content of these covers both problem and solution space. Problem space is the third diamond 
of this project. Through the first workshop, the project will uncover several different problems, 
insights and experiences first-hand from the users. In the first half of the second workshop, the 
problem space will be scoped and defined through user pains and needs.
•
The fourth and last diamond forms the solution space and starts in the middle of the second 
workshop where ideas are generated and developed. In workshop 3 the ideas are delivered 
“User experience is the totality of the effect felt by the user before, during, and after inter­
action with a product or system in an ecology. ” (Rex Hartson, Pardha Pyla, 2019)
Figure 11: The components of UX (Rex Hartson, 
Pardha Pyla, 2019)
DESIGN PROCESS
Design process
==PAGE:39==
35
in the sense of a final solution, user journey and initial prototypes. The last diamond covers 
furthermore a small section of a so-called validation space where the project creates a higher 
fidelity prototype and test it with potential users.
The content of the ‘Quadruple’ Diamond and its correlation to the different spaces can be seen on 
Figure 12. In the second diamond an additional process, co-creation process, will be introduced 
and conducted throughout the three workshops. In solution space, Build-Measure-Learn model will 
shortly be initiated and will form the process of future work. 
Co-creation
Co-creation is a highly broad term that at times may be confused or treated similarly with terms such 
as co-design, participatory design, user-centred design, etc. These are all processes that invite the 
users into the development process where products and services are designed for their needs. The 
main difference between these terms is the questions of who and when to invite into the process as 
well as what roles these users may have. In a user-centred design process, end-users are involved to 
influence how a design must take shape. Designers can either consult the users about their needs or 
involve them in the design process through requirement formulation or usability testing (Abras C., 
Maloney-Krichmar D., Preece J., 2004). 
According to (Enerson, 2013) and (Harrington, 2020), in participatory design, end-users contribute 
to the final design by either joining the decision-making and development process or managing it on 
their own. Users create their “own” product by deciding the functionality, the content, the interface, 
Figure 12: Double Diamond process extended to ‘Quadruple’ Diamond model
Design process
==PAGE:40==
36
etc. (Hartson R., Pyla P., 2019) discuss participatory design as a democratic process where all valu­
able stakeholders are actively involved in the development process and equally heard to ensure that 
the outcome meets their needs. 
These definitions are very similar to co-creation and co-design. According to (Sanders E., Jan Stap­
pers P., 2008) co-creation is referred to the act of collective creativity, while co-design is collective 
creativity applied throughout the whole span of a design process. In both cases, all valuable stake­
holders, as users, experts, researchers, designers, etc. join together to share their knowledge and skills 
and collaborate to solve a specific problem. The goal of co-creation is to approach issues from a new 
perspective and explore areas that a design team will not have identified on their own (Livescault, 
n.d.). Participatory design and co-creation have thereby some overlapping goals, that will not be 
further discussed in this project.
In this project, co-creation/co-design process will be used to establish a closer connection with the 
users and to invite them to participate in the development process on an equal level as designers and 
experts. As collaborated creativity from all stakeholders is mainly applied in the workshop space of 
this project, co-creation is the term that will be used throughout this report.
Build-Measure-Learn
A Build-Measure-Learn process is designed as 
a feedback loop containing the three steps that a 
design team can follow to build valuable prod­
ucts fast (Figure 132). This is done by develop­
ing a low-fidelity product, measure it as early as 
possible with the users and learn from the data 
to create new ideas and build a second version 
of the product (Ries, 2017). This model is high­
ly iterative as the loop preferably must continue 
while the design team keeps testing, measuring, 
learning, updating, adding new features, etc. 
In this project, Build-Measure-Learn loop will 
be conducted once to create a prototype, test it 
and discuss the outcome. However, this tool, as 
mentioned above and will be discussed in sec­
tion 12, will form the process for future work.
A more detailed content and description of methodologies used in the the research, workshop and 
validation space will be discussed in the respective chapters.
2	
https://www.mindtools.com/pages/article/build-measure-learn.htm

In addition to researching the market and current studies (section 5 and 6), further exploration and

as well as a direction for exploration. However, designing a product or a service for a specific target 
group requires an understanding of the different stakeholders involved and their experiences related 
to the project topic. 
8.1	 STAKEHOLDER MAP
To set the stage for further research, analysis and design development a stakeholder map is created 
for the project. A stakeholder map is a visual model/representation of all stakeholders involved in the 
project and may show their relation to each other. This tool is commonly used by the design team 
in the initial design phases to explore and define the stakeholders of the design space, identify po­
tential issues and challenges, as well as create conversations and discussions within the design team 
(Giordano F., Morelli N., De Götzen A., Hunziker J., 2018). In this project, the stakeholder map is 
developed to initiate the research space within the current scope, to establish an understanding of the 
stakeholders involved in the topic, before defining a boundary of the project and initiating first-hand 
user interviews. 
The stakeholder map can be seen in Figure 14. Persons with type 2 diabetes are set in the middle 
of the circle, as they are the target group of this project. This stakeholder interacts with 8 different 
groups of people from the time of diagnosis. 
Figure 14: Stakeholder Map
FURTHER EXPLORATION AND ANALYSIS
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:43==
39
Healthcare: Persons with type 2 diabetes (T2D) are diagnosed through their general practitioner, 
doctor or nurse. These stakeholders can give a subscription to medicine that a person with T2D can 
buy at the pharmacies. Hospitals can also be involved if a patient has more severe symptoms or 
complications, have additional diseases that can conflict with diabetes or simply need extra attention. 
Danish health authorities provide guidelines and improve health opportunities for all people with and 
without diabetes.
Danish Diabetes Association: When diagnosed with diabetes patients can join the Danish Diabetes 
Association, which focus on the best interest of people with diabetes while also working on improv­
ing life quality and conditions of living with the diagnosis (Om Diabetesforeningen, n.d.). Each 
municipality has their local association that works for a better knowledge, prevention and improved 
treatment of diabetes for people living in the local area (Hillerød-Halsnæs Lokalforening, n.d.). 
Social life: Relatives and friends of people with diabetes may spend time with, can have an influence 
on their social life if they know about their diagnosis. These stakeholders can motivate the person 
with T2D in their daily life along with support and help with any required decisions. Additionally, 
relatives and friends can be influenced by persons with type 2 diabetes, if this stakeholder has certain 
diet needs and the group meet for shared dinner. If the person with T2D has a job, colleagues may 
likewise influence the centre stakeholder. 
Daily activities: Grocery shops are included in the stakeholder map as food and diet are a big part 
of the daily life of people with type 2 diabetes. The same applies to sports clubs and fitness centres if 
the centre stakeholder chooses to exercise outside their home.
Politics: People with type 2 diabetes can additionally reach out to municipalities that offer health ser­
vices and facilities on a public level. Here the centre stakeholders and their relatives can be supported 
through courses and counselling that focus on improving the individual’s health and knowledge. Re­
gions and governments follow initiatives and regulations created for a higher amount of people with 
type 2 diabetes within the whole country. As Denmark is part of the European Union (EU), people 
with type 2 diabetes are also impacted by European regulations and guidelines. 
Media: Newspapers, television, radio provide news and content that may touch upon diabetes and 
influence people with the diagnosis. Social media as Facebook or Instagram have features and con­
tent that the centre stakeholder may also interact with to gain knowledge, motivation and inspiration.
Adviser/counselling: Additional support, motivation and inspiration can be gained through a differ­
ent group of stakeholders that have skills within a specific topic or area within diabetes. Diet-related 
assistance can be given by dietitians. Therapists and coaches can help with physical and psycholog­
ical needs. Motivational groups are created by private members of the Danish Diabetes Association 
with the aim of gathering people with type 2 diabetes who can perform different activities together 
(Motivationsgrupper, n.d.).
Science and development: The last group of stakeholders focuses on engineering and developing 
products for people with type 2 diabetes as well as performing research for future improvements. 
Manufacturers of equipment produce products such as insulin pens, measuring devices, needles/lan­
cets, etc. Medical suppliers produce and deliver medicine to the healthcare system and patients. Steno 
Diabetes Centre is a hospital (a healthcare stakeholder as well) specialised in treatments, prevention 
of diabetes, research and education of health professionals within diabetes (Om hospitalet, n.d.). 
Researchers and designers are stakeholders that may include people with T2D in their process to 
improve and create products for a better daily life with diabetes. 
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:44==
40
8.2	 SYSTEM DEMARCATION
Following the Stakeholder Map, a System Demarcation analysis can be created and conducted to de­
fine boundaries for the project and interesting stakeholders to proceed with. A System Demarcation 
is a tool used for analysis of the current state, where the design team can gain an overview of the 
environment and connections between the different stakeholders in a complex system. Furthermore, 
the tool demarcates the system concerning to the project goal and motivation by splitting the map into 
six areas. This procedure then helps the design team define areas for further research, observation and 
ideation (Nielsen, 2018). 
The Stakeholder Map established an initial understanding of all involved stakeholders within the 
topic of type 2 diabetes. This outcome is used to create a system demarcation of this project where 
all relations between the multiple stakeholders are outlined (Figure 15). Analysing the map it can be 
seen that the system is complex. A greater amount of stakeholders are involved with a higher amount 
of connections between each other.  
A person with type 2 diabetes has a connection with almost all stakeholders. Besides having consul­
tations at their general practitioner or hospital and receiving medicine from the pharmacies, the user 
must contact all other stakeholders him- or herself if desired. If motivation and support are needed, 
Figure 15: System Demarcation
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:45==
41
the user can contact therapists, coachers or dietitians. If motivation and inspiration is needed, the user 
can use social media to reach out to other persons with T2D. If motivation and knowledge are needed, 
the user can join the Danish Diabetes Association or the motivational groups. Additional motivation, 
support, inspiration and knowledge can further be obtained through municipalities or Steno Diabetes 
Centre, if the user reaches out to them. If a person with T2D desires some equipment for measuring 
blood glucose e.g., the user can either contact their municipality or order directly from the manufac­
turers websites. Researchers and designers are almost the only stakeholders that reach out to the user 
first, as this stakeholder has a desire to develop solutions directly for people with T2D. These insights 
are gained through detailed research online of the different stakeholders as well reading comments 
and stories on social media from users within the target group.
Having defined the stakeholders and their relation to each other, the system can be split into 6 areas 
seen in Figure 16.
Persons with diabetes within the scope (non-insulin dependent persons with type 2 diabetes), Danish 
Diabetes Association, local associations and motivational groups are defined as part of the ‘Area of 
solution’.   The first stakeholder is the target group of this project and thereby a solution must be cre­
ated to accommodate their needs. As the project collaborates with the Danish Diabetes Association a 
solution can also be created for them and their initiatives (local associations and motivation groups). 
Problems and needs of the target group can still be resolved even if a solution targets the Danish 
Diabetes Association. 
Relatives, friends and social media have a relation to the overall problem, as these stakeholders can 
motivate people with type 2 diabetes to measure their blood glucose. They are placed within the area 
of ‘Intervention system’ as it was decided that interventions and modifications can be made within 
these stakeholders and their behaviour. A potential solution can intervene with the daily lives of rela­
tives and friends by including them e.g. into an introduction or decision making process with the us­
ers. Social media can be redesigned to accommodate the users needs to a greater extent. Additionally, 
manufacturers of equipment are included in the ‘Intervention system’ as modifications concerning a 
redesign of current measuring products can be made.
The rest of the stakeholders, besides medical suppliers, grocery shops, sports clubs, fitness centres 
and persons with diabetes outside of the scope, are part of the ‘Environment’ area, as their work and 
values are relevant for the problem. Politics intervene with their regulations and guidelines, health­
care provides knowledge and measuring equipment, advisers/counsellors support and motivate users. 
Figure 16: 6 areas of system demarcation model (Nielsen, 2018)
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:46==
42
while manufacturers and researchers create and develop products. This project will not create mod­
ifications for the healthcare or  political stakeholders, as we cannot change laws, general guidelines 
or medical recommendations. Advisers/counsellors also follow specific rules related to their skills 
and profession that we cannot change. Thereby these stakeholders are not part of the ‘Intervention 
system’.
Even though advisers/counsellors, municipalities and healthcare stakeholders are not part of the ‘In­
tervention system’ area a potential solution can affect their work or the service they deliver (defined 
in the ‘Area of effect’). A potential solution can be original or redesigned of current products, and can 
change the way e.g. doctors, municipalities or therapists deliver their service to people with type 2 di­
abetes. For example, if a solution provides education to the users or tests of long-term blood glucose 
at home, the municipalities and doctors might not be needed in the same degree to support people 
with type 2 diabetes. Manufacturers of equipment will not be affected by this project, even though 
their products can be redesigned. A potential solution will “stand alone” as the outcome of the project 
and will not remove the existing version of the product from the market.  
Stakeholders within the ‘Intervention system’ and ‘Environment’ form the ‘Area of investigation’. 
However, due to the limit of the project, a couple of stakeholders within this area will be investigated 
and further analysed. The service municipalities, motivational groups and local associations provide 
has been covered in section 6. In the same section, equipment developed by manufacturers and social 
media has also been covered. As Danish Diabetes Association is part of the project, work that has 
been made by this stakeholder and that is related to the topic will be further investigated. Additionally, 
as this project is carried out by a designer, previous studies by other designers and researchers have 
been discussed in Section 5, while further analysis will take place in the next section.
8.3	 “KNOW YOUR BLOOD GLUCOSE” PROJECT
The issue with checking your bank account every third month is that you might not know the reasons 
behind your balance. Why is it so high/low? Why is nothing happing after I have stopped spending 
money on fast food? You cannot make changes to your bank account before you know what you are 
spending money on, when and how much on a daily basis.
The same applies to blood glucose levels. When you visit the doctor every third month, you get your 
long-term blood glucose measured. The doctor might say you have a great blood glucose average, 
or he/she can say, it is slightly too high/low. Neither the doctor nor you will know what affected the 
long-term blood glucose level, hence what shall you do to decrease or maintain it for the upcoming 
control. Shall you exercise more? Shall you stop eating pasta or potatoes? What about sugar at week­
ends, am I not allowed to do that? The answer to this is: try measuring your blood glucose a couple 
of times a day and learn what makes your blood glucose fluctuate.
In 2020 Danish Diabetes Association started a project, called “Know your blood glucose”, where 16 
participants with type 2 diabetes were asked to measure their blood glucose five times a day for four 
weeks in October/November. On a piece of paper, they were forced to think about their blood glucose 
by guessing the level of it, and afterwards notating the actual level through measurement. Results 
show that people measuring their blood glucose every day have gained better insight into lifestyle 
factors that affect their blood glucose than patients only being measured every third month.
“Try to imagine that you only got your balance in your bank account every third month.  
That might give some problems. ” – Tanja Thybo, head of research in Danish Diabetes Asso­
ciation, Denmark (Kend dit blodsukker – særligt ved type 2-diabetes, n.d.).
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:47==
43
Some participants from “Know your blood glucose” project have eaten a specific type of food for a 
long time, and only first when they have measured their blood glucose, they figured out that this type 
of food has not been good for their body. This is despite the fact that the food is theoretically healthy. 
As mentioned under section “What is Diabetes?” people with type 2 diabetes often do not sense 
diabetic symptoms or negative signals, and therefore only by measuring blood glucose, participants 
from “Know your blood glucose” have been able to regulate their diet, exercise, and medication as 
well as the combination of these.
The participants felt transparency was the main benefit of measuring blood glucose, as they could 
act upon it right away. Besides seeing factors that do not benefit the body, measuring blood glucose 
also reveals factors that are good and favourable. This can increase the motivation of the individual 
towards a healthy lifestyle or personal goals (Festersen, 2021).
8.3.1	 Subsequent questionnaire and report
Subsequently after the “Know your blood glucose” project, the Danish Diabetes Association sent a 
questionary out to members of a so-called The Danish Diabetes Association’s Diabetes panel, where 
1264 answers have been gathered in the period from 5/2-2021 to 26/2-2021 (an answering percentage 
of 79 %). The Danish Diabetes Association’s Diabetes panel consists of people with both type 1 and 
type 2 diabetes and is used to receive efficient and qualified feedback to challenges, experiences, and 
problems related to diabetes, its treatment, and everyday life with it (Diabetesforeningen, 2021).
The primary purpose of the questionary was to receive quantitative results regarding blood glucose 
measurement and to map out the affecting factors of measuring blood glucose voluntarily and regu­
larly. As with “Know your blood glucose” project, the main focus was on people with type 2 diabetes, 
who do not take insulin as medication and thereby have a choice whether to measure their blood 
glucose or not (section 4.2). Responses of 901 members have been used and analysed. 
The following questions were then defined and answered in the subsequent report made by the Dan­
ish Diabetes Association in June 2021:
•   How many people with T2D measure their blood glucose?
•   How do practitioners instruct people with T2D in measuring blood glucose?
•   What do people with T2D get out of measuring blood glucose?
•   How do people with T2D view their treatment?
•   What medication do people with T2D use?
“The ambition is to get rid of medication.  I have a couple of downfalls, but I am on the right 
track.  I can see that through my blood glucose” – a participants from “Know your blood 
glucose” (Festersen, 2021).
“When I started, I figured out, that I had less control over my blood glucose than I thought. 
It became visible to me in a different way.” – a participant from “Know your blood glucose” 
(Festersen, 2021) – “It can be difficult to relate to, how my blood glucose is over the couple 
of months between long-term blood glucose measurements.  But this way, I can follow my 
blood glucose now and here. ”
“I am well aware that blood glucose measurement will not solve everything alone.  But it is 
a good way to keep me conscious about the consequences of the choices I make” – a partici­
pant from “Know your blood glucose” (Festersen, 2021).
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:48==
44
•   What measuring devices are available for people with T2D?
•   Where do people with T2D get their measuring devices?
Results of the report
The results of the report focus on everything from who measures blood glucose, sociodemographic 
and disease-related factors, doctor’s recommendations, treatment of diabetes, health and insights 
related to measuring blood glucose. The overall conclusions give a clear overview and transparency 
of possible reasons and affecting factors behind people’s decisions of measuring blood glucose vol­
untarily and regularly.
Blood glucose measurement: Of people with T2D who do not take insulin as medication 68% 
measure their blood glucose. 14.7% of these people have at the time of the survey measured their 
blood glucose daily for the past month, 57% have measured one to six times a week and 28.4% have 
measured one to three times the past month (Figure 17).
Recommendation from the doctor shows to be an important factor on whether people with type 
2 diabetes measure their blood glucose or not. 46% of the respondents have been recommended to 
measure their blood glucose by their doctor. 91.7% of these people measure their blood glucose, 
while 8.3% do not. Compared to people who did not get a recommendation from their doctor, 41.7% 
of them measure their blood glucose, while 52.9% does not (Figure 18).

further 11.3% either get their blood

or think that their blood glucose is 
stable.

a recommendation from their doc­
