measure their blood glucose, 31% 
of them state that they simply have

cess, 27% does not want to measure, cannot see the point of it or thinks it is too troublesome, and 
13.6% think it is enough with measuring their long-term blood glucose at the doctor.
An interesting part of this observation is how much the doctor has an influence on whether people 
with type 2 diabetes measure their blood glucose or not. Since most of the people who get a recom­
mendation measure their blood glucose, why do all doctors not recommend it to all diagnosed pa­
tients? Another interesting fact is the reasons behind people choosing to measure or not to measure 
their blood glucose regardless of their doctor’s recommendation. The report briefly mentions some 
overall reasons, however, it does not go deeper into people’s thoughts, actions, and behaviour, where 
a possible solution can intervene. Since the doctor does not recommend measuring blood glucose, 
why do some choose to measure it anyway, while other’s do not?
Kind of recommendations: The results from above do not mention what kind of recommendations 
the doctors give to their patients. In the report from the survey, The Danish Diabetes Association state 
that only 19.6% of people get a recommendation to measure blood glucose daily. 52.6% are recom­
mended to measure one to six times a week, while 27.9% are recommended to measure as needed or 
less frequently.
This shows that even though doctors recommend measuring blood glucose, they do not recommend 
the number of measurements that the Danish Diabetes Association find is beneficial for the patients to 
learn the most about their blood glucose (which is daily). The question is then, what are the intentions 
behind the doctor’s recommendation and why do they recommend all differently?
Measuring frequency: Most people who get a recommendation from their doctor also follows the 
recommended measuring frequency. Of those who get a recommendation to measure once a day, 
61.5% follow. Of those who get recommended to measure one to six times a week, 70.5% follow 
(Figure 19). However, as seen on, there is also a high number of people who do not follow the rec­
ommendations from their doctor. These people measure fewer times than the recommended amount. 
Only 3.2% of people who get recommended to measure one to six times a week, actually measure 
their blood glucose daily.

Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:50==
46
Besides the doctors recommending different measuring frequencies, the problem may also lay upon 
some of the patients, who do not follow the recommendation from their doctors and who decide them­
selves the process of their treatment. What are the reasons for patients not following their doctor’s 
recommendations, and do they get a better treatment process without the need of their doctor? In this 
report made by the Danish Diabetes Association it is therefore still unclear, what and who are the ex­
act reasons of people not measuring their blood glucose. Is it the patient him-/herself who follows the 
doctor’s treatment, or is it the doctor, who gives recommendations for treatment? Both user groups 
might think their suggested treatment plan is the most correct, however how can “correct” be defined, 
when different patients experience their diagnosis differently?
As previously mentioned, there are also people who measure their blood glucose despite their doctor’s 
recommendation. Looking at Figure 19 we can however see that these groups of people each measure 
in different frequencies. 11.6% of people without a recommendation from their doctor measure blood 
glucose daily, 54.6% measure one to six times a week and 33.7% measure one to three times a month. 
Why some people measure their blood glucose more often than others, is an additional unanswered 
question that the report does not address.
Measurement devices: Of people who measure their blood glucose, most of them (77%) get their 
measuring devices paid either by municipality or the region. However, 18% of them pay from their 
own pocket for the devices they need. Comparing between the two groups of people, with and with­
out a recommendation from their doctor, more people have to pay themselves for measuring devices 
if they do not have the doctors recommendation. This covers over 26.7% of all the people who do 
not get a recommendation, while this number is “only” 13.3% for people who get a recommendation.
This result means that getting a recommendation from doctor to measure own blood glucose, gives 
access to measuring devices and a higher chance of them being paid for one. This fact may lead to 
inequality in health for people with type 2 diabetes. 
Prevention and treatment: People with type 2 diabetes who measure their blood glucose more often, 
have had the diagnosis for a longer period (15+ years) and have already had one or more types of 
complications. People who do not measure their blood glucose do not have as many complications, 
which questions whether this might be the reason. If they start feeling very sick, they might start 
measuring blood glucose to control it. However, in most cases in might be too late, as most compli­
cations can be reduced by acting upon it early, also when the symptoms are not present yet (section 
4.1.2). 
People who measure their blood glucose visit the doctor more often and have had him/her to develop 
a treatment plan for them. However, these people are less satisfied with the quality of their treatment 
and do not feel that they get the help and support they need to achieve stable blood glucose values. 
This shows misconceptions and misalignments between the doctors work and the patients expecta­
tions. 
Insights: The report also states that people who measure their blood glucose have started to feel 
the symptoms and periods when their blood glucose is too high. They have also gained knowledge 
and insights into what affects their blood glucose (food and exercise), as well they feel they have more 
control of their blood glucose and feel better in general. Figure 20 illustrates these results clearly.
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:51==
47
Reflection of the report
Danish Diabetes Association sent out a questionnaire to the participants involved in the project 
“Know your blood glucose” to gain insights into the outcome and success of the project. This ques­
tionnaire contained quantitative questions that gathered data to be analysed in the subsequent report, 
also made by the Danish Diabetes Association. For the report, the association selected a couple of 
questions, that they wished to answer and reflect upon (section 8.3.1). These questions have been 
answered through graphs and short conclusions.
Reflecting on the outcome of the questionary, additional questions did emerge in the different topics. 
Why do doctors not have a standard recommended procedure to a treatment plan, and why do some 
people with type 2 diabetes measure their blood glucose and others do not? Why do people measure 
their blood glucose at different times and frequencies, and how come more people who get a recom­
mendation from their doctor also get access to free measuring devices?
The results of the project were summarized through a report that has been highly successful to define 
initial perspectives, thoughts, and behaviours of people with type 2 diabetes, who do not take insulin. 
However, these initial results can be analysed further so that deeper and more detailed reasons can be 
found. With deeper and more detailed knowledge, a problem definition can be defined and a solution 
that solves the most desired and essential needs and pains of the user can be created.
The quantitative data from “Know your blood glucose” project can be used to set qualitative ques­
tions up, that can unfold additional topics or unexplored needs and pains. In this master project, we 
therefore use the initial knowledge from “Know your blood glucose” project as a great starting point 
to dig deeper into the topic of measuring blood glucose regularly and voluntarily, and understand the 
deeper reasons behind peoples experiences, thoughts and behaviours.

8.4	 MASTER THESIS BY YOSEF KHODR EL-FIL
At the beginning of the year 2021, ½ wrote his master thesis in collaboration with University of 
Copenhagen, Technical University of Denmark, and the Danish Diabetes Association. The focus 
of the project was to explore insights into the impact of blood glucose levels using semi-structured 
self-monitoring of blood glucose (El-Fil, 2021, Abstract). He was part of the “Know your blood 
glucose” project with the Danish Diabetes Association and has performed surveys, interviews, and 
focus groups to gather insights and reflections regarding participants’ experience and outcome of 
measuring their blood glucose regularly. Through research, Yosef found several studies that show ev­
idence that long-term blood glucose, HbA1c, can be decreased by day-to-day self-monitoring blood 
glucose. However, none of the studies has gained insights into patients reflections or experiences that 
document how, why, and what causes the reduction of long-term blood glucose (El-Fil, 2021, Intro­
duction).
Results of the master thesis
From the initial surveys, Yosef conducted with the “Know your blood glucose project”, the major 
outcome was that day-to-day self-monitoring of blood glucose helped the participants gain insights 
into what lifestyle factors can affect their blood glucose. After 30 days of participating in the project, 
the participants could see a pattern in how their blood glucose levels fluctuated and could thereafter 
regulate their lifestyle from the knowledge they have obtained. Most participants stated they were 
surprised by the difference between their expected and actual blood glucose levels and the difference 
between the expected levels and how they felt. Measuring blood glucose regularly and seeing the 
consequences their blood glucose levels have on their lifestyle clearly motivated the participants 
to continue. 94% of the participants would recommend self-monitoring of blood glucose to others, 
while 100% of the participants see this method as a way to obtain knowledge of what factors have an 
impact on blood glucose levels. Some of the disadvantages of this project were that the participants 
had difficulties in remembering to measure their blood glucose and that the participants were worried 
about the scar tissues occurring from regular measurements (El-Fil, 2021, Survey 2). Some other re­
sults, negative and positive experiences, can be seen in Figure 21.
Some participants from “Know your blood glucose” project continued with measuring their blood 
glucose levels, however in a different frequency. Rather than measuring blood glucose daily, these 
people performed their measurements weekly or monthly. In his master thesis, Yosef states the rea­
son for this might be the intensive process of “Know your blood glucose” project, and that a specific 
period of daily measurement can be enough for the participants to measure their blood glucose more 
loosely hereafter (El-Fil, 2021, Survey 3) . 
Reflection of the master thesis
The research that Yosef Khodr El-Fil has done in his master thesis clearly states that regular measure­
ment of blood glucose levels can reduce long-term blood glucose values. Through his research, Yosef 
concluded that the individual long-term blood glucose values are decreased due to higher knowledge 
and insights into what can affect blood glucose to fluctuate. This knowledge and insights can help 
people with type 2 diabetes make better choices regarding what to eat, when to eat or when to ex­
ercise, so that blood glucose values are kept within a range that does not increase the risk of further 
health complications (section 4.1.1).
Name of master thesis: “Empowering Patients with Non-Insulin Treated Type-2-Diabetes 
Through Self-Monitoring of Blood Glucose Levels: An Exploratory Study”
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:53==
49
The results that Yosef gained in his master thesis are used as a justification for this master project. 
Some of the participants that Yosef sent a survey concerning the “Know your blood glucose” project, 
mentioned that they have difficulties in remembering to measure their blood glucose, that they felt 
more stressed and sick knowing that they must measure their blood glucose, or they got scar issues 
that they began to worry about. Other participants mentioned, that measuring blood glucose daily 
was too expensive, it required change of everyday life to fit in blood glucose measurement at specific 
times, or  that the values received from the measuring devices confused one more gave certainty. 
Some of these problems are based on physical contact with the product or personal behaviour regard­
ing blood glucose measurement. These problems can thereby be decreased or eliminated by improv­
ing the user experience of measuring blood glucose regularly and voluntarily. Yosef’s master thesis is 
selected as a basis for the master project as his results support the reason for further investigation of 
the topic and improvement of the user experience regarding blood glucose measurement.
Figure 21: Results from Yosef’s master thesis: negative and positive experiences regarding 
self-monitoring of blood glucose.
Further exploration and analysis

8.5	 CONCLUSION TO SECTION 8
Designing a product or a service for a specific target group requires detailed research and understand­
ing of the different stakeholders involved and their experiences related to the project topic. In this 
section a further exploration and analysis of the research space focusing on the stakeholders, their 
relation to each other and their experiences within the topic is made. This is to gain background in­
sights before proceeding with first-hand interview analysis with the target group (people with type 2 
diabetes that is non-insulin dependent). The outcome from this section is:
Stakeholder Map:
•
Through a Stakeholder Map, it was discovered that a person with type 2 diabetes has 8 different 
stakeholder groups that surround him/her and that the user interacts with. These include Health 
care, Diabetes associtation, Social life, Daily activities, Politics, Media, Advisers/Counsellors 
and Science & development. 
System Demarcation:
•
A System Demarcation model uncovered the complexity of the system, which showed a high 
amount of relations between the different stakeholders. A person with type 2 diabetes has a 
connection with almost all stakeholders. Besides having consultations at their general practi­
tioner or hospital and receiving medicine from the pharmacies, the user must contact all other 
stakeholders him- or herself if desired.
•
A potential solution will mainly be used by persons with diabetes or the Danish Diabetes As­
sociation (‘Area of solution’). This project will not create modifications for the healthcare or 
political stakeholders, as we cannot change laws, general guidelines or medical recommenda­
tions. Advisers/counsellors also follow specific rules related to their skills and profession that 
we cannot change. These stakeholders are not part of the ‘Intervention system’.
“Know your blood glucose” project:
•
Danish Diabetes Association conducted a project in 2020, “Know your blood glucose”, where 
the participants were tasked to measure their blood glucose five times a day for four weeks. 
Results show that people measuring their blood glucose daily have gained better insight into 
lifestyle factors that affect their blood glucose than patients only measuring every third month. 
Additionally, the project increases the motivation of the individual towards a healthy lifestyle 
or personal goals.
•
Further studies and research by the Danish Diabetes Association (901 user responses) showed 
that 68% of people who do not take insulin measure their blood glucose and only 14.7% meas­
ure their blood glucose daily.
•
Studies also show that doctors provide their patients with different recommendations regarding 
the measurement of blood glucose and the frequency of this. This seems to be an important 
factor in whether people with type 2 diabetes measure their blood glucose or not. Half of the 
people without a recommendation, think that lack of this is precisely the reason. Only 19.6% of 
the responses were recommended to measure their blood glucose daily.
•
The report by the Danish Diabetes Association briefly mentions some overall reasons why peo­
ple choose to measure their blood glucose. It does not go deeper into people’s thoughts, actions, 
and behaviour, where a possible solution can intervene. Additional questions emerged from this 
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:55==
51
report that will be further explored and analysed in the following sections.
Master Thesis by Yosef Khodr El-Fil:
•
In collaboration with the Danish Diabetes Association Yosef Khodr El-Fil wrote his master 
thesis on the topics of “Type-2-Diabetes” and “Self-Monitoring of Blood Glucose Levels”. Re­
search studies in Yosef’s thesis showed evidence that long-term blood glucose can be decreased 
by day-to-day self-monitoring blood glucose, but not why, how and what causes this reduction. 
Through his research and surveys, Yosef concluded that the individual long-term blood glucose 
values are decreased due to higher knowledge and insights into what can affect blood glucose 
to fluctuate. 
•
Measuring blood glucose daily also creates issues and problems which are based on physical 
contact with the product or personal behaviour regarding blood glucose measurement. These 
problems can be decreased or eliminated by improving the user experience of measuring blood 
glucose regularly and voluntarily. Yosef’s master thesis is selected as a basis for the master 
project as his results support the reason for further investigation of the topic and improvement 
of the user experience regarding blood glucose measurement.
Further exploration and analysis
==PAGE:56==

EXPLORATORY
INTERVIEWS
All background research has been made, but we have not 
talked directly with the target group of this project yet. In this 
chapter, you will be introduced to the exploratory interviews, 
which were planned and conducted with the purpose of un­
derstanding the topic of diabetes from first-hand sources and 
the users who live with the diagnosis. 
The results from the interviews are then processed, analysed 
and discussed with the focus of defining a direction for fur­
ther work.
==PAGE:57==
53
An interview is a powerful and fundamental research method used commonly in the exploratory 
design phase to collect firsthand user experiences, opinions and perceptions around a specific top­
ic (Martin B., Hanington B., 2012). Interviews are particularly useful to capture personal stories, 
explore user pains or detail the reasons behind actions (Vianna M., Vianna Y., Adler I., Lucena B., 
Russo B., 2011).
In this project, interviews are set up as part of the research space to understand the topic of diabetes 
from first-hand sources and the users who live with the diagnosis. By talking with some different us­
ers, we can get a more clear picture of how it is to be diagnosed and live with diabetes, what elements 
are most troublesome and irritating in everyday life, how are some of the issues resolved and how 
their relationship is with other stakeholders or products. 
Since the interviews are conducted for exploratory purposes, the execution and format of the meth­
od are rather unstructured and flexible for diversions. At this stage, the only desired outcome is to 
understand the user and shape a relationship for future purposes. Therefore, the interviews are more 
conversational between the interviewer and the interviewee, where different topics defined before the 
interview are covered. The interview relies on the researcher who must guide the session and collect 
the necessary information within the allocated time frame. Furthermore, the unstructured format of 
the interview creates a more comfortable environment for the users, which is more preferred due to 
the first contact between the interviewer and the interviewee (Martin B., Hanington B., 2012). 
9.1	 STRUCTURING AND PLANNING INTERVIEWS
9.1.1	 Participants
A major part of the interview preparation is to recruit users for the interview. At this stage of research 
space, the project focuses on people with type-2 diabetes, who do not take insulin (section 4.3). This 
includes both people who measure and do not measure their blood glucose. The additional goal of the 
interviews is therefore to understand both user groups and their reasons for self-monitoring of blood 
glucose.
Users for these interviews are found 
through a Facebook group, called: “Dia­
betes 2 Danmark” and word of mouth. A 
post was created to find people willing 
to share their stories, experiences and 
everyday issues concerning type-2 diabe­
tes (Figure 10). When writing the post it 
was important to show transparency of the 
project by highlighting the process and its 
aim. This was done to provide a context 
for the interviews and what the users in­
sights will be used for. Providing this in­
formation may increase amount of persons 
signing up to share their insights to a good 
purpose. Additionally, the purpose of the 
interview was also important to emphasize 
as the users must not be surprised over the 
topics that are asked about. Providing the 
Figure 22: Facebook posts for recruiting users for 
exploratory interview
EXPLORATORY INTERVIEWS
Exploratory interviews
==PAGE:58==
54
user with this information can contribute to the users preparing themselves for the interview. This 
can create discussions where important information is avoided or manipulated. However, life with 
diabetes can be a personal topic to discuss with a total stranger, therefore providing this information 
can potentially contribute to more people signing up for an interview, and for some users to mentally 
prepare to share knowledge within personal topics.
In total six participants signed up for an hour-long interview, where two could unfortunately not 
make it after all. Of the four users, two of them measures their blood glucose daily, while the two 
others do not measure their blood glucose at all. Even though four participants do not represent the 
majority of people with diabetes, the distribution of the user groups (50/50) for the general outcome 
of the interviews could is appropriate. Conducting these interviews gives a better understanding of 
life with diabetes and it is possible to use knowledge from both user groups; people who measure 
their blood glucose and those who do not.
9.1.2	 Procedure
Topics and questions: Having recruited the participants, specific topics and questions must accord­
ingly be prepared (Figure 24, Figure 25 and Figure 26). Some questions act as mandatory that start a 
conversation, while others act more as back-up used if the interview comes to a standstill. The reason 
for this is again due to the unstructured format of the interview, where the user is allowed to speak 
with little or no interruptions. Additional questions from the interviewer, aside from the plan, were 
furthermore allowed in case detailing or further explanation from the user was needed.
Capturing insights: Since the outcome of the interviews will be qualitative data it must be analysed 
by identifying common insights and related themes (What are Affinity Diagrams, 2020). Affinity di­
agramming is a thematic analysis method used in the design process to capture all observations and 
insights collected from research and meaningfully cluster them into themes that have similar intent, 
problem or story (Martin B., Hanington B., 2012). With these themes, it is possible to highlight the 
most problematic areas, discuss the importance of the different topics, and compare the different user 
stories. 
9.2	 RESULTS
The four interviews gave both different and similar outcomes compared to each other. Three of the 
interviewees had similar stories and opinions regarding their diagnosis, their visits to the doctor and 
their knowledge regarding blood glucose. The fourth interviewee stood slightly out as she has rarely 
given her disease a thought and has not changed her daily habits after her diagnosis. However, she 
has similar opinions regarding the visit to her doctor, as the three other interviewees, which in her 
situation might be the reason not to measure her blood glucose. 
All results from the interviews can be seen in Appendix, p. 2.
Through affinity diagramming 11 themes were defined: Social, 
Training, Knowledge, Diet, Blood glucose, Long-term blood 
glucose (HbA1c), Health professional, Personal, Equipment, 
Internet and Measuring blood glucose. Some of the quotes can 
be applicable in several groups and therefore a decision was 
made to allow multiple positions of the quotes. This means that 
some of the same quotes can be seen under multiple themes. 
This is done to perceive and outline themes that are mentioned 
and talked about the most, as well as to identify themes with the

About yourself
•   Can you tell me a little about yourself and your history with type-2 diabetes?
-   What is your occupation?
-   How long have you had diabetes?
-   How did you get diagnosed with diabetes?
-   Does everybody in your surroundings know that you have diabetes?
-   Do you have anybody to help you in your everyday life in relation to diabetes?
Relationship with the doctor
•   Do you visit the doctor in relation to your diabetes? If yes, how often?
-   Can you tell me what happens when you visit the doctor?
-   You have received any treatment plans from your doctor?
-   Do you follow it, and why?
-   Have you doctor recommended or notified you about what and when to eat and exercise?
Medication
•   Do you take any medication in relation to diabetes? What medication?
-   Is it your doctor who has recommended the medication? Or did you choose to take it yourself?
Everyday life
•   What does your everyday life most often look like?
-   Any specific exercise of diet habits?
•   Do you think about what you do and what you eat everyday?
-   Why do you have those thoughts?
•   In general do feel you everyday life is different after you have been diagnosed with diabetes?
-   Are there any specific situations, where you or your body can feel that you have diabetes?

evening schools or from friends and families. The in­

must enlighten about all possibilities and must de­

Some of the interviewees have a great experience 
with the Facebook group “Diabetes 2 Danmark”, 
as they have learned a lot from others experiences, 
gained motivation and feel support between members 
of the group. A lot of the information the interview­
ees find on different websites or receive from their 
doctors only show one side of the story. In the Face­
book group members can share their own experienc­
es, which shows multiple truths about the disease and 
multiple different ways of how to treat it. Some of the 
other interviewees see the purpose of the Facebook 
group, however, it is a social platform, so a negative 
tone can appear as well as stories that are exaggerated 
and distorted. They feel that a good balance between 
personal experiences and professional cases would be 
great to use to learn about their disease. 
Health professional: Taking a first glance at the cat­
egory there is an unfortunate higher amount of nega­
tive experiences than positive. One interviewee feels 
that her doctor makes fun of her and her lifestyle, an­
other interviewee feels that her doctor criticizes her 
or negatively approach her, while nearly all of the 
interviewees feel that the advice and recommenda­
tions they receive from the health professionals are 
outdated, non-relevant or non-existing. These experi­
ences take a lot of space in their daily lives as all in­
terviewees must visit their doctor at least every third 
month for a check-up and long-term blood glucose 
measurement (section 4.1.5). One interviewee even 
mentioned that she only visits her doctor because it is 
a requirement. Otherwise, she would not do it. These 
negative experiences related to the visits to the doctor 
contributes furthermore to low motivation, which is 
not what the patients need when they must change/
adjust their lifestyle (section 4.1.4). 
In relation to blood glucose measurements, the doc­
tors either do not mention this to their patients or 
directly do not recommend it. Some doctors tell the 
patients their current long-term blood glucose value, 
but do not detail it with reasons why it is such high. 
The doctors and other health professionals can give 
some recommendations and treatment plans for their 
patients or customers, however, most of them are use­
less and does not help in decreasing blood glucose 
levels. 
In general, the interviewees feel that the knowledge 
Figure 28: Insights from health profession­
al theme
Exploratory interviews
==PAGE:64==
60
and recommendations they receive from their doctors 
and other health professionals vary a lot and that the ap­
proach between different professionals cannot be com­
pared or measured against each other. This creates a mi­
nor inequality between the patients which can be clearly 
seen in the Facebook group “Diabetes 2 Danmark”.
Diagnosis of diabetes is furthermore a topic that some 
of the interviewees put a lot of emphasis on during the 
interview. Through their stories, it was found out that 
diagnosis of diabetes happened by chance, and not be­
cause the patient visited the doctor suspecting diabetes, 
or the doctor summon the patient for a diabetic obser­
vation. One interviewee mentioned that she was diag­
nosed after she has been pushed to visit the doctor by her 
colleague due to her long-lasting illness. After several 
observations, the doctor finally discovered an extreme­
ly high long-term blood glucose level (130 mmol/mol) 
and sent her to the hospital for additional observations. 
Measuring blood glucose: Interviewees who measure 
their blood glucose have a great experience with the 
process and feel highly motivated to continue, due to 
an exciting process, the positive progression and higher 
knowledge of affecting factors on blood glucose. Mostly 
they measure their blood glucose after a meal or an ex­
ercise which can be up to five times a day. These inter­
viewees mentioned furthermore, that they in beginning 
were scared or not convinced to measure their blood 
glucose themselves. However public evening schools 
offered by the municipality were good at explaining 
the process and the benefits as well as they showed and 
learned how to use the different measuring equipment. 
These types of educational groups are highly beneficial 
and the interviewees feel that every newly diagnosed 
patient must go through this course. 
Interviewees who do not measure their blood glucose 
have some different reasons for that. One states very 
clearly that she does not want to perform a finger-prick 
blood test as it can hurt, it can give scar tissues and it 
can become addictive. Furthermore, she would feel that 
blood glucose tests would interrupt her everyday tasks/
chores, which will annoy both her and her workspace. 
The other interviewee has previously measured her 
blood glucose, however, she cannot remember why she 
stopped. Her doctor never mentioned anything about 
the possibility of self-monitoring of blood glucose and 
therefore she has never given this process a thought 
Figure 29: Insights from measuring 
blood glucose theme
Exploratory interviews
==PAGE:65==
61
again. Furthermore, she is bad at taking notes and remembering some of her chores, which also can 
be a reason for her not to measure her blood glucose.
Continuous Glucose Monitors (CGM) have been mentioned by some of the interviewees, both those 
who measure and those who do not measure their blood glucose. These monitors are equipment the 
interviewees highly desire so that they can track their blood glucose levels at all times and make 
better lifestyle choices. The interviewees do however also mention that it is not necessary to own a 
CGM for a longer period. A couple of weeks or months is beyond enough to learn how one’s blood 
glucose fluctuates in relation to different meals and exercises. One interviewee suggests using CGM 
as part of the diagnosis, before visiting the doctor, while another suggests using CGM for a period 
when long-term blood glucose is measured to be too high. 
9.3	 DISCUSSION
Having a semi-stuctured interview with the four participants, a lot of new insights have been gathered 
and a better understanding of how it is to live with diabetes is achieved. Even though the number of 
participants does not represent the majority of people with type-2 diabetes, several issues, problems 
and obstacles have been highlighted as well stories with positive and negative experiences were told. 
A more clear and wider picture has therefore been outlined, including themes beyond blood glucose 
and its measurement.
Different users experience different situations in their everyday life, however, all the interviewees had 
one thing in common, which is having an opinion about the following topics: knowledge regarding 
blood glucose, health professionals and measurement of blood glucose. At this stage it will make the 
most sense to choose these topics to continue the project with, however as there are several correla­
tions between these and other themes, a specific direction based on these categories cannot be picked 
yet. Talking for example about knowledge, issues related to the health professionals are also covered. 
Talking about health professionals, blood glucose, equipment and internet topics are covered. All 
themes have both positive and negative experiences that can be used to frame a direction for further 
research. It will therefore be a shame not to explore all themes further in the following workshops, as 
they all have areas in need of better solutions.
Figure 31 shows all quotes without being separated into different 
themes. The most interesting result of this figure is the amount 
of ‘Suggestions’ quotes (which is 20% of the statements) (Fig­
ure 30). All interviewees had some ideas, solutions or thoughts 
of how some of the issues (experienced themselves or have 
heard/read about) could be resolved or done better. These re­
sults demonstrate that it is not only designers or engineers that 
can invent solutions for the users. If the users have issues with­
in an area that bothers them just a little, they can create solu­
tions for themselves. These results prove and support the reason 
for conducting workshops where both issues and solutions are 
defined by the users. The majority of the statements are howev­
er either ‘No opinion’ (35%) or ‘Negative experiences’ (25%). 
This shows that there are several different issues and problem 
statements that can be taken upon and worked further on.
Figure 30: Distribution of quotes 
from exploratory interview
Exploratory interviews
==PAGE:66==
62
9.4	  CONCLUSION TO SECTION 9
The last part of the research space was focused on understanding the topic of diabetes from first-hand 
sources and the users who live the diagnosis. Four users were recruited for a rather unstructured in­
terview, where two of them currently measure their blood glucose on a daily basis and two of them 
do not.
The main outcome from this section and interview is following:
•
The three major themes that all four interviewees have the most insights about are knowledge, 
health professionals and measuring blood glucose. The majority of the insights did however 
have a correlation to the other 8 defined themes, which makes all insights interesting and valu­
able for the initial research of the project boundary.
•
Most knowledge that the interviewees have about their disease, about their lifestyle and about 
their blood glucose they have gained themselves through different stakeholders besides health 
professionals. The interviewees are satisfied with this knowledge, however, the major feeling is 
that health professionals must guide their patients more, must enlighten about all possibilities 
and must describe the consequences of non-treated diabetes.
•
All interviewees have unfortunately some negative experiences with their doctor regarding 
their diagnosis. These experiences contribute to low motivation for treating diabetes.
Figure 31: Quotes from exploratory interview w/o themes (can also bee seen in Appendix, p.8)
Exploratory interviews
==PAGE:67==
63
•
The interviewees have not received any information or recommendations from their doctor re­
garding blood glucose measurement. Some do provide treatment plans, however, most of them 
are useless and does not help in decreasing blood glucose levels.
•
The approach for treating diabetes is different between health professionals. Diagnosis is also 
made by chance, and not because the patient visited the doctor suspecting diabetes, or the doc­
tor summon the patient for a diabetic observation.
•
Interviewees who measure their blood glucose have a great experience with the process and feel 
highly motivated to continue, due to an exciting process, the positive progression and higher 
knowledge of affecting factors on blood glucose.
•
Interviewees who do not measure their blood glucose are either against a finger-prick blood test, 
scared to become addictive or anxious about developing scar tissues. Furthermore, measuring 
blood glucose might interrupt daily life, work and chores, which may annoy the individual and 
their environment. 
•
All interviewees desire Continuous Glucose Monitors (CGM) to track their blood glucose lev­
els at all times and make better lifestyle choices.
•
All interviewees had some ideas, solutions or thoughts of how some of the issues could be re­
solved or done better. These results demonstrate that it is not only designers or engineers that 
can invent solutions for the users. These results furthermore prove and support the reason for 
conducting workshops where both issues and solutions are defined by the users.
9.5	 SECOND SCOPING
Exploratory interviews end the second diamond of the research space. Together with the previous 
section 8, the focus in this space was exploring the topic and the problem more in detail, including the 
stakeholders around persons with type 2 diabetes, their relation to each other, and their experiences 
regarding blood glucose measurement. 
Through “Know your blood glucose” questionnaire and the exploratory interviews we observed that 
people with type 2 diabetes who are not insulin-treated have different viewpoints regarding meas­
uring their blood glucose themselves. Persons who do not measure their blood glucose are often not 
aware of this opportunity and do not have any opinions regarding the current experience. On the other 
side, people who do measure their blood glucose, have several stories and insights that wish to share 
and find better solutions for. 
The need of these two user groups is different regarding blood glucose measurement, as one group 
desire better solutions for better experiences while another group does not need within this topic as

At this stage of the design process. we wish explore personal 
experiences in more detail, discover additional ones and clar­
ify questions that arose throughout the project. We also wish 
to establish a closer connection with the users and hear their 
wishes and desires concerning future solutions. 
Ín this chapter you will read about the co-creation process of 
this project, about the planning and facilitating workshops,

==PAGE:70==
66
In the research space of the design process, detailed analysis and exploratory interviews were con­
ducted to gain insights into the daily lives of people with type 2 diabetes. Different results were iden­
tified, including both positive and negative personal experiences (section 9), areas with unanswered 
questions (section 8.3 and 6.5) and interesting relationships between stakeholders (section 8.2). To 
explore in detail the personal experiences, discover additional ones and clarify questions that arise 
throughout the project, a closer connection with the users must be established.
The users experience several issues in their daily life with the diagnosis that irritate them, take up a 
lot of space in their thoughts and for which they want better solutions. As it was discovered in the 
exploratory interviews, beyond the pains and the stories, the users have a great amount of ideas and 
thoughts to how their issues can be resolved. This shows that besides establishing a closer connection 
with the users, the users must be invited to the design process and be part of creating solutions that 
they can benefit of themselves. 
Co-creation as a design process discussed in section 7 becomes useful at this stage, as this is where 
knowledge and new ideas are developed in collaboration between all relevant stakeholders. The users 
are invited to the development process with the role ‘experts of own experiences’ and use it to share 
insights to define challenging problem statements and generate ideas for better solutions (B.-N. Sand­
ers E., Stappers P. J., 2008). Experts can join the co-creation process if they are affected by the prob­
lem space or the potential solution, to likewise share their insights and knowledge about the topic. 
Design workshop is a qualitative meth­
od used within the co-creation process (5 
Keys to Successful Co-Creation), where 
the design and research team physical­
ly invites all relevant stakeholders to go 
through a series of exercises to achieve a 
specific goal (Chang, n.d.). While the us­
ers and experts join the workshops as par­
ticipants, the researcher and the designer 
mainly act as the facilitators that support 
the users by providing creative tools for 
effective expression and ideation. Design­
ers may be part of the workshop in the 
ideation phase to support the discussions 
with expert knowledge from their area of 
expertise (B.-N. Sanders E., Stappers P. J., 
2008). The different roles of a workshop 
can be seen on Figure 33.
The goal of this thesis is first of all to obtain knowledge and generate new ideas with help from the 
users. We wish to develop a solution were the users have been on the development journey from the 
beginning and have had an input to every designed featured. With this co-creative process and de­
sign workshops, we additionally wish to discuss whether or not this process was effective, and if the 
outcome was successful compared to previous studies (section 5 and 6). Therefore, workshops have 
been planned to support thesis goals and to learn first-hand from the users.

10.1	 STRUCTURING AND PLANNING WORKSHOPS
For the workshops to become successful with the needed insights uncovered and ideas developed, a 
great amount of time and thoughts must be put into structuring and planning the workshops. This 
includes planning the whole workshop process, if several workshops are to be conducted, as well as 
the individual workshops. The general steps of planning a workshops process and individual work­
shops can be seen on Figure 34. Workshop planning has been done in collaboration with Dan Roland 
Persson.
Following sections describe the planning procedure of workshops conducted in this project.
10.1.1	Workshop goals and structure of whole process
The general goal of using design workshops was described in the introduction to this chapter (page 
66). The following description defines the desired goal and outcome of the workshops which will 
be kept in mind during the planning and in later discussions. Workshops are conducted to facilitate a 
co-creation process between the designers and people with type 2 diabetes within the scope.
To achieve the goal and outcome of the workshops, it has been decided that three workshops would 
be a suitable amount of workshops to conduct within the time frame of the project. The users will not 
be overloaded with a high amount of workshops and at the same time they will have sufficient time 
to express their thoughts and wishes.
The structure of the three workshops follow the ‘Quadruple’ Diamond process discussed in section 
7. The general goals of the three workshops is to discover and define the problem space followed by 
developing and delivering final concepts in the solution space. As it was planned to conduct only

The three workshops define the workshop space of this project. On Figure 35 the workshop space 
is seen in relation to the research space, conducted prior to workshop 1, and the validation space at 
the end of the project. All the results from the research space, including preliminary research, design 
space analysis and interviews, are served as a starting point for discussion in workshop 1. Between 
all workshops, time is set aside to process the outcome from workshops and prepare for the next one. 
Participants in workshop 1 are only users (will be described later) and therefore a meeting with the 
potential designers/experts between workshops 1 and 2 is planned in order for them to be acquainted 
with the project, the general co-creation process, the outcome from workshop 1 and the plan for the 
following workshops. In this way, they will be ready to participate from workshop 2 and forward. In 
the end, the results from workshop 3 form a base for validation space, where concepts are developed 
into prototypes and tested with the users (further description will follow).
10.1.2	Participants
In all three workshops, persons with type 2 diabetes within the scope are the main participants (sec­
tion 9.5). A requirement was set for the participants which was following: 1) have type 2 diabetes, 2) 
not be insulin-treated and 3) have experience with blood glucose measurement. The same users are 
allowed to participate in multiple workshops, as well as new users are welcome to join the following 
workshops.
As the goal of workshop 1 is to explore problems and insights persons with type 2 diabetes explore, 
these users are the only participants in the first workshop. The recruitment process for these users 
was highly supported by Danish Diabetes Association and CACHET, who helped with promoting the

In workshops 2 and 3 a problem scoping is made after which an ideation process begins. For these 
activities, designers with some expert knowledge within the topic must participate to support the 
discussions, the decisions and the development process. The requirement for the designer is therefore 
to have some experience or knowledge within type 2 diabetes and blood glucose measurement, as 
well as have skills within creativity and concept development. Anne-Marie Karin Wegener and Lene 
Kølle Jørgensen from Danish Diabetes Association have been invited to participate in the workshop 
together with Lotte Klim from Studies & Me and researchers from DTU that are part of this project 
(Dan Roland Persson and Per Bækgaard). The author of this thesis act as a designer as well when not 
facilitating the workshops (Figure 38).
The number of participants to recruit for each workshop is set between 9 - 12. This is the most optimal 
and desired number to achieve as it will therefore be possible to create 3 - 4 groups of 3 participants 
in each. 3 participants in each group allows each individual to be open about his/her experiences and 
share them with not too many. Insights, knowledge and ideas from 3 - 4 groups is a great amount to 
process and use for further work. In general, 9 - 12 participants is a big enough group of people to 
explore different areas of the topic and is small enough for everyone to actively participate. 
10.1.3	Location and meeting time
Most users are familiar with Danish Diabetes Association and their values. As Danish Diabetes As­
sociation is part of this project and the workshops, the first workshop was decided to run at the main 
building of Danish Diabetes Association in Glostrup. In this way the user may be more open-minded 
and acceptant in regards to joining these sessions. Furthermore, given that Danish Diabetes Associ­
ation have history in discussing personal issues and experiences with the users, their working space 
act ideally for the goal and outcome of workshop 1. Here the participants can feel that they are heard 
Figure 38: Designers for workshop 2 and 3
Workshop space
==PAGE:75==
71
and taken seriously.
In second and third workshop the participants are set to explore the solution space which require 
some creative thinking. It has been decided to run both of these workshops in the innovation hub, 
DTU Skylab, at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU), as this hub provides facilities and ma­
terials for effective innovation, that can be used for facilitating the workshop and intensifying the 
creativity of the participants (Figure 39).
All three workshops were set to run on a weekday as an assumption was made that the participants 
have plans on the weekends. Meeting time depends on the length of the workshops and must be set 
to end with a possibility for dinner. 
10.1.4	Preparation and facilitation of individual workshops
Each workshop is structured, prepared and facilitated identically. Exercises and activities are planned, 
materials are created and gathered together, participants are recruited and the workshops are carried 
out. 
Prior to each workshop exercises and activities are planned to follow an overall structure consisting 
of three phases: Create comfort, stage discussion and develop together (Valgeirsdóttir, Workshop 101 
- Making of, 2020). In create comfort phase an ice-breaker activity is always planned to introduce the 
participants to each other and to let them become more relaxed and comfortable with the workshop 
setting. Ice-breakers help to ensure that participants engage with each other and with the given mate­
rials to the fullest, as well as they break down the personal barriers that might occur in unknown en­
vironments (Heathfield, 2020). In stage discussion and develop together phases the planned activities 
vary depending on the desired outcome and the execution format. 
When the structure and content of a workshop are planned, materials for the facilitation are created 
and gathered together. This includes templates for the specific activities, boundary objects for dis­
cussions, presentation slides, post-it notes, writing tools and other objects that might be necessary 
to support a specific activity execution. Templates are made and used to collect insights in writing, 
as important quotes otherwise can be lost if they are only discussed orally. Furthermore, templates 
are made to drive the discussion in a direction that is needed to achieve the goals of the workshops. 
Figure 39: Location of workshop 1 - 3
Workshop space
==PAGE:76==
72
Boundary objects act the same way as tem­
plates and are used to facilitate a process where 
individuals can together transform their knowl­
edge into tangible insights that are aligned with 
the objectives of the workshops (Gomes de 
Lima T., Broberg O., de Assis Esteves F., 2021). 
Presentation slides are created to support the 
facilitation and guide the participants through 
the activities. Each presentation slide includes 
the purpose of the project and workshop, the 
agenda of the day with time frames, and clear 
descriptions of the exercises together with indi­
vidual goals.
The workshops are led by a facilitator, which in this case was either the author of this thesis or the 
co-planner of the workshops (Dan Roland Persson). The key role of the facilitator, besides planning 
and preparing the workshops, is to help and guide the participants through the exercises and man­
age the time and the materials used throughout the workshop (Ewa Wróbel A., Cash P., Lomberg C., 
2020). In order to preserve the discussions between the  participants, a documenter was invited to the 
workshops to take notes of insight that the templates do not capture. This is done instead of recording 
the discussions which may cause discomfort between the participants.
Throughout the workshops, a couple of breaks are included to avoid so-called ‘decision fatiques’, 
where the participants over time can become mentally exhausted from discussing the same topic or 
making constant decisions (Why do we make worse decisions at the end of the day, n.d.). Breaks also 
restore motivation to continue as well as increases productivity and creativity (Saye, 2021).
Each workshop ends with a feedback session, where the participants must answer a couple of ques­
tions regarding the workshop facilitation and the planned/executed activities. This feedback is used 
to improve the next workshops and to discuss the current workshop.
In the next sections, the different activities planned for each workshop are described, including goals, 
preparation process, and time frame. After each workshop description, the results are presented and 
reflected upon.
Workshop space

can frame the experience that fits them best. Furthermore, each card has received one or two themes 
based on their content: blood sugar and measurement, food and diet, health professional, exercise, 
knowledge, social and personal thoughts. These will mainly be used under the discussion of the 
workshop outcome where it will be possible to view the majority themes and their relation to other 
cards. All Experience Cards can be seen in Appendix, p. 17. 
The activity is divided into three parts. Firstly, the participants must as a group go through all the 
experience cards and discuss their content. Based on their discussion they must choose whether the 
experience card is applicable to them or not (Figure 40). The second part of the exercise focus on 
creating new experience cards, based on own stories, insights or knowledge. The participants receive 
blank cards, and must together as a group again brainstorm upon experiences that none of the created 
cards touches upon. After a little while, the participants also get some time to individually brainstorm 
upon their own experiences that have not been mentioned in group brainstorming. It was chosen to 
start with the group brainstorming so that participants can use each other’s experiences to initiate 
their own creativity. It was also chosen to continue with individual brainstorming to make sure that 
all participants are heard and to decrease the risk of some experiences not being shared due to prim­
ing and blocking (Brainstorming, n.d.). The main outcome of this exercise is a group of experiences 
that others besides the users from the interviews have also experienced. 
To continue exploring the problem space and to give a context to some of the previously discussed 
experiences, the participants are set to tell more detailed stories. Using the experience cards for in­
spiration, the participants must think of a scenario or a situation where something or someone has 
bothered, annoyed or saddened them in relation to diabetes. Prior to the storytelling itself, the par­
ticipants must shortly describe themselves (as defining a persona in UX design) and the story setting 
that might include location, time, surroundings, etc. These definitions may help the project in defin­
ing what goals and characteristics represent the need of a larger group of users (Faller, 2019). At the 
end of the exercise, the participants must present their stories to their neighbours and have a small 
discussion about the issues that were experienced in the story. 
The third activity in this workshop is focused on prioritizing the experience cards that were discussed 
Figure 40: Examples of experience cards placed into two piles
Workshop space

10.2.2	Results
10 participants were recruited for workshop 1 and split into 3 groups, which resulted in 10 personal 
stories, 3 timeline maps and 3 templates including a collection of different problems/issues and solu­
tion opportunities. The workshop had high success in gathering a lot of different insights, different 
experiences and different knowledge regarding diabetes and areas within. A large amount of data was 
gathered and processed into the following results.
Based on the categorization of the Experience cards, four themes were highly recurring in all groups, 
when going through the initial exercises: knowledge, health professional, exercise and blood glucose. 
So-called personal thoughts category was also recurring, but on the contrary, it was mentioned as part 
of other themes. Detailed results of the workshops can be seen in Appendix p. 21-41.
Most of the participants agree that it might be hard to find the needed knowledge regarding diabetes, 
that there is a lot of different knowledge one must relate to, and that there is a lack of introduction to 
diabetes as newly diagnosed. “What am I allowed to eat, what consequences are there on my body if 
I exclude something, does it mean anything to  get my pulse up?” These are some of the questions the 
participants do not know where to find answers to. Some of the participants have been diagnosed for 
several years and at the time of their diagnosis, they felt there were no introduction, no information 
and no help to get. Today these participants feel there is too much information and that one can easily 
be confused about where to look and 
what to trust. On Figure 41 this relation 
between knowledge and time interval 
from diagnosis till today can be seen. 
The two arrows illustrate how finding 
knowledge, in general, has become 
less problematic, while finding the 
relevant knowledge has become more 
problematic. Some of the sources that 
the participants use to find answers to 
their questions are Google, Facebook 
groups, different flyers, books and the 
Danish Diabetes Association. Most of 
the participants have additionally stat­
ed that everything they know today, 
they have found themselves using the 
sources mentioned above. 
All participants have some experience with health professionals. The majority feel that all health 
professionals have a different volume of knowledge and that patients are treated and educated differ­
ently. Most of the participants were only given medication to decrease the long-term blood glucose, 
while others were simply told how to control their diet and exercise. The participants in general 
feel that the health professionals do not take diabetes seriously and they do not outline the different 
opportunities a patient can have. This includes both knowledge-wise, medication and equipment for 
blood glucose measurement. One participant even mentioned: “I must always educate my doctor”. 
Figure 41: Workshop 1 - Timeline from group red
Workshop space
==PAGE:81==
77
In relation to diagnosis, most participants could relate to each other, as most of them were diagnosed 
by coincidence. One of them did not even take his sickness seriously, which scares the participants 
that there is not a more formal procedure of diagnosing people with diabetes. Due to self-discovered 
research, the participants know the risks and dangers of high blood glucose levels, and therefore they 
are worried on behalf of other people, who might live with diabetes without knowing it.
Half of the participants perceive themselves as bad at 
exercising. They are not sure of what, when and how 
to exercise as well as how often and how intense. Ad­
ditionally, the participants do not know how to moti­
vate themselves even though they have a high desire 
to become better at exercising. When they do exercise 
they have a hard time continuing with it, as they cannot 
see any difference or progression. The other half of the 
participants perceive themselves as great at exercising 
which they say is mostly because of group sport, where 
they must attend each time or personal goals that they 
must achieve every day/week. 
All 10 participants measure their blood glucose and 
mostly it is due to their knowledge of risks and further 
complications. Most participants are also curious about 
how their blood glucose reacts to different meals and 
exercises. Some find it demotivating to measure their 
blood glucose and change their lifestyle according to it 
if their long-term blood glucose keeps at the same lev­
els. Others find the measuring equipment highly expen­
sive and hard to get hold of, which frustrates them when 
they do wish to measure their blood glucose regularly. 
This includes CGM devices that most of the participants 
have mentioned, highly dream about, and are seriously 
annoyed that they cannot buy them in Denmark.
In Figure 42 different solution opportunities and wishes from the participants are listed and are saved 
for ideation in the next workshop.
10.2.3	Discussion
Workshop 1 provided the project with a large number of valuable insights that made us better under­
stand the users, their daily life with diabetes, and the issues they experience. Some of the questions 
that emerged while analysing the “Know your blood glucose” report by the Danish Diabetes Associ­
ation (section 8.3) were lightly answered, including why some users voluntarily choose to measure 
their blood glucose, and why some choose to ignore the doctor and their recommendations. 
Processing all insights and knowledge gathered from the first workshop, 23 problem statements 
could be defined that cover 5 topics (Table 6). Knowledge, exercise and blood glucose are three of the 
four topics that were highly recurring in all groups. Participants have additionally several experiences 
and issues regarding the health professionals that they wish major changes to. However, health pro­
fessionals are stakeholders that are out of project boundary, due to the limitations of design possibil­
ities (section 8.2) and therefore some of these issues persons with type 2 diabetes experience cannot 
be resolved during the workshops. As some of these issues are however connected to other topics,

such as knowledge or exercise, they can be resolved anyway focusing in another direction. Therefore, 
there is no problem topic called health professionals, however, some of the vital problem statements 
under this theme can be found under other problem topics. Diet and motivation are two topics that 
were not discussed that much, however, there were some experiences and issues that are worth taking 
further to discussions in the next workshops. The 5 topics selected are: knowledge, exercise, blood 
Knowledge
1. There is too much information one must relate to when newly diagnosed.
2. Information about diabetes is difficult to relate to when newly diagnosed and is not relevant for the individual.
3. There is often a lack of scientific evidence (e.g., in the litterature or when there are different views).
4. There is a lack of introduction to diabetes. 
5. It is often necessary to search for information online yourself.. 
6. Information you have found yourself can seem scary / overwhelming.
7. The symptoms or further complications are not known until they appear.
8. Various options and side effects are not discussed with the doctor.
Blood sugar
9. It is difficult to know what causes increases or decreases in blood sugar values.
10. It is difficult to know what effect blood sugar has on one's health and everyday life.
11. It is frustrating and demotivating when long-term blood sugar rises after a great effort.
12. Several types of measuring equipment are difficult to obtain and are expensive. 
13. Your own doctor can often not help with questions about blood sugar or symptoms.
Exercise
14. It is difficult to assess how much and how intensely one should move and exercise.
15. It is difficult to assess when it is best to exercise.
16. It is difficult to start exercising.
17. Motivation is low compared to training in the long run.
 
Diet
18. It can be difficult to choose food to eat when you are not at home.
19.  As newly diagnosed, it can take a lot of energy to change your diet.
20. There is a lack of better / easier apps for meals that include blood sugar measurement.
Motivation
21. It can be difficult to find others with diabetes to talk to, as newly diagnosed.
22.  There is a lack of inspiration in relation to how others with diabetes make everyday life cohesive.
23. It is difficult to find one / other diabetes group(s) with similar situations in the local area (for example to 
discuss problems with or exercise with each other).
Table 6: Problem statements defined in workshop 1
Workshop space
==PAGE:83==
79
glucose, diet and motivation (Table 6).
Comparing the results from workshop 1 with the exploratory interviews many insights and experi­
ences concerning diabetes are the same. In both research methods knowledge, health professional­
sand blood glucose themes were present as the major discussion topics. This clearly shows that the 
four users from the interviews are not the only ones experiencing their issues and problems. With the 
workshop, these areas of diabetes could be defined as the most problematic that several users inde­
pendent of each other can support.
The overall objective of this project is to create a user-friendly experience of measuring blood glu­
cose regularly. The exploratory interviews and the workshops revealed insights and topics beyond 
the measurement of blood glucose, which are also problematic for people with type 2 diabetes. It has 
therefore been decided to open the project theme to some extent and give the users in future work­
shops the possibility of creating solutions to other topics than blood glucose. 
Workshop facilitation: As this workshop was the first of its kind there were no expectations for the 
facilitation. The strategy was to follow the planned activities and the time frame as best as possible, 
to observe the discussions and make modifications on an ongoing basis if needed. The workshop suc­
cessfully initiated discussions and gathered valuable participant insights. This was reflected in high 
participant engagement and positive feedback about the goal and purpose of the workshop as well 
as the executed activities. 10 participants was a good amount for both group and plenum discussions.
However, some improvements can be done for future workshops. The participants mentioned in the 
feedback form that at some points there was a perceived lack of connection between the exercises and 
that the interruption and change between activities were sometimes too sudden. Some activities that 
included concentrated discussions (as experience cards) could use more time, as discussions were 
Workshop space
==PAGE:84==
80
highly interesting and gained several valuable insights. In general, the participants wished a larger 
mixture between the participants throughout the workshop, as they felt at one point they began to 
repeat themselves to the same people. This for example includes the storytelling presentation in pairs, 
which did not make sense as they a moment ago talked about similar stories in groups through the 
experience cards exercise. 
Throughout the facilitation, there have also been made modifications to adjust the plan according to 
the participants’ engagement and discussions. It was seen that the participants did not write any expe­
rience of their own on the blank cards. The participants talked about their own experiences through 
the existing cards and each other’s insights. However many of these were recorded by the document­
er. After the participants presented their timeline map to each other and have discussed the content 
in plenum, the participants did not vote for other experiences. It did not make sense with the number 
of participants in the workshop as they orally agreed to the experience cards through the discussion. 
Time was additionally a limit for this exercise as the first part of the workshops took more took than 
planned.
10.2.4	Conclusion to workshop 1
The goal of the first workshop was: “Explore problems and map each other’s experiences in relation 
to blood glucose and type 2 diabetes”. This workshop was part of the problem space and the discov­
er phase of the third diamond in the ‘Quadruple’ diamond model (section 7). 10 participants were 
recruited for the workshop, which resulted in 10 personal stories, 3 timeline maps and 3 templates 
including a collection of different problems/issues and solution opportunities. The outcome of the 
first workshop is following:
•
Four themes were highly recurring in all groups when going through the experience cards and 
telling stories: knowledge, health professional, exercise and blood glucose.
•
Most of the participants agree that it might be hard to find the needed knowledge regarding 
diabetes, that there is a lot of different knowledge one must relate to, and that there is a lack of 
introduction to diabetes as newly diagnosed. Finding knowledge has in general become less 
problematic while finding the relevant knowledge has become more problematic. 
•
The majority feel that all health professionals have a different volume of knowledge and that 
patients are treated and educated differently. The participants in general feel that the health 
professionals do not take diabetes seriously and they do not outline the different opportunities 
a patient can have.
•
Most of the participants were diagnosed with diabetes by coincidence, which scares them that 
there is not a more formal procedure of diagnosing people with diabetes. Due to self-discovered 
research, the participants know the risks and dangers of high blood glucose levels, and therefore 
they are worried on behalf of other people, who might live with diabetes without knowing it.
•
Half of the participants perceive themselves as bad at exercising. They are not sure of what, 
when and how to exercise as well as how often and how intense. Additionally, they are not sure 
of how to motivate themselves to start and continue exercising. The other half of the partic­
ipants perceive themselves as great at exercising which they say is mostly because of group 
sport and personal goals.
•
Knowledge of risks and further complication is the main driver for participants to measure their 
blood glucose. Additionally, they are curious about how their blood glucose reacts to different 
Workshop space
==PAGE:85==
81
meals and exercises. Some find however measuring equipment highly expensive and hard to get 
hold of which they find highly frustrating in relation to the desire to measuring blood glucose.
10.2.5	For next workshop
All insights and knowledge gathered from the first workshop are processed into, 23 problem state­
ments that cover 5 topics: knowledge, blood glucose, exercise, diet and motivation (Table 6). These 
will be taken for the next workshop which will remind the participants of the results from the first 
workshop and initiate new discussions within the topic. 
The exploratory interviews and the first workshop revealed insights and topics beyond the meas­
urement of blood glucose, which are also problematic for people with type 2 diabetes. For future 
workshops, it was therefore decided to open the project theme to some extent to give the users the 
opportunity of creating solutions to other topics than blood glucose.
For future planning of workshops, the following must be addressed:
•
A better connection between the exercises
•
A more gradual change between activities without no sudden interruptions
•
Give more important activities more time
•
A larger mixture between the participants.

10.3.1	 Methods / procedures
Presentation slides for workshops 2 can 
be seen in Appendix, p.42.
As it was mentioned in the ‘structuring 
and planning the workshops’ section 
(section 10.1.2), the participants of this 
workshop contain both users with type 2 
diabetes and designers. They are both in 
mind when mentioning the participants 
in this and the next workshop. Further­
more, when the groups are created the 
designers will be distributed equally be­
tween the users.
As part of the ice-breaker activity, the 
participants in workshop 2 must think 
of a positive thing they have achieved 
during the past couple of days, months 
or years, and present it to the rest of the 
group. As the participants in this work­
shop will have to make some decisions 
based on their judgement and preferenc­
es, the ice-breaker activity will take ad­
vantage of this to initiate the participants 
thinking and reasoning on a higher level. 
The participants are therefore additional­
ly set to think and present the reason for 
their achievement as well as their moti­
vation to continue.
This workshop encompasses both the 
problem and solution space of the 
‘Quadruple’ Diamond model (section 7), 
which means that the workshop must be 
divided into two parts in order to effec­
tively cover each space. The goal of the 
first part of the workshop is to scope the 
problem space by selecting the most in­
teresting and important problems from 
the first workshop to continue working 
on. 
The problem statements created as part of the outcome in Workshop 1 are converted into so-called 
Problem Cards. An example of these can be seen in Figure 43, while all of them can be seen in Ap­
pendix, p. 48. Each group of participants receives a deck of problem cards, which they must discuss 
the content of and based on that place on a landscape map template (Figure 43). Going through the

cards the participants can. The landscape map template contains several circles that represent the de­
gree of the problem. The problem statements that are most problematic, vital or purely interesting to 
work on are placed in the inner circle. At the outer circle, problem statements that are less important 
are placed. Making group decisions can be a difficult thing to do for some participants, and thereby 
to accommodate disagreements two landscape maps were created: a general map and a personal map. 
If the participants agree about the placement of the problem card, they place it on the general map. If 
they however disagree with the problem degree, they can place it differently on the personal map. An 
extra set of cards is therefore given to each group. 
As the recruitment process is repeated for this workshop, new users can participate in the workshop. 
They might join with additional insights that have not been covered in the first workshop and that can 
be highly valuable for the next phases. Additionally, users that have participated in the first workshop 
might have remembered some issues or experiences after they went home and that they wish to share 
in this workshop. Therefore, each group receives blank problem cards that can be used to define addi­
tional problem statements, if there were to be any. These cards are also part of the Landscape Design 
Game and must thereby also be prioritised using the template.
Based on the discussion and prioritization of the problem cards, each group must select one or two 
problems that they find most interesting or important to work on. The landscape map in the previous 
exercise is a method used to help the participants make the decision, as they already have defined the 
most important problem statements by placing them in the inner circle. It is however not a require­
ment to choose the most central problem statements if they have a good reason or a good discussion 
that can support another decision. The amount of chosen problem statements that will be worked on 
further depends on the number of participants and created groups. Several groups are allowed to work 
on the same problem statement, however, the chance of this happening is low due to a large number 
of different problems.
Before proceeding into the solution space the chosen problem must be described and explored in are­
as that have not been touched upon. This is done by providing the groups with a template containing 
several questions that can be answered and used for inspiration to uncover the whole problem space. 
How is the problem experienced, what is the problem about, why is the problem worth solving and 
what user needs must be fulfilled, are some of the questions asked in the template. Furthermore, the
