10.3.2	Results
4 users and 4 designers were recruited to participate in workshop 2. Unfortunately, this resulted in 
only 2 groups being created, and thereby only 2 problem statements being worked on. However, re­
viewing the results, the workshop was still a success as a lot of new insights were gained and a large 
number of ideas were created. Detailed results of the workshops can be seen in Appendix p. 52-62.
Many of the same experiences and issues from workshop 1 were discussed and mentioned through 
the first exercise of this workshop. This includes knowledge, exercise, trust in health professionals, 
introduction as newly diagnosed, blood glucose measurement, etc. Some new insights were however 
also gained as the designers could contribute to the discussion by asking more detailed questions.
An interesting insight that has not been mentioned before, is how diabetes is different from person to 
person. It is an individual disease, that the participants of this workshop are clearly aware of. They 
are aware that, some things might work for one person (e.g., eating potatoes and exercising a lot), 
while it might be neutral or negative for another person. The participants agree that there are several 
things, as recommendations, treatment plans or products, that cannot be generalized, and that many 
questions people with type 2 diabetes ask are based on their individuality: ‘how is my blood glucose 
affected by this product, how do I motivate myself to continue, what do I prefer and love?’
For one group of participants lack of introduction to diabetes, need of finding own information 
that might be overwhelming and scary, as well lack of discussions about possible opportunities are 
some of the most problematic issues that all the participants could agree upon. Additionally, some 
participants find it very hard to know what effect blood glucose has on their health and daily life, as 
well as where to find other people with diabetes with whom one can share different stories. Beyond 
the created problem cards, the group could agree that information is not uniform among the different 
health professionals, that busyness is a barrier to social events and that there is a lack of medication 
knowledge.
For the other group issues, regarding diet and blood glucose were more problematic. All participants 
agree that the most critical problems are: the amount of energy it can take for the newly diagnosed to 
change his/her diet, as well as the lack of knowledge regarding blood glucose and its effect on health 
and daily life. Additionally, the participants feel it is highly problematic that some persons with type 
2 diabetes have low self-awareness of the diagnosis and the need for medication, and that late diag­
nosis of type 2 diabetes is discovered by a coincidence. Most knowledge cards are also present on the 
general landscape map, however, they are placed more in the outer circle, meaning that these issues 
are not the most critical. Some participants also feel issues regarding exercise is highly problematic, 
including the fact that is it hard to start exercising and keep being motivated to continue. Other par­
ticipants also feel that lack of inspiration concerning life with diabetes is highly important to solve.
The participants chose multiple different problem statements to continue working on. These cover all 
five main topics defined after workshop 1 and 3 additional statements generated through discussion. 
The 15 chosen problem statements can be seen in Figure 44.
The participants had many different ideas about how some of the most critical issues could be re­
solved or minimised. Motivational groups and a mentoring system, where users can be matched 
Workshop space
==PAGE:90==
86
together based on mutual interest and life status, can broaden ones understanding of the disease, 
based on knowledge sharing, as well as encourage and motivate people to exercise, accomplish goals, 
go to social events, etc. Exercising communities where enrollment is more binding can help people 
uphold their habits and goals. Competitions between the users can motivate one to become better 
and achieve higher goals. Platforms that offer recommendations for travels, communities to join, 
routes to walk, can additionally motivate users to accomplish their goals as well inspire them to ac­
tively do something about their disease. Some other ideas include apps that can scan different meals 
and on a basis of this create a forecast or show trends of the blood glucose levels compared to time. 
A test-kit, including guidance and measuring equipment, can be sent to the individual’s home so that 
diabetes can be diagnosed at a more early stage.
10.3.3	Discussion
Workshop 2 gathered different kinds of insights and experiences in relation to diabetes: both some 
that were already discussed in workshop 1, and some additional ones that have not been mentioned 
before. The generated ideas spread from physical solutions, such as apps or measuring equipment, 
to societal solutions, such as mentoring systems, mutual communities, and competitions. All ideas 
from workshop 1 and 2 were gathered together and processed into 9 concepts. These concepts focus 
on solving multiple different problem statements that the participants in workshop 2 found most in­
teresting and important. As mentioned the problem statements cover all five identified topics: 6 from 
knowledge, 2 from exercise, 2 from diet, 2 from motivation, 1 from blood glucose, and 3 additional 
ones that can be categorised under the topic knowledge and blood glucose.
It is interesting how the topic of blood glucose and especially knowledge keeps recurring as one of 
Chosen problem statements
1. There is too much information one must relate to when newly diagnosed.
3. There is often a lack of scientific evidence (e.g., in the litterature or when there are different views).
4. There is a lack of introduction to diabetes.
5. It is often necessary to search for information online yourself.
6. Information you have found yourself can seem scary / overwhelming.
7. The symptoms or further complications are not known until they appear.
12. Several types of measuring equipment are difficult to obtain and are expensive. 
16. It is difficult to start exercising.
17. Motivation is low compared to training in the long run.  
19.  As newly diagnosed, it can take a lot of energy to change your diet.
20. There is a lack of better / easier apps for meals that include blood sugar measurement.
22.  There is a lack of inspiration in relation to how others with diabetes make everyday life cohesive.
23. It is difficult to find one / other diabetes group(s) with similar situations in the local area (for example to discuss 
problems with or exercise with each other).
Extra:
-  Diabetes is too often discovered by coincidence. 
-  It is difficult to find something that works for one.
-  Equipments can have limit functionality.
Figure 44: List of chosen problem statements for ideation - Workshop 2
Workshop space
==PAGE:91==
87
the most critical between the users. Both the users from the interviews and participants from the 
workshops agree that there are great issues within these areas of diabetes and that this is an important 
direction to take if new solutions were to be developed. Problem statements under these topics will be 
taken further to the next workshop. Participants from the workshop additionally find problems under 
the topics of exercise, diet and motivation critical, and thereby these problem statements will also 
be taken further to the next workshop. The project has since the first workshop not been scoped in 
regards to the main topics, however, the participants in this workshop did eliminate 8 of 23 problem 
statements, so therefore the project continues without any additional decisions that need to be made.
An illustration of the 9 concepts can be seen in Figure 45 and a full description of them in Figure 46 
at the end of this section.
Workshop facilitation: Going through the problem cards the participants discussed some of the 
same elements as in workshop 1. 3 of the 4 users participated in the first workshop as well, so a con­
siderable amount of insights were repeated among the participants. As negative as this can sound, the 
exercise was highly effective in gradually engaging the participants into the workshop setting as well 
reminding them of the purpose and topic of the workshop. 
The workshop contained several different ideation methods, including group brainstorming, individ­
ual brainstorming and plenum discussions. The reason for using all three methods was to maximise 
the number of ideas generated as well as provide the opportunity for all participants to engage. This 
had both an advantage and a disadvantage for these types of users. Providing sessions and opportuni­
ties for individual brainstorming, the users who can generate more ideas alone than discussing with 
Figure 45: 9 concepts from Workshop 2
Workshop space
==PAGE:92==
88
others were satisfied. However, most of the participants seemed to like group brainstorming more, 
as they simply skipped the individual part and kept discussing in groups. There might be several 
reasons for that: either the facilitator was not strict and clear enough about the task, the participants 
were framed by the discussion they had in groups, so they individually had no ideas themselves, or 
they simply as personality traits are more creative with others. Either way, this facilitation must be 
rethought for the next workshop if both brainstorming methods is desired.
Throughout the workshop, a significant amount of modifications were made to the plan, including 
both the exercises and the time frame. Exercises such as ‘Problem space’ and ‘Solution sketching 
- other themes’ were completely removed, while ‘The Landscape Design Game’ and ‘Solution explo­
ration’ got much more time. The primary reason for this was due to the ongoing discussions between 
the participants. In both, the problem and solution space all of the participants actively engaged in the 
discussions and made decisions relevant and valuable for the project. Due to the feedback received 
in workshop 1, it was decided to limit the number of interruptions between the participants to a min­
imum so that more detailed and deeper insights/ideas could be captured. 
Another reason for skipping the two exercises is that it made no sense to complete them in relation 
to the ongoing discussions. When it was time to conduct the ‘Problem space’ activity, many of the 
insights that the participants were supposed to explore and define, has already been discussed orally. 
Furthermore, the participants naturally shifted to the solution space and began to create ideas based 
on their chosen problem statement. It made therefore no sense to force the participants into going 
back in the process and make them repeat themselves. As this workshop is about generating many 
different ideas after a problem has been defined, the facilitation of it was rather unstructured and fol­
Workshop space
==PAGE:93==
89
lowed the flow of the participant’s discussions. The ‘Solution exploration - themes’ exercise was also 
removed due to the low amount of groups. While presenting the solutions to each other in plenum, a 
mutual discussion and ideation was formed, which was more effective and natural to end the work­
shop with than making the groups split into two again.
The exercise ‘Selection of problem’ was not conducted as planned, as the users went directly from 
placing the problem cards on the landscape to generating solutions for the most important problem 
statement. Throughout the first exercise, the participants discussed the degree of importance for each 
problem statement and therefore they have already discussed the reasons for choosing the inner circle 
placed cards. The given task might not have been very clear, as the participants did not choose one or 
two problem statements for further work. They generated ideas for several different problems mainly 
in the inner circle but also for problems in the outer circle. A clear scoping of the problem space was 
therefore not conducted and a boundary between define and develop phases in the double diamond 
became blurry. This could have had a negative effect on the project, however, the users naturally talk 
about the most critical problems in their daily life, so even though they did not physically choose a 
problem to continue with, they already discussed the most important and interesting problem state­
ments.
The collaboration between the users and the designers worked relatively good. Both types of partici­
pants provided their knowledge to the discussions which made the results more interesting and valu­
able for the project. The users could come with their insights and issues to the topic as well all wishes 
regarding solutions, while the designers could generate ideas based on the understanding of the topic 
and create a connection between the user’s wishes and actual concepts. One thing that was noticed 
under the facilitation is the distribution of activities between the users and the designers. While it was 
mostly the users that discussed their experiences, the designers wrote different comments down and 
drew the ideas. It was noticed that none of the participants used the writing utensils and it was mostly 
the designers that physically held and went through the problem cards or filled out the templates. It 
seemed that the designers took upon the role of a facilitator or moderator that controlled the process 
and made sure that the discussions were written down and did not deviate from the activity. The 
users on the other hand seemed to take the activities more casually and simply discussed everything 
they knew, also outside of the topic. They were not sitting with the boundary objects (the problem 
cards) or the templates themselves, so they might have lacked an overview of the current exercise 
and the needed discussions for it. Additionally, the users might have thought or seen the designers 
take control over the boundary objects and/or the exercises, so they naturally “handed over” the new 
assignments to the designers and talked about the things they know or feel comfortable with. 
This clearly shows misalignment of the roles in the co-creation process which resulted in several dis­
cussions deviating from the overall topic or assignment. Both types of participants might have been 
unsure of each others roles and how they could contribute to the collaborative process. The partici­
pants naturally took upon their roles from their daily life and completed some of the exercise tasks, 
such as solution sketching, in a way they are familiar with. For future workshops, it must therefore 
be extremely important for the facilitator to explain the purpose of the co-creation process, the par­
ticipant’s different roles and how they must contribute to the more collaborative exercises if this type 
of process is desired by the facilitator. 
In general, this type of collaboration will continue in the next and last workshop, to observe the 
above-mentioned concerns and to analyse the success of co-creation in the last phase of the solution 
space.
Workshop space
==PAGE:94==
90
10.3.4	Conclusion to Workshop 2
The goal of the second workshop was: “Select the most important and interesting problems and start 
creating potential solutions”. This workshop was part of both the problem and solution space cover­
ing the define and develop phases of the third and fourth diamond in the ‘Quadruple’ diamond model 
(section 7). 4 users and 4 designers were recruited for the workshop, which resulted in 2 Landscape 
Maps and 2 sets of chosen problem statements. Many of the same experiences and issues from work­
shop 1 were discussed and mentioned through the first exercise of this workshop. Some new insights 
were however also gained including the following outcome:
•
Diabetes is an individual disease that the participants are clearly aware of. They are aware that, 
some things might work for one while it might be neutral or negative for another person. There 
are several things, as recommendations, treatment plans or products, that cannot be generalized, 
as many people with type 2 diabetes desire to understand their disease based on themselves.
•
Some of the most problematic issues: Lack of introduction to diabetes, need of finding own in­
formation that might be overwhelming and scary, as well as lack of discussions about possible 
opportunities Furthermore, the amount of energy it can take for the newly diagnosed to change 
his/her diet and the lack of knowledge regarding blood glucose and its effect on health and daily 
life are also critical issues. Additionally, some participants feel it is hard to start exercising and 
keep being motivated to continue. Other participants also feel that lack of inspiration in relation 
to life with diabetes is highly important to solve.
•
Based on previous and new discussions the participants chose 15 different problem statements 
to continue working on (Figure 31). Both the participants agree that there are great issues with­
in both knowledge and blood glucose areas of diabetes and that this is an important direction to 
take if new solutions were to be developed. Participants from the workshop additionally find 
problems under the topics of exercise, diet and motivation critical, and thereby these problem 
statements will also be taken further to the next workshop. This is the reason why 15 problem 
statements were chosen for the next part of the workshop and not two (as for two groups).
•
Ideas for resolving some of the most critical issues: Motivational groups and a mentoring sys­
tem, exercising communities, competitions, platforms with recommendations, apps and test-
kits for home use.
10.3.5	For next workshop
All ideas from workshop 1 and 2 were gathered together and processed into 9 concepts (Figure 45 
and Figure 46). These cover problem statements from five identified topics: 6 from knowledge, 2 
from exercise, 2 from diet, 2 from motivation, 1 from blood glucose, and 3 additional ones that can 
be categorised under the topic knowledge and blood glucose. These 9 concepts will be taken for the 
next workshop which will be further scoped and detailed with prototypes. 
For future planning of workshops, the following must be addressed:
•
Planning of the workshops regarding the type of brainstorming sessions to facilitate must be 
given an extra thought to avoid the participants skipping some of the exercises, as an individ­
ual brainstorming session. Thoughts on what may be done differently are reflected upon in the 
discussion (section 10.3.3).
•
A more clear introduction on the purpose of co-creation process, the participants different roles 
and how they must contribute to the more collaborative exercises.

10.4	WORKSHOP 3
10.4.1	 Methods / procedures
Presentation slides for workshops 3 can 
be seen in Appendix, p.63.
As part of the initial ice-breaker, the 
participants in workshop 3 are subjected 
to a specific topic word and are asked to 
write 4 - 5 additional words that come up 
in mind when they see and think of this 
topic. Previous ice-breakers in work­
shops 1 and 2 focused on introducing 
the participants to each other and the 
workshop setting. In this workshop the 
ice-breaker is more topic-related with 
the goal of introducing and reminding 
the participants of the project as well as 
observing the participants thoughts un­
der time pressure. Blood glucose is the 
word the participants are subjected to.
Ideas from workshops 1 and 2 were pro­
cessed into 9 different concepts. In this 
workshop, the goal is to detail the 9 con­
cepts further, choose the most interest­
ing to work on and define a final solution 
with user journeys and prototypes. All 9 
concepts can be in detail seen in Appen­
dix, p. 69.
The participants must have an equal op­
portunity to explore the created 9 con­
cepts and detail them with any additional 
thoughts or ideas. Instead of starting the 
workshop with group brainstorming, as 
in previous workshops, an individual in­
tensive session of brainstorming is fa­
cilitated. The purpose of this is, first of 
all, to engage all participants in the discussion from the very beginning and second of all to examine 
the success of the individual-to-group process and compare it to the opposite group-to-individual 
exercises in previous workshops. In this intensive brainstorming, the participants are divided into 
smaller groups, where each receives a stack of templates corresponding to the 9 concepts. Each tem­
plate includes the name, the description and an illustration of the concept, corresponding problem 
statements and a couple of questions for inspiration. With these questions the participants can think 
about the target group, the use setting (where, when and with whom), functions, content, etc. The 
questions are defined by the planning and facilitation team and are customized for each concept. This 
is done to help the participants in case they get stuck in the ideation process and to maximise the 
outcome of the exercise.
10 min
-

goal of the day
Goal: Welcome the participants.

when you hear the word BLOOD

of words and then present in

the participants.

idea.

from previous workshops and

continue to work on and they think 
addresses the biggest issue

Workshop space
==PAGE:98==
94
Each participant then takes one concept and gets a couple of minutes to study the templates and add 
additional thoughts or ideas to it. The templates are then passed around to the next participant, who 
also gets time to study the concepts and detail them further. As is it the same templates the partic­
ipants use to detail the concepts, comments from previous participants are available for all others. 
These comments are more than welcome to be used for inspiration and questionable thoughts. The 
exercise continues until all participants have had the opportunity to review all concepts and add their 
input to them.
After this exercise, the participants meet in groups and discuss the content of the concepts as well 
as their additional generated ideas. Based on this the groups must choose one concept and corre­
sponding problem statements that they either find interesting or important to discover and develop 
for future use.
In the second part of the workshop, the participants are in groups provided with different tools they 
can use to detail the final concepts. With a user journey template, they must describe how the con­
cept/product will be used, what thoughts will be in mind in relation to this concept and what oppor­
tunities for improvement does the concept have. The participants must relate all these questions to 
the journey of the concept which consist of three phases: before use, during use, and after use. This 
exercise is a simplified version of a customer journey map, which is a method used by designers to 
visually map out a storyline of product interaction and engagement by the user or customer. Outlining 
the process helps gain a better understanding of the customer’s experiences in real-time, and may 
unveil some needs or pains that must be 
addressed or further analysed (Bischoff, 
n.d.). 
Continuing with the task of detailing 
their chosen concept, the participants re­
ceive different materials that they must 
use to build their solutions. They must 
think about how the product shall work 
and how it shall look like. Prototyping 
the concepts may include building a 
physical product using paper, cardboard, 
pencils and tape, or visualising digital 
solutions through small cut pieces of pa­
per, stickers and blank smartphone wire­
frames. In general, prototyping is an ef­
fective method used to produce low key 
representations of a product and to gain 
feedback, ideas and perspectives on the 
built concept. The prototypes can create 
a mutual understanding between the par­
ticipants and can test how the users think 
and feel about the products (Prototyping: 
Learn What Works with Low-Cost/No-
Cost Methods, 2020). 
The participants end the workshop by 
presenting their detailed concepts to­

10.4.2	Results
In workshop 3, 10 participants were in total recruited, including 6 users and 4 designers. With this 
number only 2 groups were created again, which produced in total 2 final concepts. Beyond the 2 
detailed concepts, including user journeys and corresponding prototypes, 7 other concepts were de­
tailed as well with a relatively large amount of additional ideas, thoughts, suggestions and improve­
ments. Detailed results of the workshops can be seen in Appendix p. 81-100.
The first and highly interesting outcome from this 
workshop is the model including all words that came 
up in participants’ minds when thinking of the word 
‘Blood sugar’ (Figure 47). Measurement is the word 
that appeared the most time, followed by diabetes and 
exercise.
The general feedback from the participants about the 
processed concepts was positive. Naturally, already 
through the brainstorming, some concepts became 
more likeable than others. Some concepts were also 
more inviting to detail and define, as it took more 
time for the participants to write their thoughts down 
compared to some other concepts. This for example 
includes ‘Diabetes kit for home testing of diabetes 
and prediabetes’ and ‘Introduction program to diabe­
tes’.  The reason for this is a great question and may 
be based on the insights they have in relation to this 
specific concept, problem statement or topic.

After a round of intense individual brainstorming, instead of further discussing the content of the 
concepts and selection based on group discussions, the participants voted individually for their most 
liked concepts. Two groups were then created based on the two most voted concepts, so people got 
an opportunity to work with the ideas they prefer the most. The two most voted concepts can be seen 
in Figure 48. All participants agreed that concepts 1 and 2 (modular CGM and an app for meals and 
blood glucose) could be combined to consist of two parts: one to measure blood glucose and one to 
collect data and present it.	
Both concepts got a large number of ideas, suggestions and improvements for detailing and future 
work. The participants were good at exploring all phases of the product journey describing the user 
activities, user thoughts and potential opportunities. The participants were also good at creating visual 
prototypes having in mind, how the concept must work and look.
The first concept covers problem statements from blood glucose and diet categories: ‘Several types 
of measuring equipment are difficult to obtain and are expensive’, ‘Equipment can have limit func­
tionality’, ‘As newly diagnosed, it can take a lot of energy to change your diet. ’ and ‘There is a lack

of better / easier apps for meals that include blood glucose measurement. ’.
Concept 1+2: If it were for the participants, the doctors and nurses must inform their patients about 
CGM measuring equipment at their very first consultation. They must also recommend the app for 
collecting data and learning from it. It might not be necessary to have a CGM equipped for a longer 
period of time. 3 months is enough to learn from your blood glucose and change your lifestyle 
accordingly. Therefore the participants agree that patients can subscribe to a CGM equipment for 
a maximum of 3 months, and then deliver it back when they have learned from the collected data. 
Whenever the user feel off again or the long-term blood glucose values begin to increase, the user 
can subscribe to the CGM device again. The device and app can always be acquired without having 
the diagnosis as it can benefit the users to identify their disease or change their lifestyle before it is 
too late or goes in the wrong direction. While using the app, the users can view a graph representing 
blood glucose values together with different meals they have eaten throughout the day or hours they­
have slept. Having an opportunity to add pictures and descriptions make the concept more personal 
and give the blood glucose measurements a context. This context adds freedom and gives motivation 
to the individual, as one can see how their blood glucose reacts to different elements. Besides collect­
ing and representing data, one can also set boundaries for goals and desires as well as export or share 
data with their doctor.
It must be noticed in this concept that the participants did not interpret the CGM as modular (device 
where only needles are changed) but more focused on how to provide current CGM to users (sub­
scription-based). Therefore for future work, CGM is no longer modular but referred to as a service 
delivered from health professionals to patients.
The second concept covers problem statements from the knowledge category: ‘There is a lack of in­
troduction to diabetes’ and ‘There is too much information one must relate to when newly diagnosed. ’.
Concept 9: The way the users must learn about this concept is the same as in concept 1+2. The 
participants agree that at the first consultation with the doctor, the user must receive a brochure 
that describes the main facts about diabetes and includes a link to a website with all other sorts of 
information. Here the user can learn more about the disease and the diagnosis, read about possible 
opportunities, meet a role model or a mentor, connect to other social groups (such as Facebook), or 
chat with an expert from e.g. Danish Diabetes Association. By a different kind of information and 
inspiration, the user might get a better picture of what works best for them as well learn to change 
their lifestyle without concentrating on diabetes too much or compromising on some life values. To 
avoid an overwhelming experience entering this website as a newly diagnosed user, the information 
and knowledge are divided into three subsections depending on one’s status. Newly diagnosed will 
only read material that is relevant for them in the beginning, while a more experienced user will go 
to the section with more detailed and broaden knowledge. 
In Appendix, p. 91-96 prototypes for these concepts can be seen.
10.4.3	Discussion
Workshop 3 was successful in gathering feedback on all 9 concepts created from the outcome in 
workshop 2. The participants actively engaged in adding additional thoughts or suggestions and 
were positive about the different ideas. The two final concepts produced a large number of different 
insights and ideas that all had the potential in being integrated into the final products.
The categories that the chosen concepts cover are knowledge, blood glucose and diet. Having con­
ducted all three workshops it becomes very clear that knowledge and blood glucose are two general 
Workshop space
==PAGE:103==
99
topics within diabetes that the users find most problematic, most interesting and with the most poten­
tial for improvement. 
Within knowledge, the participants have scoped to focus on the initial phases of being diagnosed 
including introduction and available information. This relates to the discussions made under the 
‘Timeline Map’ exercise in Workshop 1, where the participants also set emphasis on the fact that there 
is too much information for a newly diagnosed and that one does not know where to find the relevant 
information. 
Within the blood glucose category, the participants mostly focus on the CGM measuring equipment 
and their unavailability due to costs and government law. This issue was highly discussed and men­
tioned by both the user from the exploratory interviews and by the participants in workshops 1 and 
2. It is therefore not a surprise that this area within diabetes has gone all the way through to the final 
concepts. 
Diet is a category that has not been put a large emphasis on during the first workshop. It has been 
mentioned by a few participants and therefore it was decided to also include problem statements 
within this area for further work in the next workshops. It was first during the ‘Landscape Design 
Game’ that issues within diet became central for one group’s discussions. These issues mostly focus 
on the need for user-friendly solutions that combine blood glucose measurements with meals and 
that can help newly diagnosed patients to change their diet at their own pace. This category slightly 
overlaps the blood glucose category and therefore it made perfect sense to combine these two prob­
lem areas into one concept. 
In general, it is great to see that some of the most discussed issues and experiences in previous in­
terviews and workshops are to be solved by the two chosen concepts. Additionally, even though the 
project after workshop 1 has broadened to include other themes within diabetes than blood glucose, 
it is interesting to see that one of the chosen concepts (concept 1+2) fits within the initial project defi­
nition and goal. It shows that the background for the project is valid and that the participants are in 
need of better and more user-friendly solutions for blood glucose measurements.
Workshop facilitation: Out of the 10 participants, 1 designer and 1 user were present only in the 
first workshop, while 1 other participant joined the workshops for the first time. There has therefore 
been a mixture of knowledge regarding the outcome of the workshops, as some participants knew 
the whole process and the decisions made, while other participants saw the materials for the first 
time. This combination of participants did not create any issues, instead, it was seen that everybody 
contributed equally and listened to each other. Naturally, the one participant that has joined the pro­
cess for the first time, had a large number of experiences and problems she wanted to share with the 
group. Like no one else she had no idea of what has been discussed in previous workshops so she 
contributed with the insights she knows. As the three conducted workshops were interconnected a 
more detailed recap of the previous workshops could have been mentioned as part of the introduction. 
This can remove any misunderstandings and doubts, as well as it can guide the participants into the 
current setting and purpose of the workshop. 
Conducting the individual brainstorming, some concepts were more understandable than others. The 
participants did not ask many questions regarding the content of the concepts, however, confusion 
in participants behaviour could clearly be seen occasionally. There might be different reasons for 
this: either the concepts were not explained enough, the title was misleading, the illustrations and the 
text were not aligned together or the concepts were simply not meaningful. The big discussion when 
planning the workshop was whether or not to include a mutual presentation of all concepts prior to 
Workshop space
==PAGE:104==
100
the individual brainstorming. “Shall the participants get to understand the concepts by themselves or 
shall we create some mutual ground for understanding before brainstorming?”. It was decided not to 
include a concept presentation to avoid priming and to see how the participants understand the con­
cepts. In this way, the participants can frame the concepts as they wish, which is a valuable insight 
for the discussion.
After an individual brainstorming the participants were supposed to discuss the content of the con­
cepts in groups and together select one for further detailing. However, it was in the middle of the 
workshop decided to change the selection process into dot voting. Here each participant received a 
sticker and could individually vote for any concepts. This is done due to the limited time, as the indi­
vidual brainstorming took longer than planned, and to try a new decision-making method. Dot-voting 
is in general a method used to allow all participants to make a quick and easy decision and to narrow 
down the alternatives (How dot voting helps with group decision-making, n.d.). Reflecting upon this 
change of plan, the participants seemed to be positive about voting individually. Everybody got to be 
democratically part of the selection process and it was conducted quickly. It seemed the participants 
after the brainstorming session could not wait to detail the concepts further so instead of forcing them 
into a group discussion they were let to quickly continue with the ideation part. Additionally, after the 
voting session, the participants were allowed to form the groups themselves based on what concept 
they individually prefered to work on. This made sure that the participants detailed the concepts with 
interest and that the groups did not work on the same concepts. Furthermore, based on the desired 
feedback from workshop 1, this allowed a higher degree of mixture between the different participants. 
Compared to the discussion in workshop 2, the roles between the participants in this workshop were 
Workshop space
==PAGE:105==
101
almost the same. Both the users and the designers were active in the discussions, and it felt that all 
participants were heard. However, the designers were still the ones who created the visual prototypes 
and took notes of the discussed ideas. The designers asked questions of how the users wanted the 
design of the concepts, which sometimes made the discussion more one way (as an interview) rather 
than both ways (as in co-creation). Looking at the overall facilitation of the workshop and the ex­
ercises, it seemed that the users were positive about collaborating with the designers. An additional 
reason for conducting the individual brainstorming was also to make sure that all users got to expe­
rience the role of a designer, which they are not really experiencing when they are in a group with 
actual designers.
Conducting individual brainstorming before a group exercise worked considerably good, as all par­
ticipants seemed to be actively engaged in the exercise and no one felt uncomfortable or unwanted. 
Compared to the previous workshops, that started with group sessions, it seemed in this workshop 
that more participants were engaged in the following group discussions. The participants warmed 
up their creativity individually and were then ready to contribute in a group. In other workshops, it 
seemed that some of the participants were more active than others and took more responsibility for 
the decisions made.
The first and the last workshop might not be a good idea to compare due to the type of participants. 
First of all, in the first workshop designers were not part of the participants and were therefore not 
there to control the process as they did in the second and last workshop. Second of all, the users that 
joined the first workshops experienced the setting and the facilitation for the first time and therefore 
might not have been absolutely comfortable with the group discussions. In the last workshop, most 
of the users are joined for the second or third time and therefore are more experienced in relation to 
the co-creation process and collaborative discussions. 
For future workshops is it therefore a good idea to know your participants before planning different 
exercises. Starting with either individual brainstorming or group brainstorming depends on the type 
of participants and how familiar they are with workshops and co-creation.
Unfortunately, not a larger amount of concepts were detailed further through user journeys and proto­
types. However as all remaining 6 concepts have high potential in being further developed, the con­
cepts are available for future work or other projects. Additionally, two groups detailing two concepts 
made the next prototyping and validation phase more straightforward, as the two concepts could be 
split between this thesis and the co-planners project.
10.4.4	Conclusion to Workshop 3
The goal of the third and last workshop was: “Select the most interesting concepts and build upon 
them together”. This workshop was part of the solution space covering the deliver phases of fourth 
diamond in the ‘Quadruple’ diamond model (section 7). 10 participants were in total recruited, in­
cluding 6 users and 4 designers. This resulted in 9 detailed concepts and 2 final solutions with user 
journeys and prototypes. The outcome of the first workshop is the following:
•
The 9 concepts were detailed through intense individual brainstorming. The participants active­
ly engaged in adding additional thoughts or suggestions and were positive about the different 
ideas.
•
The two most voted concepts: 1) Modular continuous blood glucose sensor w. app + meal app 
with a focus on blood glucose, and 2) Introduction program for diabetes and blood glucose 
measurement.

10.4.5	For next phase
The two generated and detailed concepts with their user journeys and prototypes will both be taken 
for future testing and validation. They will be split between this thesis and the co-planners project 
(more detailed description in the next section).
10.4.6	For future workshops
In this project, there are no more workshops to plan and facilitate. However, from this workshop, 
there are additional aspects that may be considered when planning workshops in the future. 
•
Add a more detailed recap of the previous workshops as part of the introduction.
•
Make sure the participants understand the content of the exercises if the purpose of these exer­
cises is to gain a high amount of different knowledge from the users.

10.5	WORKSHOP DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION
The goal of conducting a co-creation process with three workshops was to explore first-hand personal 
experiences and issues of living with type 2 diabetes (section 10). Several questions arose through 
research and exploratory interviews that the planning team behind workshops were ambitious to 
uncover and clarify (sections 6 and 9). Furthermore, it was observed in the initial research that many 
persons with type 2 diabetes have several ideas, thoughts and wishes of how some of their problems 
may be resolved (section 9). Thereby, inviting them to the development process and letting them con­
tribute with the problem definition and solution creation on an equal level with designers and experts, 
final concepts may become more usable, useful and meaningful, as well as create emotional impact. 
In other words, increase user experience for people with type 2 diabetes (section 7). 
Looking back on the following goal of the workshop the planned activities and the participated users 
were great in supporting and achieving the goal. 
In workshop 1, problems related to life with type 2 diabetes were explored, identified and discov­
ered. Beyond the area of blood glucose and controlling its levels, issues and experiences regarding 
knowledge, health professionals and exercise were additionally discovered. The first workshop there­
by gave an overall understanding of diabetes and how it is to live with today. From this workshop, 
the planning team thereby created 23 problem statements covering the most discussed issues by the 
participants. 
In workshop 2, the 23 problem statements were further discussed and additional insights were gath­
ered due to the participation of experts. 13 of these 23 problems were chosen as the most critical, 
important and interesting to solve while 3 additional ones were also defined as important. The partic­
ipants had several different ideas of how some of these problem statements could be solved, which 
resulted in 9 full concepts. 
In workshop 3, the 9 concepts were reviewed and detailed through intense individual brainstorming 
after which a voting session determined the 2 most interesting and important concepts for further 
work. These 2 concepts were further detailed through discussions, user journeys and low-fidelity 
prototypes.
The two concepts that defined the main outcome of the three workshops are now taken further for the 
next phase: creating a higher fidelity prototype and testing it with potential users. The two concepts 
are split between this thesis and the co-planners project (Dan Roland Persson PhD. paper’).
10.5.1	Reflection over the workshop planning and facilitation
Before proceeding with the decisions regarding further work, the workshop planning and facilitation 
is reflected upon. During structuring and planning the workshops, both the overall structure and the 
individual workshops, several steps were managed and even more, decisions were made. How many 
workshops to conduct? Which types of participants and how many to invite? How shall the work­
Goal for all workshops: 
Discover and identify problems related to life with type 2 diabetes and 
the process of controlling blood glucose levels. Discuss reasons behind 
the different problems/experiences and create concepts based on the de­
termined user needs/pains and different ideas/thoughts. 
Workshop space
==PAGE:108==
104
shops be connected? And where shall the workshops be facilitated? These are some of the questions 
that has been made a decision upon and which can, after the whole process, be reflected upon for 
success. 
Number of workshops
One can always discuss whether three workshops were enough to produce valuable insights and con­
cepts. In some workshops, it was discussed that the time frame was too tight and that a high amount 
of interruptions caused the participants not to finish or skip some of the exercises. In these workshops, 
the outcome was still successful in gathering a large amount of knowledge and ideas for future con­
cepts, even though some exercises were not fully completed or were skipped. Thereby, in this process, 
the strict time frame did not cause major problems for facilitation and outcome. This can additionally 
always be argued that the facilitator was less strict in the last workshops than in the first, which made 
the flow between exercises more natural. 
However, in future workshops, the time frame and the change between exercises must be more re­
alistic than optimistic. Unless the exercise is meant for intense content development where the par­
ticipants must generate ideas within a short period of time, have rather more time set aside for the 
exercise than too little. The participants can always discuss more if there is time left or have an 
additional break. 
Limitation: Furthermore, the time frame for conducting all workshops was highly limited due to 
the time of this project. The workshops were conducted in November which was the boundary for 
conducting these workshops. Adding additional workshops, giving more time between workshops or 
pushing them further, would have resulted in a more strict timeframe of this project and more limited 
resources for validation space. 
Interconnection between workshop
Additionally to the number of workshops, several thoughts were given on how the workshops must 
interconnect. As the general outcome of the whole process is divided into three workshops, the out­
come must somehow continue from one workshop to another in a most meaningful way. Experience 
Cards, Problem Cards and concept templates were used as boundary objects to remind the partici­
pants of previous discussions and decisions as well as initiate new discussions. This method seemed 
to work great as the participants were engaged in the content of the boundary objects and initiated 
interesting discussions based on these. There was less awkwardness between the participants as the 
boundary objects created some sort of comfort (the same as a board game within a new group of 
friends). Some more interconnection could have been facilitated by starting the workshop with a 
recap of previous workshops. 
Amount of participants
In total 12 different users participated in the workshops. The first workshop consisted of 10 partici­
pants, while only 4 and 6 users participated in workshops 2 and 3 respectively. The number of partic­
ipants in these workshops could have been larger, as in both workshops only two groups were created 
and thereby only 2 sets of outcomes (templates, decisions, etc.). 
Limitation: Recruitment of participants was additionally a high limitation for this project, due to time 
and resources. Between workshops there were only 2 weeks available for processing results, plan­
ning new workshops, creating boundary objects and recruiting new participants. Due to the number 
of tasks needed to be done, time spent on recruiting participants was limited. This also meant that the 
full potential of finding valuable users was not utilized. Beyond emails and Facebook post-its, more 
direct contact with valuable stakeholders could have been conducted. Furthermore, several rounds of 
Workshop space
==PAGE:109==
105
recruitment could have been facilitated in case some valuable users did not respond the first time. For 
future workshops, it can also be discussed whether the workshops can be facilitated online. Several 
participants revealed their desire to participate in the workshops if they were not facilitated physi­
cally in Copenhagen. A lot of people in Denmark are starting to get used to online courses, lectures, 
meetings, etc. due to the COVID-19 pandemic. With the current technology (Zoom, Skype, Micro­
soft Teams)  workshops could therefore easily be set up to engage users within the whole country. 
This is however a different discussion as the facilitation of an online workshop can result in a com­
pletely different outcome.
Participant experience
Joining the workshops required the participants to have type 2 diabetes and not be insulin-dependent. 
Furthermore, the users had to have some sort of experience measuring blood glucose to explore is­
sues and solutions regarding this process. This requirement for the participants seemed to work great 
as they each has some insights they could share regarding this process. However, it can not be con­
cluded whether or not this requirement was successful for the workshops as we do not have anything 
to compare this method with. In the future, workshops including persons with no experience can be 
conducted to observe and discuss the revealed results in relation to this project. 
Roles between the participants
This topic has been discussed a couple of times throughout the workshop sections. The major reflec­
tion from this discussion is whether the difference between the designers and the users are a good or a 
bad thing for the workshop. As mentioned, the workshops did create valuable and interesting insights 
for the project, thereby the collaboration between the designers and users must have been great. How­
ever, it is unknown exactly how the results have been formed and whether a different combination of 
roles would have changed the result completely. For future work, it is thereby interesting to research 
more about the topic of ‘roles within a co-creation workshop’ as well as to conduct additional work­
shops with a different balance of participants and different exercises. 
In general, the workshops were welcomed and created positive energy between the participants. For 
the design team, a high amount of insights were gathered and ideas developed. Many of the questions 
that were set in sections 6 and 8 were answered and clarified, including: 1) what people think about 
the different offers and products that are provided for treatment of diabetes, 2) how do people manage 
the many different offers and products, as well as how do they choose which to purchase or accept, 
3) why do people measure blood glucose in general and how did they start? and 4) how do they treat 
their diabetes today?
Not only did the design team gain positive results. Some participants gave additional feedback stating 
that the workshops also helped them in gaining new knowledge and feel more positive about their 
disease. Some of the quotes can be seen in Figure 50.
Workshop space
==PAGE:110==
106
10.5.2	Chosen concept for further work
This project takes upon one of the concepts created in the three workshops for the last space in 
‘Quadruple’ Diamond, validation space (section 7). The concept that is chosen to proceed with is a 
combination of a CGM device on subscription and a correlated app for blood glucose measurements, 
meals, exercises, etc. 
The concept consists of both a service (CGM device on subscription) and a digital product (an app). 
The creation, building and testing part of these products are different from each other as it requires 
different materials and methods to design and develop these solutions. Due to the time limit of the 
project, we choose to focus on one part of the solution.
The app can function with any measuring blood glucose device. As long as the user can measure the 
glucose values that are entered into the app (either manually or automatically), the value of the con­
cept is not decreased. Furthermore, in section 6 it was discussed whether or not the users will create 
an additional mHealth app if they got an opportunity to be involved in the design and development 
process themselves. The question was following: “What if users are part of creating solutions them­
selves, will they design them in a way that they would find useful and satisfying over the long-term?” 
Will the users be captured for a more long-term period after designing the product themselves com­
pared to traditionally designed apps? And will the app be more useful for a successful self-manage­
ment of diabetes? In this project, we have an opportunity to explore the potential of a mHealth app 
created through a co-creation process. It has therefore been decided to continue only working and 
testing the app, with an assumption that the users have a device for measuring blood glucose levels, 
either a BGM or a CGM. 
Figure 50: Feedback from the users on the workshops
Workshop space
==PAGE:111==
107
VALIDATION
SPACE
The final stage of this design process focuses on creating a 
prototype and validating it with potential users. In this chap­
ter, you will be led through the decisions made concerning 
the concept and its value for the users. You will be present­
ed with the current version of the solution through a click-
through prototype. Thereafter, you will be able to follow the 
process of validation and the correlated results. 
==PAGE:112==
108
Through the three workshops, users and designers collaborated to discover and define insights and 
issues related to diabetes and blood glucose, as well as developed and selected concepts that could 
solve the most important and interesting problems. In the last phase of the Double Diamond process, 
a final design of a concept must be delivered through prototyping and testing. 
The concept that was chosen to continue within this project was an app for blood glucose measure­
ments with other different diabetes-related parameters, such as meals, sleep, activity, etc.
The participants from the workshops have created this concept themselves through several different 
exercises. One might therefore think that the concept is already the best compared to competitors and 
is the most user-friendly. However, the participants have not used or interacted with the real product 
yet. They have not seen the UX of the product, they have not tried using the product in a context, 
and they have not felt how it is to live with the product. We can first conclude whether or not a user 
will use the product after we have created an interactive prototype and tested it for satisfaction and 
efficiency. “The closer you can get to showing people a real product, the more accurately you can 
predict whether people will use that product” (Klein, 2013). 
The goal of a prototype is to evaluate the flow of the design solution and gather feedback on it before 
committing expensive resources to build the real product. Through prototypes, the design team can 
fail faster, learn sooner and succeed earlier (Hartson R., Pyla P., 2019). This means that by providing 
an early-stage model or representation to the users, failures can quickly be found and fixed in no time. 
Prototypes also create a baseline for communication between the users and the designers as well as 
help the designers to observe the user performance and consequences of the design decisions (Hart­
son R., Pyla P., 2019). 
In workshop 3 the participants created very low-fidelity prototypes which were mainly used to create 
the first draft and establish a first impression of the intended design. In order to test the design with the 
users again, the prototypes can be created into medium-fidelity prototypes. Here the different wire­
frames are connected together with links and active buttons allowing the user to sequence through 
the design by clicking around the prototype. This so-called click-through prototype demonstrates the 
interaction flow by simulating the navigation through the wireframes (Hartson R., Pyla P., 2019). 
As the last part of this project and last phase of the Double Diamond process, a medium-fidelity click-
through prototype is created in Adobe XD and tested with users from the workshops. 
11.1	PROTOTYPE CREATION
In order to create a prototype for testing, we must first define: what are solving, who are we solving 
for and how do we solve the problem. These three steps will mainly be defined through a Lean Can­
vas and a supporting User Story Map.
11.1.1	Lean Canvas
An initial vision for the concept must be written down. Instead of creating a multiple-page business 
plan, a one-page business model diagram, Lean Canvas, can be used to quickly and concisely outline 
“A prototype is a primitive representation or version of a product that a design team or front-end-de­
velopment team typically creates during the design process” (Smith, 2019). 
VALIDATION SPACE
Validation space
==PAGE:113==
109
the business model of the concept without spending several months explaining the essence of the 
product in detail. Furthermore, the model is highly portable so it is easy to share with any potential 
stakeholders and modify in any time. The Lean Canvas is adapted from Alex Osterwalders’ Business 
Model Canvas and is optimized to describe product start-ups. This model is created by deconstruct­
ing the business model of the concept into nine subparts: problem, customer segment, unique value 
proposition (UVP), solution, key metrics, unfair advantage, channels, cost structure, and revenue 
streams (Maurya, 2012).
In this project, we wish to create a Lean Canvas business model to outline the concept and its value 
for the customer segment in relation to the problem. The four first subparts of the model will therefore 
mostly be focused on through the following descriptions. 
Problem
A newly diagnosed patient must relate to a lot of different information and take several actions in 
a short period of time. Different doctors, nurses and other health professionals have their own way 
of providing information and treating diabetes. Some give their patients medication, some provide 
diet and exercise plans, some inspire to contact local groups, while others do nothing. In either case 
the patient must on his/her own initiative start acting upon the disease, by reaching out to the right 
people, getting in hold of the needed products, and/or changing lifestyle habits in relation to diet and 
exercise. The patients must learn what it is to live with diabetes and what one can do to decrease the 
risk of further complications. Changing one’s mindset and daily life within a short time can be highly 
stressful and full of anxiety, which is a demotivating factor in taking active responsibility. 
Figure 51: Lean Canvas model for concept 1 + 2 
Validation space
==PAGE:114==
110
Furthermore, users feel that current recommendations and solutions created to treat diabetes, are 
too generic and that they must be adjusted to accommodate the individual and their lifestyle. Some 
people cannot endure medications, some have hard times motivating themselves to exercise, others 
have difficulties in concentrating on diabetes while they have other more serious diseases in parallel. 
Currently, there are a low amount of solutions that focus on individuals’ preferences, habits, and life 
situations.
According to the users, there are currently no user-friendly solutions that can help and motivate newly 
diagnosed people with type 2 diabetes to take responsibility and action for their disease. Many de­
vices and apps are created to measure blood glucose and to present the values on a graph. There are 
also many devices and apps that measure activity, sleep, heartbeat, calories eaten, etc. The users find 
it troublesome to shift from app to app and to combine data themselves, in order to get a picture of 
what factors affect their blood glucose. Users that do not have smartwatches or devices that measure 
sleep, exercise, and calories, find it even more annoying to look at the blood glucose values and not 
understand the reason why it is so high or low. Not knowing whether it was the potatoes one has 
eaten or the low amount of sleep that is the reason for high blood glucose values is frustrating and 
demotivating. Additionally, when visiting the doctor for a 3-month check, one will never know why 
the long-term blood glucose is at the level it is measured. 
Customer segment
As mentioned under ‘problem’ when a person becomes diagnosed with diabetes, there are a lot of dif­
ferent things one must do, think, relate to, act upon, etc. This can create a large amount of unnecessary 
stressful thoughts which increase the risk of one not taking action upon own disease at all.
Therefore newly diagnosed persons with type 2 diabetes are the main target group of this concept. 
Additionally, these people must have an interest in acting upon their disease by learning more about 
their blood glucose and lifestyle factors that can affect it. By having no interest in this topic, the users 
will experience other issues that are not covered in this project. 
Unique Value Proposition
When defining the unique values of the concept the ‘Elements of value’ pyramid is used to evaluate 
and address the concept in relation to four kinds of user needs: functional, emotional, life-changing, 
and social impact (Almquist E., Senior J., Bloch N., 2016).
The concept takes advantage of existing devices and apps that measure different kinds of parameters 
(blood glucose, exercise, sleep, etc.) and integrates them into the platform to control all these param­
eters in one place. All data and graphs are organised and simplified in a way that is understandable 
and pleasant for the user, as well as reduces stress and anxiety from too much information. 
The concept can inform the user about their status of goals, their progress, and their actions. Using 
AI the concept can predict when the user normally will eat, exercise or measure blood glucose, and 
thereby send notifications as a reminder. When the user has achieved personal goals or come through 
a good period the concept can reward them, which in the long-term can help the users feel more 
secure and less worried (reduce anxiety). Additionally, by providing a modular and preference ad­
justable platform, the user can feel more appreciated and accepted while interacting with the product. 
In general, the concept improves people’s physical and mental state by focusing on wellness and 
positive health progression. Providing this type of platform with the above-mentioned functions and 
values, the user can achieve motivation to reach goals and change lifestyle according to the learned 
material. Additionally, the concept can help the user achieve self-actualization by revealing personal 
Validation space
==PAGE:115==
111
accomplishements and improvements. 
Solution
The concept is an app that collects multiple different data from different devices and presents them 
in a visual manner through graphs and illustrations. A list of activities and measurements can also 
be viewed and selected to combine multiple parameters in one window. In this way, a context to the 
blood glucose measurements can be viewed and relation between blood glucose values and other 
parameters can be understood. All these activities and measurements are categorised to provide a 
simple and organised structure in the app. The user can additionally define and view goals in relation 
to the different measured parameters to see their own progress and achievements. 
If the user does not wish to be subjected to many different parameters from the beginning, he/she can 
choose to have the data and selection opportunities provided gradually and at the time when the user 
is ready him-/herself. 
A so-called buddy or helper is a robot programmed in the app to follow the users’ actions and meas­
urements. It points out areas when the users’ are doing great as well as provides inspiration and 
motivation Recommendations from the buddy are based on the users’ preset preferences and inputs 
through the setup and daily use of the app.
11.1.2	User Story Map
Having defined the business model of the concept, a more detailed exploration of the system, includ­
ing goals, activities, and functional tasks must be conducted. A tool called User Story Map is used 
by a design team to map out the different features that are to be included in the final concept and to 
organize them in different levels related to a Minimal Viable Product (MVP). Through this tool, the 
team can gain a better understanding of the concepts’ functionality and reasons for these, as well as 
save time and resources due to repeating discussions and features that must be reprogrammed (Patton, 
2014).
A Minimal Viable Product can help explore and define the necessary features of the concept that can 
fulfill the need of the customers to a degree that they are willing to start using it (Patton, 2014).
As products are not created perfectly from the first iteration, an absolute Minimal Viable Product is 
not formed until all features and tasks are tested and modified (will be detailed later). In this project, 
the aim is to create the first draft of a Minimal Viable Product that can continuously be iterated 
upon until the absolute necessary features are defined for the first release. To identify the necessary 
features for the concept a User Story Map is created, where activities and tasks placed in slice 1 are 
an attempt to form an MVP. Activities and tasks in slice 2 can wait to be implemented after release 
(Patton, 2014).
Most of the features that are created and defined by the participants in workshop 3 are placed in slice 
1. These we will use to test whether or not they are actually needed for an MVP and if some of them 
can wait to be implemented in future releases. As the created prototype is at a very early stage it must 
contain a limited amount of features. Therefore some desired functions by the users are placed in 
slice 2. These functions do however have the potential of being part of an MVP, so therefore as part 
of future work, these functions can be implemented into the prototype, be tested by the users, and be 
removed again if they are found to be less necessary.
In Appendix, p.101 a full User Story Map of the concept can be viewed. Following is a more detailed 
description of the goals and the different activities and tasks that the concept must perform.
Validation space
==PAGE:116==
112
The current version of the MVP prototype focus on achieving two goals: “Have an account based on 
personal preferences” and “Obtain a combined knowledge of all affecting factors”. 
The first goal is accomplished by setting up the device. First, the user must create a new profile and 
go through the introductory pages. With these pages, the users get acquainted with the concept and 
its purpose of it. Thereafter the users can voluntarily choose to setup four different elements as part 
of the preparations for the app: ‘Details about diagnosis’, ‘Device connections’, ‘Learning period’ 
and ‘‘Goal setting’ (Figure 53). The reason for doing this is to give the users a more personalised and 
pleasant experience with the app, by knowing their background, preferences, and wishes.  
‘Details about diagnosis’: Here the participants get an opportunity to enter details about their diagno­
sis. This includes the date of diagnosis, the long-term blood glucose value at the time of diagnosis, 
and additional details about their medication if they take any (what medication they take, how often, 
and how much).
‘Device connections’: Here the participants can connect any device they wish that can measure any 
data relevant to their blood glucose. Certainly, a blood glucose measuring device would be a good 
idea to connect, however, this is not a requirement as values can be input manually. Devices such as 
smartwatches or GPS’s are examples of what can be connected.
‘Learning period’: The participants can choose to gradually learn about their blood glucose and fac­
tors that can affect it. One by one the individual elements a user can measure will be available. Some 
time between the ‘openings’ will allow the user to start with measuring a new element, get used to 
Have an account based on 
personal preferences
GOAL 1
Obtain a combined knowl­
edge of all affecting factors
GOAL 2
Figure 52: Two main goals of MVP product (User Story Map)
Figure 53: Wireframes - Set up of app
Validation space
==PAGE:117==
113
it, and learn from it. In this way, they can start focusing on one thing at the beginning and slowly 
be introduced into a new life with diabetes. Under this setup option, the user can choose to keep the 
following measuring elements as a surprise (to not think about too much) or choose to open them and 
even edit if another order makes more sense. On the next page, the user can setup a buddy if wanted 
that includes a name and a figure representation. These different opportunities highly focus on pro­
viding the user a personalised experience based on their own preferences.
‘Goal setting’: If the user already has some personal goals or the doctor has already set some for one, 
the user in this setup option can define these goals and their time of achievement. The number and 
type of goals the user can define depend on whether or not he/she has chosen a learning period. All 
options including blood glucose, diet, exercise, sleep, etc. will be available if the user has not chosen 
a learning period, while if he/she has chosen this process only blood glucose and long-term blood 
glucose values will be present.
The second goal is accomplished by interacting with the app. There are several different functions 
here that the user can play around in any order they wish. This mainly includes viewing the measured 
data, going through the input activities and combining them with the graphs, viewing goals, adding 
new activity/measured data, etc. 
Main page:
On the main page, the user can view a graph of blood glucose measurements over a 
specific period of time. The user can move the graph from right to left to view other 
time intervals or change between data recorded throughout a day, week, month, or 
year (Figure 54).
Below the graph, the user can see all the activities he/she has manually input into the 
app or been automatically inputted for them. There are no boundaries of what activi­
ties the user can input here. This is mainly used as a diary, where one can enter where 
they have been, what they have been doing, or who they have been with. This can for 
example be dinner plans, parties, social activities, work-related, etc. If the user has 
gone for a walk and their smartwatch records the kilometers and time walked, the 
activity will automatically be inserted in the list (Figure 54). 
Figure 54: Wireframes - Main page with graph and list of activities. 
Select an activity and view details.
Validation space
==PAGE:118==
114
The activities can among other things be sorted by category, time zone, or degree of 
exercise. The activities can also be filtered to view the activity by alphabet or by time. 
Activities:
The user can choose to view details of the activities as well as its relation to the blood 
glucose graph. By clicking on an activity a dot with a similar colour appears on the 
blood glucose graph which shows when the activity was performed. In this way, the 
user can visually view the relation between the activity and the blood glucose value 
and better understand the reason why it is such high/low. Additionally, looking back 
on the graphs the user can remind him-/herself what they have been doing at the time 
the blood glucose started to increase, decrease or act weirdly (Figure 54). 
Clicking on the dot representing an activity more details about it appear. The user can 
view the photo or the video he/she has inserted, a description of the activity (content 
and calories eaten if it is a meal, GPS routes if it is exercise-related, etc.), and a com­
ment filed. In the comment field, the user can write anything they have in their mind 
related to the activity at any time (Figure 54).
Modules:
Modules is something different than activities. While activities show in detail what 
the user has been doing throughout the day, modules focus more on the measured and 
inputted data over a longer period of time. Most modules can also be seen as overall 
categories for activities. By choosing the modules an additional graph or points will 
appear on top of the blood glucose graph, so the user again can see a relation between 
the blood glucose measurements and other factors. There is no boundary of how many 
additional modules (graphs or points) the user can choose to view. Examples of mod­
ules that can be viewed as a graph are sleep curve, amount of steps, time of active ex­
ercise, etc. Examples of modules that can be viewed as points are meals, social events, 
workdays, etc (Figure 55).
Theoretically, the user can also choose to remove the blood glucose graph and present 
any other graphs through the modules option. The modules option is therefore a win­
dow where the user can change the view of the graphical area of the main page. Here 
they can personalize their app as they wish.
Figure 55: Wireframes - Selection of modules
Validation space
==PAGE:119==
115
Goals:
 The defined goals by the user can be viewed in relation to the different graphs. By 
clicking through the goals, the graphs adjust to additionally show boundaries, lines, or 
forecasts (Figure 56). 
New activity:  A new activity can be created by choosing the ‘plus’ icon on the right. Here the user 
can add a title, define time and date, choose an overall category, add a description with 
a picture or video and add a location. Title, date, and category are mandatory fields to 
define, while description and location are voluntary.
Slice 2:
 As mentioned previously, some functions that the users have identified in workshop 3 
are not part of the current prototype. They are placed in slice 2 of the User Story Map 
and are included in future versions of the prototype or final product. Some functions 
such as ‘manually add a new measurement’, ‘add a goal’, and ‘view activities  within 
a module’ must be part of slice 1 even though they currently lay in slice 2. If the user 
must be able to see blood glucose values on the graph and he/she does not own a CGM, 
the user must also be able to manually add these values into the app. The same applies 
to goals that can be added or changed in the app and not only when setting the app up. 
These functions are not implemented into the current prototype due to the time limit 
and focus on other more testable features.
In the future versions of the product, the user will receive reminders and notifications 
from their buddy when a goal is achieved or the user is doing in general great. This 
part of the product must be tested a couple of times to define what kind of interaction 
there must be between the buddy and the user.
Furthermore, the user will be able to export all data in a user-friendly way and share 
it with their doctor. This can contribute to a more effortless connection and dialogue 
between the user and their doctor as all measurements, activities, graphs, etc. can be 
visualised and brought up during the consultation. Additionally, all measurements that 
the doctor makes can be automatically be sent to the app, so that the user has all values 
Figure 56: Wireframes - Selection of modules
Validation space
==PAGE:120==
116
gathered in one place. 
Currently, the user can set up a learning period in the preparation wireframes, however, 
this function is not included in the current prototype. Being able to gradually learn 
about diabetes and blood glucose is a goal that can be achieved in future versions of 
the product. It was however chosen to include and introduce the users with this feature 
in the preparation wireframes to receive initial thoughts that could be discussed in this 
project.
