11.2	PROTOTYPE TESTING
Now that an executable prototype is created, the concept can be tested with the users. The reason for 
validating the concept early is to make sure it is compelling and user-friendly before a high amount 
of resources on development are spent (Klein, 2013). Preferably when the product is released to the 
users it must include all the features a user needs and wants, as well as it must be error-free and create 
no misunderstandings. By testing the concept with the users, we can figure out whether the product 
solves the identified problem for the specified market, whether the product has all the necessary func­
tions, and whether the user is satisfied when interacting with the product (Klein, 2013).
Alongside validating the product, the design team must additionally validate the problem and the 
market. This is to ensure that the right issues are to be solved for the right people (Klein, 2013). In 
this project, we only focus on validating the product as it has been developed through a co-creation 
process where the users themselves have defined the problem and created the concept solving it. 
There are several different ways to explore and validate the potential of a product/concept, including 
different methods and approaches. Defining a hypothesis for the test helps determine the direction 
one should take, regarding what data to collect, what methods to use, and who to test on. A hypoth­
esis is a prediction of a test result that can be disproven based on user opinions and behaviour. The 
hypothesis is therefore a clear statement of how the design team thinks the concept will be compre­
hended and interacted with (King R., F. Churchill E., Tan E., 2017). 
11.2.1	Planning the test
As this product validation is the first of its kind where the users for the first time see and interact with 
a simulated version of a real product, the testing procedure will be more observant and perceptive. 
The users have not seen the product since they have created it in workshop 3 and therefore it is highly 
interesting to hear their initial thoughts and observe how they interact with a product they see for the 
first time. 
https://xd. adobe. com/view/62b1a05b-918b-4cf9-8d7c-d5ec991354d6-37e2/?fullscreen
“Best way of figuring out if idea solves somebody’s problem: Show them something and 
observe their reactions. ” (Klein, 2013)
Validation space
==PAGE:121==
117
Empirical methods will mostly be used during the testing, as we wish to observe the performance 
of the users and obtain knowledge coming directly from the user. A thinking-aloud technique is an 
empirical data collection method that has the users verbally express their thoughts, motives, or per­
ceptions while interacting with the prototype and performing the different activities and tasks. This 
technique captures the user’s expectations, understanding, and judgment of the product at the precise 
time of their occurrence. Thereby, the design team can collect insights that otherwise will not have 
been revealed during a conversation or other methods (Hartson R., Pyla P., 2019). 
Some users might not feel comfortable speaking aloud while interacting with an app. Therefore in 
case, the users do not perform the thinking-aloud technique as required, a couple of questions are 
prior to the test defined to continue the conversation. These questions are also used to gain insights 
about specific features and functions that were not covered by the thinking-aloud technique. Some 
questions directly ask the user about their opinion and perception of a specific element. Other ques­
tions focus on the workflow of the prototype and therefore ask the user to perform a specific task and 
observe how they proceed. 
While the user verbally expresses their thoughts on the prototype’s performance, the designer takes 
notes and observes the interaction. Some of these interactions require an evaluation or analysis to 
understand the UX problem of the performed tasks. Therefore Expert UX inspections method (part of 
analytical evaluation methods) will be used both during and after the test to pinpoint areas where 
users’ additional thoughts might be needed and areas for further improvement (Hartson R., Pyla P., 
2019). 
Hypothesis
Hypothesis for this prototype testing session are divided into two types: ‘Feature and value based 
hypothesis’ and ‘Interface based hypothesis’ (Figure 57). The reason for having both hypotheses is, 
as mentioned previously, to gain initial insights on the overall concept and the prototype’s functions. 
In further validation sessions and iterations, a more specific and fixed direction will be taken, to go 
more into detail of the different concept goals. 
‘Feature and value based hypothesis’ focuses on testing four different features: setting up the app, 
viewing modules and activities, defining goals, and reading additional information. In the first hy­
pothesis, we wish to observe the user’s understanding of the four categories of setting up the app 
and if they find them necessary for their motivation and satisfaction with the app. In the second goal, 
users’ interaction with the modules and activity features are observed to see if they understand the 
difference between the two features and their purpose (which is creating a personalised platform). 
Additionally, we wish to gain insights into whether or not these modular functions can increase the 
user’s knowledge about their disease. In the third hypothesis, we wish to see if setting goals in the app 
can motivate the user to achieve them and thereby improve their health state. In this test, we cannot 
observe if their health state gets better, however, we can see in the users understand the purpose of 
setting goals up, and if they find it necessarry for their regular use of the app. In the fourth hypothesis, 
the main goal is to observe the understanding of the additional information feature placed around the 
app, and if it contributes to decreasing the amount of misunderstanding and doubts of the different 
features the app provides (Figure 57). 
In the ‘interface based hypothesis’ we test three different elements: the workflow of the app, the in­
formation provided to the user, and the selection opportunity. In hypothesis 5 we observe the user’s 
general interaction with the app and if they without any issues and complexities can navigate through 
the different features. In hypothesis 6 we observe the user’s understanding of the learning period 
feature and whether they find the elements necessary to keep secret under the setup or they would 
Validation space
==PAGE:122==
118
rather have all elements open. Lastly, in hypothesis 7, we predict that the user would rather choose an 
animal than a human representation when setting up their buddy (Figure 57). 
Target group
Due to the limit of time, a big recruitment process was not initiated for this validation session. As 
the participants from workshops have formed the concept themselves, including defining a problem 
direction, they should have the opportunity to follow the development process further and view the 
executable prototype built on their ideas. It was therefore decided to invite all participants from all 
three workshops to this first test of the prototype. 
The procedure and questions
Figure 57: Hypothesis for first testing session
Validation space
==PAGE:123==
119
The test starts by welcoming the users back to this project and describing the process to this point that 
they have not been part of. Users that participated in the last workshop know the process prior to the 
workshop and know the outcome of it. Thereby a big introduction was not needed for them. However, 
users that participated only in the first or second workshop received a more detailed introduction, for 
them to understand the context of the test and the reason for having them engaged.
The users then start the app and proceed through the introductory and set-up pages by clicking on 
any available features (Figure 58). The designer asks the users about the overall experience and 
understanding of these pages. Thereafter the users proceed to the main page where they are allowed 
to navigate around as they wish. The thinking-aloud technique takes effect asking the user to speak 
aloud while interacting with the different features. 
‘Find functions’ questions are defined to observe how the user will find and navigate to the requested 
functions. These are asked if the user thinks he/she is done navigating around the whole app, while 
the designer knows that some functions are yet not discovered by the user. Here we can detect prob­
lems regarding unclear design if users have high difficulty in finding the requested functions (Figure 
58). 
‘Understanding’ questions are defined to gain insights about the user’s understanding of the different 
features and the difference between them. Mainly goals, activities, and modules can be difficult to 
understand difference between, and thereby if the user has not mentioned it during the thinking-aloud 
technique, the designers can ask these questions (Figure 58). 
Lastly, general questions are mainly focused on the overall prototype and concept. They are asked 
at the end of the testing session to initiate a small discussion about the users’ opinions about the app, 
about positive, and negative experiences during the interaction and about potential improvements 
(Figure 58).

==PAGE:124==
120
11.2.2	Results
Out of the total 12 different user participants from all three workshops, 7 users signed up for testing 
the prototype (Figure 59). 3 of them have followed the whole development process through all three 
workshops, 1 of them participated in the two last workshops, while the rest 3 users have only par­
ticipated in one workshop (2 in workshop 1 and 1 in workshop 3). The 7 users combined therefore 
formed a broad space of knowledge that was useful to identify different insights during the testing 
sessions. All results from the testing sessions can be found in Appendix, p.102.
Each user focused on different areas of the concept while conducting the thinking-aloud technique. 
They have all different needs regarding the app and the solution in general, and therefore a lot of 
different insights and issues regarding the design were gained. Some comments from the users were 
Figure 58: Questions for testing session
Validation space
==PAGE:125==
121
more detailed than others which also made some comments more useful for the next iteration than 
others. In general, the feedback was highly positive and most users could see themselves using the 
app in the future. The users were so deeply engaged in the prototype during the test as they, beyond 
commenting on the functionality and design of the current version of the app, also gave additional 
ideas and improvements for future iterations. 
Going through the introductory wireframes the users clearly understood the purpose of the pages 
and thought it was a great way to be acquainted with the app. The set-up pages gave, however, bigger 
problems as different users perceived the different functions differently. Briefly looking on the overall 
set-up page one user found this as a preparation of the app with some settings and some descriptions 
of how to use the app. Other participants did not know what to say about it, and instead immediately 
started commenting on the four different set-up elements. The UI of the setup page was not very clear 
as the users did not know that the four elements were clickable and that there were more interaction 
behind them. Furthermore, only one user understood and used the ‘information’ icons to read about 
the different set-up elements. 
‘Details about diagnosis’: Most of the users see this function as an option to input some information 
about their diagnosis made by their doctor. They are slightly unsure wheth­
er is it themselves or the doctor who fills this information out. In general, 
the users find this feature interesting and great as a starter for the app. As 
an improvement, one user wanted a possibility to enter multiple long-term 
blood glucose values, as she was confused about what “current” long-term 
blood glucose value means. 
‘Device connections’:
 Briefly reading the title of the set-up element, most users do not understand 
what the function is and what value it gives to the user. Some see it as a 
description of how the app works, others think this is where the app con­
nects to the internet or Bluetooth. Once the users click on this feature and 
see the content of it, they immediately understand the purpose of it, which 
is connecting multiple devices that can measure the needed data to the app. 
‘Learning period’:
 This function was the most misunderstood and confusing feature of the 
set-up page. None of the users understood this feature clearly and instead 
came with a lot of interesting guesses, some that even can be used for 
future iterations. Some thought it was a period one gets, where he/she is 
not allowed to view the data (as they are learning it). Some thought it 
helped the user learn how to use the app over a specific time period. Others 
thought it showed for how long one has been measuring data.
Figure 59: Participants for testing session
Validation space
==PAGE:126==
122
The understanding of this feature did not increase when the users clicked 
on the ‘Learning period’ and saw the list with “presents”. Most users did 
not read the text above the list, as it was too small and less interesting 
compared to the rest of the page. Interestingly enough, most users each had 
a thought in their mind regarding the meaning of the “presents”. As they 
had to verbally express their thoughts, the users seemed highly excited and 
satisfied about the feature, not knowing the real purpose of it. Some users 
related this list with goals that are achieved after a specific period of time. 
As the users could not view the elements hidden behind the “presents” they 
thought all of them were blood glucose-related. Additionally, the users 
thought the “presents” were actual rewards. This could be that the user 
receives a recipe for a cake or a subscription to the nearest fitness center. 
Opening the “presents” gave additional confusion. One user even men­
tioned: “Now I am confused why I should receive this present...” in rela­
tion to that the real function is not goal-related. 
In general, what confused the users the most is the list with periods in be­
tween and the “presents”. Most users were highly curious about what the 
real function is, and thereby it was decided to reveal the real intention of 
the function to hear the users’ thoughts about it. After revealing the real 
function all users understood the purpose of it clearly and thought it was a 
great idea.
Concerning to the personal buddy feature, most users could see the purpose 
of it but could not see themselves using it. They mentioned the feature is 
preference-based, and thereby they think some users would like this, while 
others not. It should thereby not be removed from the app and should be 
kept voluntarily. One user suggested that this feature could expand to hav­
ing real-life buddies instead of a robot, where the users can match together 
and motivate each other.
Another interesting fact is that one user thinks this feature is mostly for the 
newly diagnosed. They would need to learn the different affecting factors 
and would like to do it in a gradually. He could see himself using the app 
to test some different elements and to see how his blood glucose is doing, 
however, he can not see himself using the ‘Learning period’. Another user 
mentioned some of the same things, however, he does not think this fea­
ture must be provided to the newly diagnosed. He thinks there is too much 
going on in this set-up element and that there are too many decisions one 
must take in relation to their learning period. 
‘Goal setting’:
 Most users understood this feature as a place to input personal goals in 
relation to their diabetes that must be achieved after some time. However, 
one user thinks this feature is presented too early for the newly diagnosed 
person. When the person gets the diagnosis he/she might not know what 
their goals are yet. Therefore, it is either the doctor who must set goals for 
their patients or this option should not be available for the user as a set-up 
element.
Validation space
==PAGE:127==
123
Design-wise some users thought the workflow was slightly confusing, as 
it took them some time to find out how to input a specific goal. 
Continuing to the main page the users found the design of it compelling and elegant, something that 
did not scare the users away from continuing exploring the app. The users could see a graph which 
there were different opinions and understandings about. Some found the graphs highly intuitive, easy 
to understand, and to manage, while others could not figure out what kind of data is presented and 
how it correlates to the list below. One user thought there was a connection between the pink colour 
of the graph and the activities below. She started searching for a pink activity, but could not find any, 
which greatly confused her. Another user could not understand the graph as he could not see any axis 
title. 
List of activities:
 Looking at the list of activities below the graph, the users had also different 
opinions. One user could not understand the content of it as the date above the 
graph did not correlate with the dates near the activities. He would prefer that 
the list only included activities that were inputted for the current day. A couple of 
other users liked the overview that they clearly understood the purpose of. They 
liked the opportunity that one could input different kind of activities and then 
see them correlated to the blood glucose values. Some users, however, did not 
understand the round dot on the graph and the letter next to it, once they clicked 
on an activity. The correlation between the dot and the activity was not clear, as 
some users even thought the dots simply pointed out a specific area of the graph 
(highest/lowest point). 
Goals:
The function of viewing goals in relation to the graph was relatively clear for 
all users. They understood that it is a tool the users can activate to guide them 
towards positive values. Only one misunderstanding was regarding the blood 
glucose value goal itself. The users did not find this function useful, as it does 
not make sense for the user to reach a specific value at a specific time. Instead, 
the user should be able to see a line (as for the medium glucose level) under/over 
which one should keep his/her blood glucose.
Modules:
 All users had confusion about the ‘modules’ function. The word itself was not 
inviting or user-friendly at all, and none of the users correlated the title with 
the actual features beneath, once they opened this element. Looking at the list 
of modules some users were slightly confused about them, as they were not 
sure how this data is collected and how it is represented. Some users requested 
a better categorization, while others requested a larger correlation between the 
modules and goals. The difference between the modules and activity was clear 
for most of the users, however, they did not recognize the connection between 
these two features. 
New activity:
 The most interesting feedback about this feature is that only one out of 7 could 
navigate to this. The rest of the users searched for it in the activity list. Most us­
ers found all the possible input opportunities clear and useful, while some users 
did not understand the purpose of categories.
General feedback of the concept was, as mentioned, highly positive. One user could see the app as a 
great help to get started with one’s treatment. Another user could see how the app can motivate one 
to measure blood glucose more often. The third user could see the app help increase one’s awareness. 
Validation space
==PAGE:128==
124
While the fourth user sees the app as a personal friend one can make agreements with. 
11.2.3	Discussion
The testing sessions with 7 different users were highly successful in gathering a lot of different com­
ments in different areas of the concept. During the thinking-aloud technique and additional questions, 
the users covered almost all features of the app as well as had comments and suggestions for im­
provement to many functions both experience (UX) and design-wise (UI). 
A problem that the app wishes to solve, is that a newly diagnosed person must take too many actions 
and relate to too much information. These users have a lot on their minds regarding the disease and 
therefore an app that requires one to take several decisions and thoughts from the beginning is not 
attractive. During the set-up of the app, a lot of discussions occurred regarding the ‘Learning period’ 
function. Currently, the app provides the user with an opportunity to start a learning period. This func­
tion requires the user to understand the meaning and purpose of the learning period and the whole list 
with hidden “presents”. Regardless of how great this function is for a newly diagnosed, providing this 
information and opportunity from the beginning may confuse the user more than necessary. Thereby, 
with this feature, we may move away from the main value of the app towards maintaining the current 
user problem which is: “As newly diagnosed there are too many actions one must do and too much in­
formation one must relate to. ” (Lean Canvas (section 11.1.1)). The same applies to the ‘Goal setting’ 
feature. The content of the setup page is therefore too excessive and some decision-making elements 
must be considered to be removed. 
The above-mentioned insight is mostly applicable for the newly diagnosed. Users with more expe­
rience might not have issues with several decision-making elements from the start use of the app. 
As the current customer segment focuses on the newly diagnosed people with type 2 diabetes, the 
above-mentioned insight is interesting to explore further and create a solution to accommodate this 
issue. An idea could be to flip the procedure of the initial wireframes and have the users already be 
part of the learning period when starting the app. The users can then later turn it off if wanted. Setting 
personal goals can be part of the learning period, where the user after some time learns how to set 
realistic and positive goals.
The users mentioned some of the values defined in the Lean Canvas (section 11.1.1) which shows 
that the overall concept and the functions within are being accepted. Through an integrated platform 
where data is organized and simplified the users feel motivated to reach their goals and to measure 
their blood glucose. Having the opportunity to set personal goals, to choose what data to represent, 
and to make own conclusions based on an altogether overview makes the solution highly personal­
ised. This solves the following user problem from Lean Canvas (section 11.1.1): “Recommendations 
and solutions, created to help treat diabetes, are too generic and does not accommodate the individ­
uals’ life. ” The following problem statement is somewhat also solved as a solution combining mul­
tiple data is created: “There are no user-friendly solutions managing blood glucose measurements 
together with all different lifestyle factors in one.”.
However, exploring the functionality and experience of the different features in the app, the users, 
unfortunately, encountered several misunderstandings, confusions, and doubts, which does not make 
the current version of the concept very user-friendly. In some cases, the user only understood the real 
functionality after it was revealed by the designer. Most of the hypotheses set prior to the interview 
are disproven, while the rest could not be determined, as the hypothesis were not clearly tested. On 
Figure 60 the reason for the hypothesis being disproven can be seen. Disproven hypotheses are great 
in indicating the direction the design team must proceed as well as identifying areas where further 
research, ideation, and testing must be needed (King R., F. Churchill E., Tan C., 2017). For further 
Validation space
==PAGE:129==
125
validation, some hypotheses might also need to be remade to focus more on the functions and the ex­
perience of the interface instead of the values. Users’ values, like wellness, motivation, and satisfac­
tion, are hard to prove over a small testing session, where the user does not perform a real interaction 
with the prototype. The users must interact with the prototype for a longer period in order to observe 
a difference in user behaviour and thoughts. 
Even though there were several misunderstandings of features as ‘Learning period’, activities and 
modules, the users were great in guessing the purposes of the functions and suggesting alternatives. 
Most of their guesses and suggestions were useful for the project as they could be integrated into a 
future version of the app. An example related to the ‘Leaning period’ function, is including goals one 
can achieve as part of the “present” a user can open. Presents could include real rewards that would 
be sponsored by different shops or facilities. An A/B testing method (Klein, 2013) could in the future 
be conducted to test two different versions of the ‘Learning period’: 1) the current version where dif­
ferent lifestyle factors open after a certain time and 2) a version where goal set on different lifestyle 
factors must be achieved after a certain time. Thereby each version can be shown to different users, 
who then can determine which function is more valuable and necessary for the user (Klein, 2013).
Figure 60: Proven/disproven hypothesis from testing session
Validation space
==PAGE:130==
126
Some feedback from the users was unfortunately too detailed, which made some comments more 
meaningsless than others for the next iterations. Some users focused too much on the colours, the text 
sizes, and the content of the texts (e.g. time and date) that they did not notice or did not comment on 
some of the more important features. While designing the prototype, many of these interfaces fea­
tures were simply added and not further considered or reflected upon. One user for example noticed 
that the general date did not correlate with the activity’s date and decided to focus and comment on 
this element for a couple of minutes. This was slightly frustrating as his comment made perfect sense 
and the prototype should have accommodated this error prior to the test. Instead, the user spent time 
commenting on a small interface error than on the important features of the concept.
These comments are not completely useless, they are simply given at the wrong time. The hypothesis 
for this test (section 11.2.1) focused on the workflow of the app, the understanding of the different 
functions, and user preferences. These were defined due to the early stage of testing that focuses more 
on the experience of the app and not the interface design. Once the minimal viable product with the 
necessary functions is defined, the designer can proceed with tests focusing on the layout and visual 
representation of data. Therefore, reflecting on the creation of the prototype, the fidelity of the proto­
type must have been more correlated with the hypothesis set for the initial test. A more low-fidelity 
prototype should have been created in order for the users to focus to a greater extent on the functions 
rather than the interface (more discussion under ‘Future work’).
It was interesting to see that several users came with multiple different suggestions, ideas, thoughts 
for improvement, etc. instead of purely identifying issues and problems of the user experience. All 
users that tested the product also participated in the facilitated workshops. Here they got acquainted 
with the co-creation process and creative thinking. This may be the reason for them being more com­
fortable and relaxed about ideating upon critical issues. Additionally, as the users have contributed to 
forming the concept, they may feel more comfortable in giving inputs, than people who never have 
seen the concept before. In future testing sessions, it could therefore be interesting to test the concept 
on completely new users, to not only explore additional problematic areas of the app but also to ob­
serve their behaviour during testing and their insights in relation to this.
11.2.4	Conclusion to testing sessions
In order to figure out: 1) if the general concept solves the identified problem of newly diagnosed 
people with type 2 diabetes, 2) if the concept has all the necessary functions, and 3) if the user is 
satisfied while interacting with the concept, a prototype of the concept was tested with 7 users from 
the workshops. From the results and discussions, the main outcome is following:
•
Most users can see themselves using the app in the future, as they find it motivating and helpful 
to start treating diabetes, start measuring blood glucose, and reaching personal goals. The con­
cept solves most of the problems that the customer segment defined in Lean Canvas (section 
11.1.1) experiences.
•
The content of the set-up page is too excessive, as it includes several different options and deci­
sion-making elements that a user must consider and relate to when being introduced to the app. 
This contradicts with the goal of the app, which is removing the pain of too much information 
and action a newly diagnosed person must relate to. These insights, mainly directed towards 
features such as ‘Learning period’ and ‘Goal setting’ will be explored further to create an attrac­
tive solution that accommodates this issue. 
•
The current version of the prototype is not very user-friendly as the users encountered several 
misunderstandings, confusion, and doubts in relation to the different features while testing the 
Validation space
==PAGE:131==
127
concept. Most hypotheses were disproven which shows that the prototype has several areas 
for improvement for the next iterations. Some hypotheses will be adjusted to focus more on 
the functions and the experience of the interface, while the rest of the hypotheses will form the 
direction the design team can proceed with. 
•
During the test users were great at guessing the purposes of the functions and suggesting al­
ternatives when they did not understand the feature. Some ideas generated by the users, as 
including goals in the “present” packages and providing the user with real rewards behind the 
“present”, can be further analysed and possibly included in future versions of the prototype. An 
A/B testing method can additionally be used to test the different ideas of one function.
•
The fidelity of the prototype must have been more correlated with the hypothesis set for the 
initial test, as the users commented excessively on the design of colours, text size, and content 
of text rather than the functionality and general workflow. For future work, creating a more 
low-fidelity prototype can be considered, in order for the users to focus to a greater extent on 
the functions rather than the interface.
This phase is the last of the Double Diamond process and concludes this project. In the next sections 
,a discussion of the overall process will be conducted together with a reflection and a recommendation 
for future work.

DISCUSSION &
FUTURE WORK

reflected upon, and concluded. Recommendations for future 
work are described and the problem statement for this project 
will be answered.
==PAGE:133==

The goal and problem statement of this thesis is:
Several different methods have been used to explore the problem statement and create a solution for 
it. This includes research of current studies and market, analysis of the current state, interviews for 
exploratory purposes, workshops through a co-creation process, and testing for validation. All phases 
of this ‘Quadruple’ Diamond design process (section 7) were connected and results from one phase 
have been used and explored in the next phase. In this section, the interconnection between the differ­
ent methods and phases are discussed, where the outcome of the project is compared to the problem 
statement of the thesis. 
The outcome of this project is an app combining blood glucose measurements with other affecting 
lifestyle factors, to gain an overview and context of reasons behind glucose level fluctuations. The 
app is mostly intended for newly diagnosed persons with type 2 diabetes, who has the need for more 
personal recommendations and solutions that can manage blood glucose measurement and other af­
fecting factors in one and in a user-friendly manner (section 11.1.1).
The app and its functional content originated from three workshops where experienced T2D users 
with blood glucose measurement collaborated with experts and designers in exploring and defining 
different problems as well as developing and detailing ideas and concepts. 
The process towards this solution differed from previous studies by including the users throughout 
the whole design and development process. Furthermore, the users were on an equal level with de­
signers where they mutually with each their own skills and knowledge could create problems and 
concepts together. The success of this so-called co-creation process can however first be assessed 
during the test to validate the concept and its user experience. UX design process method was used 
during the whole project to ensure that the final product is usable, useful, meaningful, and creates 
emotional impact for the target group. 
Final outcome
(Catherine H Yu et al., 2014) conducted focus groups and individual testing sessions to define the ac­
ceptability, sustainability, strength, and weaknesses of their product. (Wayne W Zachary et al. 2017) 
additionally created a solution first and thereafter invited the users to test and redefine the content 
and design of the product. In both studies, the results showed some user needs not met due to a lack 
of essential functions. If the design team has involved the users from the very beginning of the de­
sign process, will these unmet needs and functions have been defined and incorporated early in the 
process? Compared to this projects’ co-creation process, some of the functions (Catherine H Yu et al., 
2014) identified missing in her product, the participants in workshops have explored and integrated 
into the app. This includes access to tailored knowledge and mechanisms to control and track their 
disease. Opportunities to share the experience with other persons with diabetes have been mentioned 
by some participants in the workshops, however, the need was not crucial enough to implement in an 
MVP. Additionally, the users themselves identified the need for education and knowledge for improv­
ing self-management of blood glucose and life quality which supports the study by (Sanbao Chai et 
How may we improve the user experience of measuring blood glucose 
levels on a regular basis, through a co-creation process methodology?

DISCUSSION AND REFLECTION
==PAGE:134==
130
al., 2018) and (Yee Cheng Kueh et al., 2015).
One may always discuss that the outcome from the first validation session was not perfect either. 
All users had some misunderstandings, were confused, and had better ideas for some of the current 
functions. However, none of the participants directly mentioned lacking or missing functions in the 
prototype, as well as user needs that have not been met. Even though the current version of the pro­
totype must be tested some more to improve the overall user experience of the product and measure 
blood glucose in general, the prototype is already closer to achieving the goal of the project than 
previous studies following a user-centered and participatory design approach. Through a co-creation 
process and workshops, the users had more time with the product than through usual interviews and 
focus groups. Therefore, the users have additionally defined interesting nice-to-have features to the 
app that other process methodologies may not uncover. These nice-to-have features do not neces­
sarily form an MVP, however, they do contribute to an overall user experience of the product over a 
longer period of time. This was discussed by (Povalej Brzan P., Rotman E., Pajnkihar M., Klanjsek P., 
2016) who focused on the need for additional features in apps to increase usefulness for successful 
self-management of diabetes and to preserve users long-term.  
Studies as (Fatehi F., C. Gray L., W. Russell A., 2017) also discuss a need for engaging the patients 
with T2D more in future research and products to retain them over a longer period of time. As this 
project only conducted the Build-Measure-Learn cycle ones (section 7), we cannot conclude yet 
whether the users will be retained long-term and whether the co-creation process was a success in 
terms of improving the overall user experience of measuring blood glucose. After the first validation, 
it was discussed that the outcome was positive and that the prototype was closer to achieving the pro­
ject goal. However, the final success cannot be measured until the prototype is tested with a signifi­
cant amount of users, all functions have been evaluated and the product is used in the relevant context 
for some time. This is additionally applicable for the four user experience components, as emotional 
impact and meaningsfulness can be evaluated after several iterations (discussed more in future work). 
Research questions throughout the process
Throughout the preliminary research and analysis of the current state, several research questions were 
introduced to challenge the study as well as to form a research direction for further design phases, 
such as exploratory interviews, workshops, and validation. In a user-centered design process, the 
design team may define these research questions, have some of them answered through focus groups 
and interviews, and thereafter based on their interpretation create or refine solutions. In this projects’ 
co-creation process, research questions were more helpful when planning the workshops (to form a 
direction) as well as initiating the discussions between the users in the workshops. The participants 
answered some of these research questions which could be used for discussion of the results. There­
after the designers together with the users could identify problems or create solutions that either 
detailed or resolved some of these research questions. Thereby, no interpretations of the results were 
needed to answer the research questions, which make the discussions of the results more interesting 
and valuable. 
Co-creation definition
In ‘Related work’ chapter (section 5) it was revealed and discussed that a more clear definition of the 
used co-creation process and methods within must be established when conducting such a process. 
Throughout the report, it was, therefore, important to be precise and straightforward in relation to the 
methods used and the decisions made. For future projects and reflections, it is, therefore, easier to go 
back and see where potential changes could be made to the process. 
Discussion and further work
==PAGE:135==
131
Overall process and its limitations
The overall process of planning and conducting analysis, interviews, workshops, and testing sessions 
were highly limited by time and resources. In the first couple of months, there was enough time to 
plan the different phases and the desired outcome. However, once the interviews were initiated there 
was limited time in further planning and recruiting participants, as other tasks (such as processing re­
sults, creating boundary objects, etc.) were equally important. Looking back on the process the time 
before the interviews could have included more preparations for the upcoming phases. 
Beyond the slight stress, there has been between interviews, workshops, and testing, the process has 
been highly interesting to follow and a lot of new insights have been gained. This includes not only 
topic-related insights but also the facilitation and planning part of the workshops. From the partici­
pants’ feedback regarding the workshops, it was very nice to hear that the process, the exercises, and 
the discussions were also valuable for them. 
12.1	FUTURE WORK
Throughout the whole process and the discussions made along the way, there are several aspects in 
the project that set a basis for future work. This includes further research, analysis, product develop­
ment, and testing. The Build-Measure-Learn model must be used to iterate upon the current version 
of the prototype and test it with potential users. Aspects that invite for future work are listed here:
Research and analysis
•
Based on the System Demarcation model this thesis focused on a limited part of the ‘Interven­
tion system’ and ‘Environment’ area. In this project, only research made by the Danish Diabetes 
Association and previous work was fully analysed. However, it may also be interesting to ad­
dress projects made by other stakeholders as well as hear their experiences with T2D persons. 
These stakeholders may have a larger impact on the project than it was initially assumed. 
•
A lot of research and previous studies are made on mHealth systems and apps. For future work, 
it may be interesting to do more analysis on what makes a great app for the healthcare sector as 
well as how to implement an additional app into a market full of solutions. 
•
Further studies may also include research upon individual creativity. What kind of people are 
more creative and why, as well as what type of people to include in tests. This research is main­
ly based on the different roles that are gathered together in a collaborative co-creation process. 
Prototype
•
The creation of the prototype has been discussed under the validation space (section 11). A 
great emphasis was put on the fidelity of the created prototype. In this phase, the functions giv­
en to the prototype were mainly based on the outcome from the workshops and the design of it 
was based on own interest. As it was highly interesting to work in Adobe XD the design of the 
prototype may have been carried away which also affected the outcome of the tests. For future 
work, it thereby may be important to go slightly back in the process and redesign the prototype 
to a lower level of fidelity. From there the fidelity of the prototype must increase slowly for each 
iteration. Furthermore, the design team can include a more systematic and theoretic approach 
to the prototype creation, to make sure that all aspects of the concept are covered in a most 
efficient way (this includes theory about creating horizontal vs. vertical prototypes (Hartson R., 
Pyla P. (2019)),
Testing
Discussion and further work
==PAGE:136==
132
•
For testing the product it is highly important to include users that have not been part of the 
workshops and thereby part of creating the concept. We wish to observe the experience of user 
interaction from persons that never have seen the concept and hear whether their needs are 
solved.
•
In the future it may also be interesting to test with users that are outside of the current scope and 
segment. This is part of testing the market and the problem again to determine that the direction 
the participants from the workshops have defined is the most important and critical.
•
The current version of the prototype could not incorporate all desired functions from the partic­
ipants in the workshops. It is therefore too early to say if the current functions define an MVP. 
For future work, tests must be made on other features of the product as well. 
•
Numerous amount of iterations must be made before reaching a Minimum Viable Product and 
releasing it to the market!
Discussion and further work
==PAGE:137==

13.	 CONCLUSION
The goal of this thesis was to conduct research, analysis, interviews, workshops, and testing sessions. 
This was to investigate the current state of life with type 2 diabetes and self-management of blood 
glucose as well as to explore the potential of improving the user experience of measuring blood glu­
cose on a regularly. This project followed a ‘Quadruple’ Diamond model consisting of three spaces: 
research space, workshop space, and validation space. Through a mixture of UX design process and 
co-creation process, the following can be concluded for the project.
Diabetes is a chronic disease that, due to complications, symptoms, risks, prevention, and treatment 
of it, is very different from person to person. Persons with type 2 diabetes can mainly treat their 
disease by self-management of ordinary lifestyle factors, like diet, exercise, and sleep, as well as 
self-monitoring of blood glucose levels. Through a regular measurement of blood glucose levels, a 
person with type 2 diabetes can detect potential deviations (very high or low values) that may indi­
cate what factors have a negative influence on the blood glucose. With these insights a person with 
type 2 diabetes can regulate their diet, exercise, sleep, and medication according to what their body 
responds positive to.
Previous studies have shown that education and knowledge about self-management may improve 
patients’ psychological status affecting blood glucose in a positive direction. Studies have also shown 
that mobile health apps (mHealth apps) may improve the quality of diabetes care, communication 
between patients and health professionals, enhancement of self-management, and reduction of costs. 
Other research states that gaining insight and knowledge about blood glucose and affecting factors 
may help persons with type 2 diabetes make better life choices. 
However, measuring blood glucose can for most people be discouraging, demotivating, and stressful 
due to the current practices and user experiences. Besides having consultations with general prac­
titioners or hospitals and receiving medicines from pharmacies, a person with type 2 diabetes must 
contact all other stakeholders, such as the Danish Diabetes Association, municipalities, dietitians, 
product manufacturers, etc. him-/herself if desired. Exploratory interviews have shown that some 
persons with type 2 diabetes gain their own knowledge about their disease as they feel health profes­
sionals do not guide the patients, do not enlighten them about all possibilities, and do not describe the 
consequence of non-treated diabetes. Persons with type 2 diabetes do not get any recommendations 
from the health professionals about self-management of blood glucose, and therefore some are not 
even aware of this opportunity. Persons who do measure their blood sugar have a great experience 
with the process and feel highly motivated, however, there are some disturbing factors regarding the 
user experience, such as interruption of daily life, work, and chores, irritation of remembering all 
equipment, etc. 
To establish a closer connection with the users and to let them be part of the whole design process in 
collaboration with designers and experts, a co-creation process was used and three workshops were 
conducted. The goal was to obtain knowledge and generate new ideas within type 2 diabetes and 
self-management of blood glucose with help from the users and designers combined. 
In workshop 1, problems related to life with type 2 diabetes were explored, identified, and discov­
ered. Beyond the area of blood glucose and controlling its levels, issues and experiences regard­
CONCLUSION
Conclusion
==PAGE:138==
134
ing knowledge, health professionals, and exercise were additionally discovered. The first workshop, 
thereby gave an overall understanding of diabetes and how it is to live with today. From this work­
shop the planning team thereby created 23 problem statements covering the most discussed issues by 
the participants.
In workshop 2, the 23 problem statements were further discussed and additional insights were gath­
ered due to the participation of experts. 13 of these 23 problems were chosen as the most critical, 
important, and interesting to solve while 3 additional ones were also defined as important. The par­
ticipants had several different ideas of how some of these problem statements could be solved, which 
resulted in 9 full concepts. 
In workshop 3, the 9 concepts were reviewed and detailed through intense individual brainstorming 
after which a voting session determined the 2 most interesting and important concepts for further 
work. These 2 concepts were further detailed through discussions, user journeys, and low-fidelity 
prototypes.
The two concepts that defined the main outcome of three workshops are: 1) Combination of a mod­
ular CGM and an app for blood glucose measurement and meals, and 2) Introduction program for 
diabetes and blood sugar measurement. In this project the first concept is chosen to proceed with. The 
creation, building, and testing part of the two products the first concept consist of are different from 
each other as it requires different materials and methods to design and develop these products. Due 
to the time limit of the project, it was chosen to focus on one part of the solution. In this project, we 
have an opportunity to explore the potential of a mHealth app created through a co-creation process. 
It was therefore decided to continue only working and testing the app, with an assumption that the 
users have a device for measuring blood glucose levels, either a BGM or a CGM. 
A medium-fidelity click-through prototype was created in order to test the workflow of the app and 
understand: 1) if the general concept solves the identified problem of newly diagnosed people with 
type 2 diabetes, 2) if the concept has all the necessary functions, and 3) if the user is satisfied while in­
teracting with the concept. The general outcome from the testing sessions was positive as most users 
could see themselves using the app in the future, they find it motivating and helpful to start treating 
diabetes and blood glucose. The concept solves most of the needs and pains the users have including: 
a) need of managing blood glucose measurements together with all different lifestyle factors in one, 
and b) need for more personalised solutions and recommendations for the individual. 
However, the solution does not perfectly solve the problem of “As newly diagnosed there are too 
many actions one must do and too much information one must relate to.” as the content of the set-up 
page is too excessive. The current version of the prototype is not very user-friendly either as the users 
encountered several misunderstandings, confusion, and doubts in relation to the different features 
while testing. In future work the prototype must therefore be iterated and tested several times to ad­
just the functions more related to an MVP, to make the concept more user-friendly, and to solve all 
the right needs.
None of the participants directly mentioned lacking or missing functions in the prototype as well as 
user needs that have not been met. Even though the current version of the prototype must be tested 
some more to improve the overall user experience of the product and measuring blood glucose in 
general, the prototype is already closer to achieving the goal of the project than previous studies fol­
lowing a user-centered and participatory design approach has.
The following goal is thereby on the way to being achieved. User experience of measuring blood 
Conclusion
==PAGE:139==
135
glucose levels on a regular basis have can be improved through a co-creation process methodology as 
the users get to be part of designing and forming their desired wishes into real solutions. The users are 
supported by experts and designers that with their knowledge and skills can develop and implement 
solutions that may have high potential within the big market. 
Defined through the co-creation process in this thesis, user experience of measuring blood glucose 
levels may be improved by an app that collects all kind of different data from multiple devices and 
presents them to the user in a graphical and visual manner. The user can add actitivities to the blood 
glucose measurements to give the measured data a context and can freely choose what data to include, 
how to input and how/what to view. This personal platform informs the user about their status and 
actions (and at the right time) and rewards them when it goes well. Seeing and feeling an improved 
wellness status keeps the users motivated to reach and maintain their goals, and provides a sense of 
self-actualization when personal goals are accomplished for a longer period.
For future work, these values must be tested further with a significant amount of users over a longer 
period of time to make sure that the user experience of measuring blood glucose is actually improved. 
How may we improve the user experience of measuring blood glucose 
levels on a regular basis, through a co-creation process methodology?
Conclusion
